Zakah on Prepaid Rent: The Fraction Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Detailed calculation of zakah on hiring with upfront payment over ten years

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the zakah owed by a lessor in a prepaid long-term lease and explain the difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on fractions.

Core Idea
Prepaid zakah decrements yearly; fractions split the school.

When a lessor receives all rent upfront, his zakatable wealth shrinks each year as part of the payment becomes a debt. This passage from al-Muhit walks through the numbers. It also reveals a classic intra-Hanafi debate: do fractional amounts less than a full nisab unit trigger zakah? Abu Hanifa says no; the Sahibayn say yes. Let's see how that plays out in the calculation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract and the parties
A man pays 1,000 dirhams upfront for a ten-year house lease (100 per year). He never moves in. The lessor (owner) receives the full amount at once.
This is a special case where the lessor has immediate possession of the entire rent.
2
Trace the lessor's zakatable amount year by year
In year 1, the lessor owns 900 dirhams (since 100 immediately becomes a debt owed to the lessee due to non-delivery). He pays zakah on 900. In year 2, another 100 becomes debt, so his base is 800, but he also subtracts the zakah already paid in year 1 (22.5 according to the Sahibayn, or a different rounding according to Abu Hanifa).
The base is 800, then subtract zakah paid in year 1. This leads to two different figures.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on fractions
At year 2, Abu Hanifa calculates the zakatable amount as 760 dirhams, while the Sahibayn calculate 777.5. The 17.5 difference arises because Abu Hanifa ignores the fractional 20 dirhams (or the fractional part of the zakah) – he does not consider amounts below a certain fraction to be subject to zakah.
The exact basis of the difference: 800 - 760 = 40 vs 800 - 777.5 = 22.5. The extra 17.5 is the fraction on which Abu Hanifa does not impose zakah.
4
Apply the ruling to the lessee
The lessee (hirer) does not pay zakah in the first two years: in year 1 his remaining wealth is below nisab, and in year 2 the year does not complete on his recovered money. He starts paying in year 3.
The lessee only pays when he has a full nisab for a complete lunar year.
5
Confirm the condition of the lunar year
The passage ends by citing the hadith that no zakah is due until the year passes, and notes that the lunar year is the standard (not solar).
This is a basic condition for zakah obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط معزياً إلى الجامع : رجل له ألف درهم لا مال له غيرها استأجر بها داراً عشر سنين لكل سنة مائة، فدفع الألف ولم يسكنها حتى مضت السنون والدار في يد الآجر زكى الآجر في السنة الأولى عن تسعمائة وفي الثانية عن ثماني مائة إلا زكاة السنة الأولى، ثم يسقط لكل سنة زكاة مائة أخرى وما وجب عليه بالسنين الماضية لأنه ملك الألف بالتعجيل كلها، فإذا لم يسلم الدار إليه سنة انقضت الإجارة في العشر لأنه استهلك المعقود عليه قبل التسليم فزال عن ملكه مائة وصار مصروفاً إلى الدين. وكذلك في كل حول انتقض ويصير مائة ديناً عليه ويرفع من النصاب . ثم عند أبي حنيفة يزكي للسنة الثانية سبعمائة وستين، وعندهما سبعمائة وسبعة وسبعون ونصف لأنه لا زكاة في الكسور عنده، وعندهما فيه زكاة ولا زكاة على المستأجر في السنة الأولى والثانية لنقصان نصابه في الأولى ولعدم تمام الحول في الثانية ويزكي في الثالثة ثلثمائة لأنه استفاد مائة أخرى ثم يزكي لكل سنة مائة أخرى وما استفاد قبلها إلا أنه يرفع عنه زكاة السنين الماضية ا هـ. والمراد بكونه حولياً أن يتم الحول عليه وهو في ملكه لقوله عليه الصلاة والسلام «لا زكاة في مال حتى يحول عليه الحول»(۱) قال في الغاية : سمي حولاً لأن الأحوال تحول فيه. وفي القنية : العبرة في الزكاة للحول القمري .
And in al-Muhit, attributed to al-Jami': A man has one thousand dirhams, no property other than it. He rents a house with it for ten years, each year one hundred. He pays the thousand and does not reside in it until the years pass, while the house is in the hand of the lessor. The lessor pays zakah in the first year on nine hundred, and in the second on eight hundred minus the zakah of the first year. Then one hundred is deducted each year for zakah, and what was obligatory on him for past years, because he owned the thousand in full by acceleration. When the house is not delivered to him for a year, the lease terminates within ten because he consumed the subject matter before delivery, so one hundred leaves his ownership and becomes spent on a debt. Similarly in every year, it is canceled and one hundred becomes a debt upon him and is removed from the nisab. Then according to Abu Hanifa, he pays zakah for the second year seven hundred and sixty, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) seven hundred and seventy-seven and a half, because there is no zakah on fractions according to him, while according to them there is zakah on them. And the lessee pays no zakah in the first and second years due to the deficiency of his nisab in the first and lack of complete year in the second. He pays in the third year three hundred because he gains another one hundred. Then he pays for each year another one hundred and what he gained before, except that the zakah of past years is lifted from him. End. And the meaning of it being annual (hawli) is that the year passes while it is in his ownership, due to his peace be upon him saying: 'No zakah on wealth until the year passes over it.' He said in al-Ghayah: It is called hawli because the conditions change in it. And in al-Qunyah: The criterion in zakah is the lunar year.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed case: a man pays 1,000 dirhams upfront for a ten-year lease (100 per year). He does not use the house. The lessor (owner of the house) must pay zakah each year on the amount he owns. Since he received all 1,000 upfront, but each year 100 becomes a debt (because the lease fails year by year), his zakatable amount decreases. The calculation shows that the lessor pays in year 1 on 900, year 2 on 800 minus the first year's zakah, etc. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa does not count fractions (so he pays on 760 in year 2), while the two disciples count fractions (777.5). The lessee does not pay zakah in the first two years because his nisab is deficient or the year incomplete, but he pays later when he effectively recovers the money. The hadith at the end confirms the condition of the annual cycle. Note that the lunar year is the standard.

Key Terms
إجارة (ijarah): Lease contract
أجير (ajir): Lessor (owner) in this context
مستأجر (musta'jir): Lessee/hirer
نصاب (nisab): Minimum threshold for zakah (200 dirhams silver)
كسور (kusur): Fractions less than a complete nisab unit (e.g., less than 5 dirhams)
حول قمري (hawl qamari): Lunar year for calculating the annual cycle in zakah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the hire example, why does the lessee (musta'jir) not pay zakah in the first and second years?
A) Because he never owned the house
B) Because his nisab is deficient in the first and the year is incomplete in the second
C) Because the lessor owns the money
D) Because zakah is only on the lessor

Key Takeaway
Zakah on prepaid lease requires annual recalculation; the intra-Hanafi dispute on fractions affects the exact amount but not the overall structure.

Zakah on Retracted Acknowledgment of Debt
Ruling on zakah after retraction of debt acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zakah ruling when a debt acknowledgment is later retracted by mutual agreement and explain why no zakah is due.

Core Idea
Mutual retraction of a debt acknowledgment retroactively nullifies ownership, so no zakah is due.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario where a person acknowledges a debt, pays it, but later both parties agree that no debt ever existed. You might think zakah would be due because the money was possessed for a year, but the mutual agreement changes the legal reality. Understanding this ruling helps clarify the principle that ownership must be established for zakah to be obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and sequence
Outline the steps: A acknowledges a debt of 1000 dirhams to B, pays it, then after one year both agree there was no debt.
The payment occurred before the agreement.
2
Recognize the mutual agreement (tasadaq)
Tasadaq means both parties consent that the debt never existed, retroactively nullifying the acknowledgment and payment.
This is not a case of forgiveness; it's a mutual rescission.
3
Apply the principle of retroactive nullification
Since the debt is deemed nonexistent from the start, B never truly owned the money. Hence, no one held the money as a liable owner for a full year.
Ownership is a condition for zakah; here ownership never crystallized.
4
Derive the ruling
Therefore, no zakah is due on either A or B. A did not owe zakah on the money while it was with B, because A was not the owner. B did not owe zakah because the ownership is retroactively void.
This aligns with the Hanafi principle that zakah requires actual ownership for the full lunar year.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك رجل أقر لرجل بدين ألف درهم ودفع الألف إليه ثم تصادقا بعد الحول أنه لم يكن لا علیه دین زكاة على واحد منهما.
Similarly, a man acknowledged a debt of one thousand dirhams to another and paid the thousand to him; then after the year they both agreed that he had no debt upon him: no zakah on either of them.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This brief ruling states that if a person acknowledges a debt, pays it, but later both parties agree that no debt existed, then no zakah is due on either the payer or the recipient. The payment is treated as a void transaction, so the money is not considered owned by the recipient for zakah purposes.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a debt
تصادق (tasadaq): Mutual agreement after the fact
دين (dayn): Debt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a retracted acknowledgment of debt, who pays zakah?
A) The one who acknowledged
B) The one who received the money
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
No zakah is due on either party when a debt acknowledgment is mutually retracted, because the debt never existed and ownership is retroactively void.

Zakah on a Revoked Gift: Obligation Dropped by Revocation
Ruling with analogy on zakah after gift revocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakah when a gift is revoked after the year and explain why it is treated like the destruction of wealth.

Core Idea
Revoking a gift after the year retroactively cancels the zakah obligation like destruction of wealth.

This lesson examines a specific scenario: a donor gives a gift and later revokes it after a full year has passed. The initial ruling seems to deny zakah entirely, but the commentator clarifies that the obligation is dropped retroactively, analogizing it to the case where wealth perishes after the obligation arose. We will unpack the reasoning and understand why neither party pays zakah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A donor gives a gift (e.g., 1000 dirhams) to a recipient. After a full year, during which the recipient held and owned the money, the donor revokes the gift and reclaims the money.
The revocation may be by court order or without.
2
State the initial ruling from the text
The text says: 'no zakah on either of them.' This means neither the donor nor the recipient pays zakah on that money for the past year.
The 'apparent meaning' is that no zakah was ever due.
3
Recognise the problem
If the recipient owned the money for a full year, zakah would normally be due. So why is it not due? The commentator flags this as 'problematic'.
The donee had both possession and ownership for a full year – the usual trigger for zakah.
4
Understand the analogy (talil)
The commentator explains that the revocation is like the 'destruction of wealth after the obligation arose' (halak al-mal ba'd al-wujub). Just as wealth perishing after the hawl drops the obligation, so does revocation.
This is not the same as saying the obligation never existed; it is cancelled retroactively.
5
Confirm the final ruling
Thus, the correct view is that no zakah is due on either party. The obligation is dropped, not because it never existed, but because the effective cause (ownership) was nullified by the revocation, similar to destruction.
The commentator cites al-Walwalijiyyah for support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك رجل وهب لرجل ألفاً ودفع الألف إليه ثم رجع في الهبة بعد الحول بقضاء أو بغير قضاء واسترد الألف لا زكاة على واحد منهما ا هـ . وظاهره عدم وجوب الزكاة من الابتداء وهو مشكل في حق من كانت في يده وملكه وحال الحول عليه، فالظاهر أن هذه بمنزلة هلاك المال بعد الوجوب وهو مسقط كما في الولوالجية، وإلا فتحتاج المتون إلى إصلاح كما لا يخفى.
Similarly, a man gifted to another a thousand dirhams and paid the thousand to him, then he revoked the gift after the year, by court order or without, and took back the thousand: no zakah on either of them. End. The apparent meaning is that no zakah is obligatory from the beginning, but this is problematic concerning someone who had it in his hand and ownership and the year passed over it. So the apparent view is that this is like the destruction of wealth after obligation, which drops the obligation, as in al-Walwalijiyyah. Otherwise the texts would need correction, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage addresses a gift that is revoked after a year. The initial ruling (from al-Khaniyyah or similar) states that no zakah is due on either party. The commentator, however, finds this problematic because the donee had the money in his possession for a full year, so zakah should be due. He explains that the correct understanding is that the revocation is like the destruction of wealth after the obligation arose; the obligation is dropped, similar to a case in al-Walwalijiyyah where money perishes after zakah becomes due. Thus the ruling is consistent: no zakah is paid because the obligation is retroactively nullified.

Key Terms
هبة (hibah): Gift
رجوع في الهبة (ruju' fi al-hibah): Revocation of a gift
هلاك (halak): Destruction or loss of wealth

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is no zakah due when a gift is revoked after a year?
A) Because the donee never owned it
B) Because the revocation is like destruction of wealth after the obligation arose, dropping it
C) Because the donor pays it instead
D) Because the year did not complete

Key Takeaway
A revoked gift after a full year drops the zakah obligation for both parties, analogized to the destruction of wealth after the hawl.

The Condition of Freedom from Debt for Zakah: Debts to People vs. Debts to Allah
Discussion of which debts prevent the obligation of zakah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish which debts prevent the obligation of zakah according to the Hanafi school and identify the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the debt of zakah itself.

Core Idea
Only debts with a human claimant prevent zakah; debts to Allah do not.

The condition that one must be free from debt for zakah to be obligatory is firmly established, but not all debts are treated equally. The Hanafi school draws a critical line between financial obligations owed to people and those owed to Allah. This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq explains the rationale and addresses a dispute among the Hanafi imams regarding the debt of zakah itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition and its rationale
Locate the statement of the condition (shart farāgha ‘an al-dayn) and the analogies used: like water due to thirst, like the mukātab slave. Understand why a debt-occupied wealth is considered non-existent for zakah purposes.
The core analogy: a thirsty person's need for water makes his water 'non-existent' for others; similarly, a debtor's wealth is needed to pay the debt.
2
Classify debts into two categories
From the text, extract which debts are said to prevent (those with a human claimant, mutālib min jihāt al-‘ibād) and which do not (debts like nadhr, kaffārah, zakah itself, sadaqat al-fiṭr, ḥajj, etc.). Note the distinction: only debts to people prevent.
The text explicitly excludes divine obligations from the preventing category.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Identify the positions of Zufar (on both remaining nisab and after consumption) and Abu Yusuf (on after consumption) who argue that the debt of zakah does prevent, because there is a claimant (the Imam). Contrast with the majority view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad that it does not.
This dispute hinges on whether the Imam or his deputies are considered 'human claimants'.
4
Determine the relied-upon position
Assess which view is muftā bihī. The text favors the view that debts of divine obligations do not prevent. Conclude that zakah is obligatory even if one owes a previous zakah, because that debt is not a claim of people.
The practical consequence: if the debt of zakah prevented, a person with nisab could postpone paying indefinitely. The dominant view ensures continuity of the obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط فراغة عن الدين لأنه معه مشغول بحاجته الأصلية فاعتبر معدوماً كالماء المستحق بالعطش، ولأن الزكاة تحل مع ثبوت يده على ماله فلم تجب عليه الزكاة كالمكاتب، ولأن الدين يوجب نقصان الملك ولذا يأخذه الغريم إذا كان من جنس دينه من غير قضاء ولا رضا. أطلقه فشمل الحال والمؤجل ولو صداق زوجته المؤجل إلى الطلاق أو الموت. وقيل: المهر المؤجل لا يمنع لأنه غير مطالب به عادة بخلاف المعجل. وقيل: إن كان الزوج على عزم الأداء منع وإلا فلا لأنه لا يعد ديناً كذا في غاية البيان ونفقة المرأة إذا صارت ديناً على الزوج إما بالصلح أو بالقضاء ونفقة الأقارب إذا صارت ديناً عليه إما بالصلح أو بالقضاء في معراج الدراية. وقيد نفقة الأقارب في البدائع بقيد آخر وهو قليل المدة فإن المدة إذا كانت طويلة فإنها تسقط ولا تصير ديناً وشمل كلامه كل دين . عليه يمنع . كذا وفي الهداية والمراد دين له مطالب من جهة العباد حتى لا يمنع دين المنذر والكفارة ودين الزكاة مانع حال بقاء النصاب لأنه ينتقص به النصاب وكذا بعد الاستهلاك خلافاً لزفر فيهما، ولأبي يوسف في الثاني لأن له مطالباً وهو الإمام في السوائم ونوابه في أموال التجارة كان الملاك نوابه اهـ. وكذا لا يمنع دين صدقة الفطر ووجوب الحج وهدي المتعة والأضحية. وفي معراج الدراية ودين النذر لا يمنع ومتى استحق بجهة الزكاة بطل النذر
And the condition of being free from debt, because with it he is occupied with his essential need, so it is considered non-existent, like water due to thirst. And because zakah is not obligatory while his hand is established over his wealth, as in the case of a mukatib. And because debt necessitates a deficiency in ownership, and thus the creditor takes it if it is of the same type as his debt without decree or consent. He made it general, so it includes present and deferred debts, even the deferred dower of his wife until divorce or death. And it was said: the deferred mahr does not prevent because it is not usually demanded, unlike the immediate. And it was said: if the husband is determined to pay, it prevents; otherwise no, because it is not considered a debt. Similarly in Ghayat al-Bayan, the maintenance of a woman if it becomes a debt on the husband either by settlement or court order, and the maintenance of relatives if it becomes a debt on him either by settlement or court order, as in Mi'raj al-Dirayah. And he restricted the maintenance of relatives in al-Bada'i' with another restriction: a short period; if the period is long, it drops and does not become a debt. His statement includes every debt which prevents. Similarly in al-Hidayah: what is meant is a debt that has a claimant from the side of people, so that the debt of a nadhr, kaffarah, and the debt of zakah itself do not prevent because it diminishes the nisab. And similarly after consumption, contrary to Zufar in both, and to Abu Yusuf in the second, because it has a claimant: the Imam for grazing animals and his deputies for trade properties, the owners being his deputies. Similarly, the debt of sadaqat al-fitr, the obligation of Hajj, the gift of tamattu', and udhiyah do not prevent. And in Mi'raj al-Dirayah: the debt of a nadhr does not prevent, and when one becomes entitled to zakah, the nadhr becomes void.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive discussion of the condition that one must be free from debt for zakah to be obligatory. The analogy is drawn with thirst: a thirsty person's need for water is like a debtor's need for money to pay debts; such money is considered non-existent for zakah purposes. The condition applies to all debts that have a human claimant (debt of people). Debts to Allah, such as nadhr (vows), kaffarah (expiation), the zakah itself, sadaqat al-fitr, etc., do not prevent zakah because they are not claims of people. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Zufar and Abu Yusuf differ on whether the debt of zakah itself can be a preventing debt. Abu Yusuf says it does because there is a claimant (the Imam). The text also discusses details like deferred mahr, maintenance debts, and whether a resolved intention to pay matters. The relied-upon position is that only debts with an active human claimant prevent zakah.

Key Terms
شرط فراغة عن الدين (shart faraghah 'an al-dayn): Condition that one must be free from debt for zakah to be obligatory
دين (dayn): Debt, financial obligation
مطالب (mutālib): Claimant who demands payment
منذر (mundhir): Vow (nadhr) that creates a financial obligation
كفارة (kaffārah): Expiation for sins, e.g., breaking oath
زكاة الزكاة (zakāt al-zakāh): The zakah itself as a debt
سوائم (sawā’im): Grazing animals liable for zakah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, which of the following does NOT prevent the obligation of zakah?
A) A debt owed to a friend
B) A deferred dower (mahr) that is due
C) A debt of kaffārah (expiation)
D) A debt for the purchase of a car

Key Takeaway
Only debts with an active human claimant prevent the obligation of zakah; debts owed to Allah (like kaffārah, nadhr, and zakah itself) do not.

Zakat on Invalid Debt or Revoked Gift
Ruling on zakat for invalid debt and revoked gift, with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat when a debt is later invalidated or a gift revoked, and explain the legal analogy that resolves the apparent contradiction.

Core Idea
Zakat obligation drops if wealth is later invalidated — like destruction after obligation.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents two scenarios: an admitted debt that both parties later agree never existed, and a gift that the donor revokes after a year. In each case, the possessor owned the wealth for a full lunar year, yet the ruling states no zakat is due on either party. The commentator, al-Marghinani, resolves the apparent inconsistency by comparing these situations to the destruction of wealth after zakat becomes obligatory, which nullifies the obligation. This lesson unpacks the ruling and the reasoning behind it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The text gives two distinct scenarios: (1) a man admits a debt of 1000 dirhams, pays it, then both parties later agree no debt existed; (2) a man gifts 1000 dirhams to another, possession transfers, then the donor revokes the gift after a year (with or without a court order) and reclaims the money.
2
State the ruling for both
In both cases, the ruling is explicitly 'لا زكاة على واحد منهما' — no zakat is due on either the payer/donor or the recipient/donee.
This is the plain meaning of the matn.
3
Spot the apparent problem
The recipient of the money (whether as payment of a 'debt' or as gift) had the wealth in his possession for a full year — the normal condition for zakat obligation is a full year's ownership. Why then is no zakat due?
The matn does not explain; the commentator raises this problem.
4
Understand the commentator's resolution
The commentator equates these cases to 'halāk al-māl baʿda al-wujūb' — destruction of wealth after zakat becomes obligatory. He cites al-Walwaljiyyah to show that subsequent invalidation nullifies the obligation retroactively. Thus, zakat was initially owed but is dropped due to later events.
This resolves the conflict and protects the matn from needing correction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك رجل أقر لرجل بدين ألف درهم ودفع الألف إليه ثم تصادقا بعد الحول أنه لم يكن لا علیه دین زكاة على واحد منهما. وكذلك رجل وهب لرجل ألفاً ودفع الألف إليه ثم رجع في الهبة بعد الحول بقضاء أو بغير قضاء واسترد الألف لا زكاة على واحد منهما ا هـ . وظاهره عدم وجوب الزكاة من الابتداء وهو مشكل في حق من كانت في يده وملكه وحال الحول عليه، فالظاهر أن هذه بمنزلة هلاك المال بعد الوجوب وهو مسقط كما في الولوالجية، وإلا فتحتاج المتون إلى إصلاح كما لا يخفى.
And likewise a man who admitted to a debt of a thousand dirhams and paid the thousand to him, then after the year they both agreed that there was no debt on him, there is no zakat on either of them. And likewise a man gifted a thousand to a man and paid the thousand to him, then he revoked the gift after the year by judicial order or without, and took back the thousand, there is no zakat on either of them. end quote. And its apparent meaning is no obligation of zakat from the beginning, but this is problematic regarding one who had it in his hand and ownership and the year passed over him. So the apparent is that this is like destruction of wealth after obligation, which nullifies it, as in al-Walwaljiyyah; otherwise the texts would need correction, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq, followed by the commentator's analysis. The first ruling: if a man admits to a debt of 1000 dirhams and pays it, but later both agree that there was no debt, no zakat is due on either party. The second: if a man gifts 1000 dirhams and the recipient takes possession, but then the donor revokes the gift after a year (with or without a court order) and reclaims the money, again no zakat is due on either. The commentator notes that the apparent ruling—no zakat due from the beginning—is problematic because the possessor owned the wealth for a full year. He resolves this by explaining that these cases are analogous to the destruction of wealth after zakat becomes obligatory, which nullifies the obligation. This is the view in al-Walwaljiyyah. If not for this interpretation, the matn would require correction. Thus, the ruling stands, but with a subtle understanding that zakat was initially owed but is dropped due to subsequent events.

Key Terms
الهبة (al-hiba): A gift that is revocable by the donor before possession or under certain conditions.
الرجوع في الهبة (al-ruju' fi al-hiba): The donor taking back the gifted property after it has been given.
هلاك المال بعد الوجوب (halak al-mal ba'd al-wujub): Destruction of wealth after zakat has become obligatory, which nullifies the obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only the donor owes zakat.
B) Only the donee owes zakat.
C) No zakat on either party.
D) Both owe zakat because the year passed.

Key Takeaway
When wealth is later invalidated by mutual agreement or revocation, zakat obligation is dropped by analogy to destruction after obligation.

Zakat Liability When Trade Asset Perishes Before Delivery
Ruling on zakat liability for seller and buyer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the zakat obligation on seller and buyer when a trade asset is destroyed before possession, applying the rules of hawalan al-hawl (yearly cycle) and infisakh al-bay‘ (rescission).

Core Idea
Zakat on the trade asset depends on ownership duration, not just intention.

This passage from al-Khaniyyah presents a nuanced scenario: a man buys a slave for trade, pays the price, but the slave dies before delivery. The year passes. Who owes zakat? The answer turns on whether the buyer's ownership of the trade asset was effective for a full year. Understanding this ruling sharpens your ability to apply zakat conditions to modern trade transactions where goods are lost before delivery.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two parties
A man buys a slave for trade (value 200 dirhams). He pays the price but does not take possession. The slave dies with the seller after one full year has passed. Two parties: seller (original owner) and buyer (would-be owner).
The slave was a trade asset ('abd al-tijara), not for personal use.
2
Determine the seller's zakat liability
The seller owned the price (200 dirhams) for a full year. Rescission does not negate that. Therefore, seller owes zakat on 200 dirhams.
The debt incurred by rescission (returning the price) arises after the year, so it does not interrupt hawalan al-hawl.
3
Determine the buyer's zakat liability in the general case
The text initially states that the buyer also owes zakat. The reasoning: the slave was for trade, and upon rescission the buyer gets back the price. This effectively means the buyer owned the trade asset (the price) for the full year.
This applies when the slave's value equals the price (200 dirhams).
4
Apply the condition when value differs from price
If the slave's value was only 100 dirhams, the buyer does not owe zakat. He never owned 200 dirhams for a full year; the rescission gives him only 100 (or the price he paid? Actually he paid 200, but the asset was worth 100. The text says he does not owe zakat because the price left his ownership completely, and he acquires it anew after the year.
Key: ownership of the trade asset (price) must be continuous for the year.
5
State the final ruling and mufta bihi
Seller always owes zakat on the price he received, because he owned it for a full year. Buyer owes zakat only if the value of the slave equals the price he paid, so that his ownership of the price via rescission is effectively continuous.
This is the relied-upon position in the Hanafi fatwa tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية أيضاً رجل اشترى عبداً للتجارة يساوي مائتي درهم ونقد الثمن ولم يقبض العبد حتى حال الحول فمات العبد عند البائع، كان على بائع العبد زكاة المائتين وكذلك على المشتري أما على البائع فلأنه ملك الثمن وحال الحول عليه عنده وأما على المشتري فلأن العبد كان للتجارة وبموته عند البائع انفسخ البيع والمشترى أخذ عوض العبد مائتي درهم فإن كانت قيمة العبد مائة كان على البائع زكاة المائتين لأنه ملك الثمن ومضى عليه الحول عنده وبانفساخ البيع لحقه دين بعد الحول فلا تسقط عنه زكاة المائتين ولا زكاة على المشتري لأن الثمن زال عن ملكه إلى البائع فلم يملك المائتي حولاً كاملاً، وبانفساخ البيع استفاد المائتين بعد الحول فلا تجب عليه الزكاة ا هـ.
And in al-Khaniyyah also: A man bought a slave for trade worth two hundred dirhams, paid the price, but did not take possession of the slave until the year passed, then the slave died with the seller. The seller of the slave must pay zakat on the two hundred, and likewise the buyer. As for the seller, because he owned the price and the year passed over it with him. As for the buyer, because the slave was for trade and by its death with the seller the sale is rescinded, and the buyer receives two hundred dirhams as the price of the slave. If the slave's value was one hundred, then the seller owes zakat on the two hundred because he owned the price and the year passed over it with him, and by the rescission of the sale he incurs a debt after the year, so the zakat on the two hundred is not waived. And no zakat on the buyer because the price left his ownership to the seller, so he did not own the two hundred for a complete year, and by the rescission he acquires the two hundred after the year, so zakat is not obligatory on him. End quote.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage from al-Khaniyyah presents a detailed scenario. A man buys a slave for business purposes (trade asset) worth 200 dirhams. He pays the price but does not take physical possession of the slave until after a full year has passed. During that year, the slave dies while still in the seller's possession. The ruling addresses zakat liability. Seller: He must pay zakat on the 200 dirhams because he owned that money (the price) and a full year passed with it in his ownership. The rescission of the sale does not relieve him, as the debt (returning the price) arises after the year. Buyer: He also must pay zakat on the 200 dirhams? Wait—the text says 'and likewise on the buyer' initially, but then gives a condition: if the slave's value was 100, then the seller owes zakat on 200, but the buyer owes nothing. The initial statement is general: if the slave is worth 200, both owe zakat. But if the value is only 100, then the seller owes zakat on the full 200 (since he owned that money), and the buyer does not because he never fully owned 200 for a complete year. The reasoning for the buyer: his ownership of the 200 dirhams (the price he paid) was not complete because the sale is rescinded, and he only gets back the price after the year, so no full year of ownership. For the seller, he had the money (the price) for a full year, so zakat is due even though the sale is rescinded later.

Key Terms
التجارة (al-tijara): Intent to trade; the slave was purchased for resale.
انفساخ البيع (infisakh al-bay'): Automatic rescission of the sale due to the subject matter's destruction before possession.
حول (hawl): A complete lunar year of ownership over the zakatable asset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, when does the buyer of a trade slave owe zakat on the price he paid?
A) Only when the slave's value is less than the price.
B) Only when the slave's value equals the price.
C) In all cases, because he intended trade.
D) Never, because the sale is rescinded.

Key Takeaway
The zakat obligation on a trade asset destroyed before delivery turns on whether the seller and buyer each owned the equivalent of the price for a full year, with the buyer's liability conditional on the asset's value matching the price.

The Condition of Being Debt-Free for Zakat: General Ruling and Types of Debt
Ruling that debt prevents zakat obligation, with details on types of debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi condition that one must be free from debt to owe zakat, and identify which types of debt (immediate, deferred, dower, maintenance) prevent obligation according to the relied-upon view.

Core Idea
Debt with a human claimant makes wealth virtually non-existent, preventing zakat; but not all debts are equal.

The Hanafi school conditions zakat on the absence of debt. This lesson unpacks that condition: what counts as a preventing debt? We explore the proofs and then detail how different debts—immediate, deferred, dower, and maintenance—are treated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
The condition is that one must be free from debt for zakat to be obligatory. This is a condition of obligation, not validity.
2
Examine the proofs
Three proofs: (1) debt occupies basic need, so wealth is virtually non-existent; (2) analogy to the mukatab who doesn't owe zakat; (3) debt reduces ownership, as creditors can take property without consent.
These proofs establish the principle.
3
Understand the scope
The condition covers all debts, immediate and deferred, including deferred dower.
The author of Kanz al-Daqaiq made it absolute.
4
Analyze the dower controversy
There are two views: (a) deferred dower does not prevent because it is not usually demanded; (b) if the husband intends to pay, it prevents. The latter is from Ghayat al-Bayan.
The relied-upon view is that deferred dower does not prevent unless intention to pay.
5
Consider maintenance debts
Wife's maintenance becomes a preventing debt when settled by court or agreement. For relatives, only if the debt is of short duration; long durations drop the debt.
Al-Bada'i restricts relatives' maintenance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط فراغة عن الدين لأنه معه مشغول بحاجته الأصلية فاعتبر معدوماً كالماء المستحق بالعطش، ولأن الزكاة تحل مع ثبوت يده على ماله فلم تجب عليه الزكاة كالمكاتب، ولأن الدين يوجب نقصان الملك ولذا يأخذه الغريم إذا كان من جنس دينه من غير قضاء ولا رضا. أطلقه فشمل الحال والمؤجل ولو صداق زوجته المؤجل إلى الطلاق أو الموت. وقيل: المهر المؤجل لا يمنع لأنه غير مطالب به عادة بخلاف المعجل. وقيل: إن كان الزوج على عزم الأداء منع وإلا فلا لأنه لا يعد ديناً كذا في غاية البيان ونفقة المرأة إذا صارت ديناً على الزوج إما بالصلح أو بالقضاء ونفقة الأقارب إذا صارت ديناً عليه إما بالصلح أو بالقضاء في معراج الدراية. وقيد نفقة الأقارب في البدائع بقيد آخر وهو قليل المدة فإن المدة إذا كانت طويلة فإنها تسقط ولا تصير ديناً وشمل كلامه كل دين . عليه يمنع . كذا وفي الهداية والمراد دين له مطالب من جهة العباد حتى لا يمنع دين المنذر والكفارة ودين الزكاة مانع حال بقاء النصاب لأنه ينتقص به النصاب وكذا بعد الاستهلاك خلافاً لزفر فيهما، ولأبي يوسف في الثاني لأن له مطالباً وهو الإمام في السوائم ونوابه في أموال التجارة كان الملاك نوابه اهـ. وكذا لا يمنع دين صدقة الفطر ووجوب الحج وهدي المتعة والأضحية. وفي معراج الدراية ودين النذر لا يمنع ومتى استحق بجهة الزكاة بطل النذر
And the condition is being free from debt because with it he is occupied with his basic need, so it is considered non-existent, like water that is claimed by thirst. And because zakat is due along with his possession of his wealth, so it is not obligatory on him like the mukatab. And because debt necessitates deficiency of ownership, hence the creditor takes it if it is of the same type as his debt without court order or consent. He (the author) made it absolute, so it includes immediate and deferred, even the deferred dower of his wife until divorce or death. And it is said: the deferred dower does not prevent because he is not usually demanded, unlike the immediate. And it is said: if the husband intends to pay, it prevents; otherwise no, because it is not considered a debt. This is in Ghayat al-Bayan. And the maintenance of a woman when it becomes a debt on the husband either by settlement or court order, and the maintenance of relatives when it becomes a debt on him by settlement or court order, in Mi'raj al-Diraya. And al-Bada'i restricted the maintenance of relatives with another restriction: it is of short duration, because if the duration is long, it is dropped and does not become a debt. And his statement includes every debt that prevents. As such in al-Hidaya: the intended meaning is a debt that has a claimant from among the people, so that the debt of nadhr and kaffarah does not prevent, and zakat debt prevents while the nisab remains because it decreases the nisab, and likewise after it is consumed, contrary to Zufar in both, and to Abu Yusuf in the second because it has a claimant, which is the Imam for grazing animals and his deputies for trade goods, as the owners are his deputies. Ended. And likewise, the debt of sadaqat al-fitr and the obligation of hajj and the hady of tamattu' and udhiyyah do not prevent. And in Mi'raj al-Diraya: the debt of nadhr does not prevent, and when it becomes due by way of zakat, the nadhr is nullified.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage explains the condition of being free from debt for zakat obligation. The sharh opens by stating the condition: one must be free from debt because debt occupies a basic need. Debt is considered like non-existent property, analogous to water that is reserved for a thirsty person. Three proofs are given: (1) zakat is due when one actually possesses wealth, but debt creates a virtual deficiency; (2) analogy to a mukatab (slave who has a contract of manumission) who does not owe zakat; (3) debt reduces ownership, as creditors can take property without consent if it is of the same type. The author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) made the condition absolute, covering both immediate and deferred debts, including deferred dower. However, there are two opinions: (a) deferred dower does not prevent because it is not usually demanded; (b) if the husband intends to pay, it prevents. The latter is from Ghayat al-Bayan. Maintenance debts (for wife or relatives) that become due by settlement or court order also prevent, but al-Bada'i restricts relatives' maintenance to short durations; long durations drop the debt. The passage then quotes al-Hidaya: the debt that prevents is only a debt that has a human claimant. Therefore, debts like nadhr (vow) and kaffarah (expiation) do not prevent zakat. In contrast, zakat debt itself (unpaid zakat from previous years) does prevent because it has a claimant (the Imam for grazing animals, and deputies for trade goods). This is the Hanafi view, contrary to Zufar in all cases and Abu Yusuf in the second case (after consumption). Also, debts for sadaqat al-fitr, hajj, hady of tamattu', and udhiyyah do not prevent. Finally, from Mi'raj al-Diraya: the debt of a nadhr does not prevent zakat; if zakat becomes due, it nullifies the nadhr (because zakat takes precedence).

Key Terms
الدين (al-dayn): A liability that one owes, which prevents zakat if it has a human claimant.
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has a contract to purchase his freedom; he does not owe zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following debts does NOT prevent zakat according to the Hanafi school?
A) A debt owed to a friend.
B) Deferred dower if husband intends to pay.
C) Maintenance of wife settled by court.
D) A vow (nadhr) to give charity.

Key Takeaway
Debt with a human claimant prevents zakat; immediate and deferred debts are included, but dower and maintenance have nuanced rulings.

Debts That Prevent Zakat vs. Those That Do Not: The Human Claimant Distinction
Ruling on which debts prevent zakat, with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between debts that prevent zakat (those with a human claimant) and those that do not (religious obligations like nadhr, kaffarah), and explain the intra-Hanafi differences regarding zakat debt.

Core Idea
Only debts with a human claimant prevent zakat; religious debts like nadhr and kaffarah do not.

While debt generally prevents zakat, the Hanafi school clarifies that not every liability is considered a preventing debt. This lesson examines the key criterion: the debt must have a human claimant. We also explore disagreements among Hanafi imams on whether zakat debt itself prevents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the criterion
From al-Hidaya, the debt that prevents is only a debt with a human claimant (min jihat al-ibad).
2
List non-preventing debts
Debts like nadhr (vow), kaffarah (expiation), sadaqat al-fitr, hajj, hady al-tamattu, udhiyyah do not prevent.
These are religious obligations without a human claimant.
3
Analyze zakat debt
Unpaid zakat from previous years does prevent because it has a claimant: the Imam for grazing animals, or his deputies for trade goods.
Thus zakat debt is treated as a human-claimant debt.
4
Intra-Hanafi differences
Zufar disagrees completely: zakat debt does not prevent. Abu Yusuf agrees that it prevents while nisab remains, but disagrees after consumption. Abu Hanifa holds it prevents in both cases.
The relied-upon view is Abu Hanifa's.
5
Practical implication
When calculating zakat, one should subtract any human-claimant debts from assets; but not subtract religious obligations.
This affects the net zakatable wealth.

Source Text

Zakat vs Nadhr: Allah's Right Takes Precedence
Numerical examples demonstrating the precedence of zakat over a vow

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that zakat obligation (as a right of Allah) overrides a personal vow (nadhr) when both involve the same wealth, and calculate the correct amounts in the two given scenarios.

Core Idea
Zakat is Allah's right; a vow cannot override it—zakat is deducted first from the vowed amount.

What happens when a person who owns the nisab also makes a vow to give charity from the same wealth? Does the vow fulfill zakat? The Hanafi school teaches that zakat is a divine obligation that takes priority. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq illustrates this principle with two detailed numerical examples, showing how the zakat portion is always deducted first, and how the remaining vow must be fulfilled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two cases: (a) a vow linked to a specific sum from existing wealth, and (b) an absolute vow that becomes a liability (dhimma). Determine which scenario applies in a given situation.
The key difference is whether the vow is tied to specific coins or is a debt on the person.
2
Calculate zakat on the nisab
For 200 dirhams (nisab), the zakat due is 2.5 dirhams (1/40). This amount is non-negotiable and belongs to Allah.
Zakat rate is fixed: 2.5% if nisab is reached and a lunar year passes.
3
Apply the rule of precedence
In the first scenario, the vowed 100 dirhams is reduced by the zakat amount. In the second, the absolute vow remains binding on the dhimma, and paying the vowed amount does not cancel the zakat—the zakat portion is taken from the payment.
The underlying principle: 'zakat is specified by God's specification' (تعيين الله) and cannot be invalidated by the person's own specification (تعيينه).
4
Determine the final obligations
In scenario (a): pay 2.5 dirhams zakat, then 97.5 dirhams for the vow. In scenario (b): pay 100 dirhams, of which 2.5 is zakat, then pay another 100 for the vow (since the first 100 is partly consumed by zakat).
The person ends up paying 102.5 dirhams in total in scenario (b), fulfilling both obligations.
5
Understand the rationale
Zakat is a right of Allah (fard) established by definitive texts; a personal vow (wajib) is subordinate and cannot supersede a divine command.
This reflects the Hanafi hierarchy: fard > wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب عليه في الأولى اثنين وعشرين درهماً، ويكون الباقي . معه في الثانية سبعمائة وثمانية وسبعين فيزكي عن سبعمائة وستين عنده كما سيأتي . فيه بيانه : له مائتا درهم نذر بأن يتصدق بمائة منها وحال الحول سقط النذر بقدر درهمين ونصف ويتصدق للنذر بسبعة وتسعين ونصف، ولو تصدق بمائة منها للنذر يقع در همان ونصف عن الزكاة لأنه متعين بتعيين الله تعالى فلا يبطل بتعيينه لغيره. ولو نذر بمائة مطلقة لزمته لأن محل المنذور به الذمة، فلو تصدق بمائة منها للنذر يقع درهمان ونصف للزكاة ويتصدق بمثلها عن النذر ا هـ
The answer to that is: in the first case, twenty-two dirhams, and the remainder is with him; in the second case, seven hundred and seventy-eight, so he pays zakat on seven hundred and sixty according to him, as will come. In it is its explanation: He has two hundred dirhams, he vowed to give one hundred of them in charity, and the year passed. The nadhr is reduced by two and a half dirhams, and he gives for the nadhr ninety-seven and a half. And if he gave one hundred of them for the nadhr, two and a half dirhams fall for zakat because it is specified by God's specification, so it is not invalidated by his specification for other than it. And if he vowed an absolute one hundred, it becomes binding because the place of the vowed item is the dhimma. So if he gave one hundred of them for the nadhr, two and a half dirhams fall for zakat, and he gives the like of it for the nadhr. End quote.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage provides two numerical examples. The first part (from page 357) seems to answer a previous question: the answer is 22 dirhams in the first case and 778 in the second, and he pays zakat on 760. Then the explanation on page 358: A man has 200 dirhams (nisab) and vows to give 100 of them in charity. When the year passes, the nadhr is reduced by the zakat due on the 200 dirhams (2.5 dirhams). So he effectively gives 97.5 dirhams for the nadhr. If he actually gives the full 100 dirhams for the nadhr, 2.5 dirhams are automatically assigned to zakat because zakat is specified by Allah and takes precedence; his specification for nadhr does not nullify Allah's right. Then a second scenario: if he vows an absolute amount (e.g., 100 dirhams) without linking it to a specific fund, it becomes binding on his dhimma (liability). So if he then gives 100 dirhams from his wealth (which is the same 200 dirhams), 2.5 dirhams go to zakat, and he must give another 100 dirhams for the nadhr (since the first 100 is partly consumed by zakat). Thus, zakat always takes priority over vows.

Key Terms
النذر (al-nadhr): A vow to perform a charitable act, which becomes binding once declared.
الذمة (al-dhimma): The legal capacity to incur obligations; the place where the vowed amount becomes due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 97.5 dirhams
B) 100 dirhams
C) 2.5 dirhams
D) 102.5 dirhams

Key Takeaway
Zakat is a divinely ordained right that takes priority over a personal vow; the zakat amount is always deducted first from the vowed sum.

Zakat on Unpaid Years: The Two-Year Rule
Ruling statement on zakat arrears for two years

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat liability when two years pass without payment and calculate the due amounts for the example of 25 camels.

Core Idea
When two years pass without paying zakat, only the first year's obligation stands; the second year does not accrue the same liability.

Zakat is an annual obligation, but what happens if you miss a year? The Hanafi school provides a specific rule: if a full nisab passes two years without payment, the second year does not simply double the debt. Instead, the first year's zakat is due, and the second year incurs a different, often smaller, obligation—illustrated by the camel-to-sheep conversion. This lesson unpacks that rule with a concrete example.

Guided Path
1
Understand the General Rule
The passage begins with a broad statement: if a nisab (minimum taxable wealth) remains unchanged for two full years and no zakat is paid, there is no zakat owed for the second year on that same nisab. This means the obligation does not compound.
This prevents double burden on the same asset.
2
Apply the Example of 25 Camels
The text gives a specific case: 25 camels. For the first year, the standard zakat is a bint makhad (one-year-old female camel). For the second year, instead of another camel, the due amount is four sheep.
This is a special Hanafi rule for delayed livestock zakat.
3
Identify the Rationale
The rule avoids excessive burden; the second year's zakat is calculated differently to account for the delay without penalising the owner doubly. The sheep rate is a standard alternative when camel rates do not apply.
This reflects the principle of ease (taysir) in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Memorise the Numbers
For memorisation: 25 camels → first year bint makhad; arrears for second year → 4 sheep. This is a key detail for zakat on camels.
The four sheep are in lieu of the second year's camel zakat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو كان له نصاب حال عليه حولان ولم يزكه فيهما لا زكاة عليه في الحول الثاني، ولو كان له خمس وعشرون من الإبل لم يزكه حولين كان عليه في الحول الأول بنت مخاض وللحول الثاني أربع شياه
If he had a nisab and two years passed over it and he did not pay zakat in them, there is no zakat on him in the second year. And if he had twenty-five camels and did not pay zakat for two years, he must pay in the first year a bint makhad and for the second year four sheep.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This is a concise ruling from the matn. If someone has a nisab (the minimum threshold) and two full years pass without paying zakat, the obligation for the second year is not added. Instead, only the zakat for the first year is due. The example: for 25 camels, the first year's zakat is a bint makhad (one-year-old female camel), and for the second year, only four sheep (the zakat for the second year is not an additional camel but a sheep? Actually the rule: for 25 camels, the zakat is a bint makhad. For the second year, if unpaid, the ruling is that four sheep are due? This seems inconsistent with normal camel zakat schedules. This is a specific rule for when payment is delayed: the second year's zakat is calculated based on the same nisab but at sheep rate. This is a detailed point from the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
بنت مخاض (bint makhad): A female camel in its second year (one-year-old).
شاة (shah): A sheep or goat (one head).
نصاب (nisab): The minimum threshold of wealth liable for zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Two bint makhad.
B) One bint makhad and four sheep.

Key Takeaway
When zakat is missed for two years on the same nisab, the Hanafi school requires only the first year's normal zakat plus a reduced arrear payment (e.g., four sheep for 25 camels), not double the original obligation.

The Effect of Selling Zakatable Livestock Before Hawl on the Substitute
Ruling on zakat when livestock is sold before hawl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat when a nisab of grazing livestock is sold before the year ends and a substitute is received, and explain the underlying principle of istihlak.

Core Idea
Selling grazing livestock before the year ends terminates the hawl; the substitute starts a new year, regardless of intent.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a specific branch of zakat law: when a person sells his grazing livestock (sa'imah) before the completion of the year and receives a substitute, that substitute does not inherit the old year. The sale is considered consumption (istihlak) of the original asset, whether the seller intended to evade zakat or not. This principle applies to all types of substitutes—similar livestock, different animals, or cash—with a special note for cash when combined with other assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three substitute scenarios
Find the three types of substitutes mentioned: a similar grazing animal, an animal of another type, or dirhams (cash).
2
Note the irrelevance of intent
Observe that the text says 'intending to flee from sadqah or not'—the ruling is the same regardless of motive.
3
Recognize the exception for dirhams
If dirhams are received and the owner has other assets to combine, zakat may be due after combining; otherwise, a new year applies.
4
Grasp the underlying principle of istihlak
The text explains that replacing a grazing animal with anything else is considered consumption (istihlak) that breaks the year, unlike non-grazing (trade) animals where substitution may not break the year.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن فروعه ما إذا باع نصاب السائمة قبل الحول بيوم بسائمة مثلها أو من جنس آخر أو بدراهم يريد به الفرار من الصدقة أو لا يريد لا يجب عليه الزكاة في البدل إلا بحول جديد أو يكون له ما يضمه إليه في صورة الدراهم وهذا بناء على أن استبداله السائمة بغيرها مطلقاً استهلاك بخلاف غير السائمة. كذا في فتح القدير وفي البدائع
And among its branches: if he sold a nisab of grazing livestock a day before the year for a similar grazing animal or of another type or for dirhams, intending to flee from sadqah or not, zakat is not obligatory on the substitute except with a new year, or if he has something to add to it in the case of dirhams. And this is based on the fact that replacing a grazing animal with other than it is absolutely consumption (istihlak), unlike non-grazing animals. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and in al-Bada'i.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses branches of the previous ruling. If someone sells a nisab (minimum threshold) of grazing livestock (sa'imah) one day before the completion of the year, and receives as substitute either more grazing animals, or animals of another type, or dirhams, whether the seller intends to evade zakat or not, no zakat is due on the substitute until a new year passes. However, if he receives dirhams and he has other assets that can be combined with the dirhams to reach a nisab, then he may owe zakat after combining. The underlying principle: replacing a grazing animal with anything else (even another grazing animal) is considered consumption (istihlak) that breaks the year. This is contrary to non-grazing animals (e.g., trade goods) where replacement may not be consumption. This view is from Fath al-Qadir and al-Bada'i.

Key Terms
السائمة (al-sa'imah): Grazing livestock that feed themselves in pastures; subject to zakat.
الاستهلاك (al-istihlak): Consumption; here meaning the disposal of the original asset such that the year is broken.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) He does not owe zakat on dirhams.

Key Takeaway
When a nisab of grazing livestock is sold before the year ends, no zakat is due on the substitute until a new year, because the sale is considered consumption (istihlak) of the original asset.

Debt of Kharaj and 'Ushr as a Preventer of Zakat and Ranking Assets
Ruling on debt preventing zakat and asset ranking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a debt of kharaj or 'ushr prevents zakat, and apply the Hanafi ranking of assets for subtracting such debt.

Core Idea
A demanded debt of kharaj or 'ushr prevents zakat; the debt is subtracted from the easiest asset first.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq concerning the effect of kharaj and 'ushr debts on the obligation of zakat. The Hanafi school distinguishes between the mere obligation of 'ushr on crops (which does not prevent zakat on other assets) and the personal debt that arises when the crop is destroyed. Additionally, the passage provides a detailed ranking of assets for paying such debts, with a qualification from al-Mabsut regarding the presence of the zakat collector.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of debt
The passage discusses two types of debt: kharaj (land tax) and 'ushr when it becomes a personal debt (e.g., by destroying the crop). Distinguish these from the mere obligation of 'ushr on the crop itself.
The obligation of 'ushr on the crop is not a personal debt and does not prevent zakat.
2
Determine when debt prevents zakat
The debt must be 'demanded' (i.e., due and payable). If it is a present, demandable debt, it prevents zakat. Understand that this is a man'i (preventer) of zakat obligation.
The condition is that the debt is in the dhimma and currently claimable.
3
Apply the ranking of assets for debt payment
When a person has multiple nisabs (e.g., cash, trade goods, grazing animals), the debt should be assigned to the easiest asset to pay in order: first cash, then trade goods, then sa'imah. Among sa'imah, assign to the least valuable type.
If multiple types are equal in value, there is choice. The passage gives examples of sheep, camels, and cows.
4
Consider the qualification from al-Mabsut
The absolute rule is qualified: if the zakat collector (musaddiq) is present, he decides; if not, the owner may choose which asset to assign the debt to, paying zakat from the other.
This shows the practical operation of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا دين الخراج يمنع وجوب الزكاة لأنه يطالب به، وكذا إذا صار العشر ديناً في الذمة بأن أتلف الطعام العشري صاحبه، فأما وجوب العشر فلا يمنع لأنه متعلق بالطعام وهو ليس من مال التجارة، وذكر الشارح وغيره إن كان للمديون نصب يصرف الدين إلى الأيسر قضاء فيصرف إلى الدراهم والدنانير ثم إلى عروض التجارة ثم إلى السوائم، فإن كانت أجناساً صرف إلى أقلها حتى لو كان له أربعون من الغنم وثلاثون من البقر وخمس من الإبل صرف إلى الغنم أو إلى الإبل دون البقر لأن التبيع فوق الشاة فإن استويا خير كأربعين من الغنم وخمس من الإبل. وقيل: يصرف إلى الغنم لتجب الزكاة في الإبل في العام القابل هكذا أطلقوا. وقيده في المبسوط بأن يحضر المصدق أي الساعي فإن لم يحضره فالخيار إلى صاحب المال إن شاء صرف الدين إلى السائمة وأدى الزكاة من الدراهم وإن شاء صرف الدين إلى الدراهم وأدى الزكاة من السائمة لأن في حق صاحب المال هما سواء ا هـ.
And they said: the debt of kharaj prevents the obligation of zakat because it is demanded. And likewise when 'ushr becomes a debt in the dhimma — for example, if the owner destroys the 'ushri food. As for the obligation of 'ushr itself, it does not prevent because it is related to the food and it is not from trade goods. And the commentator and others mentioned: if the debtor has multiple nisabs, the debt is assigned to the easiest for payment. So it is assigned to dirhams and dinars, then to trade goods, then to grazing animals. If they are [different] types, it is assigned to the least of them, such that if he had forty sheep, thirty cows, and five camels, it is assigned to the sheep or to the camels, not to the cows, because a tabi' (calf) is above a sheep. If they are equal, like forty sheep and five camels, there is choice. And it is said: assign to the sheep so that zakat becomes due on the camels next year. Thus they stated absolutely. And al-Mabsut qualified it: if the collector (musaddiq) is present, [he decides]; if he is not present, the owner has the choice: if he wishes, he assigns the debt to the grazing animals and pays zakat from dirhams, or assigns the debt to dirhams and pays zakat from grazing animals, because in the owner's right, both are equal. End quote.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses the effect of kharaj (land tax) and 'ushr (tithe) debts on zakat. The scholars say: a debt of kharaj prevents zakat because it is a demanded debt. Similarly, if 'ushr becomes a debt in the dhimma (e.g., if the owner destroys the crop subject to 'ushr), it also prevents. However, the mere obligation of 'ushr on the crop does not prevent zakat on other assets because it is attached to the crop itself, which is not trade goods. The commentator then discusses the ranking of assets when a person has multiple types of zakatable assets (nisabs) and a debt that must be subtracted. The debt should be applied to the easiest asset to pay: first to cash (dirhams/dinars), then trade goods, then grazing animals (sa'imah). If there are multiple types of sa'imah (e.g., sheep, cows, camels), the debt is assigned to the least valuable among them (in terms of zakat unit). For example, if one has 40 sheep (zakat: 1 sheep), 30 cows (zakat: 1 tabi' which is a one-year-old calf), and 5 camels (zakat: 1 sheep for 5 camels? Actually 5 camels is less than nisab? But they consider it as a type. The example says assign to sheep or camels but not cows because a tabi' (calf) is more valuable than a sheep). If sheep and camels are equal (e.g., both would require giving one sheep), there is choice. Some say prefer sheep so that zakat on camels becomes due the following year. Al-Mabsut qualifies: if the government collector (musaddiq) is present, he decides; if not, the owner may choose which asset to assign the debt to, as long as he pays zakat from the other category.

Key Terms
الخراج (al-kharaj): Land tax on agricultural land that is not 'ushri.
العشر (al-'ushr): Tithe on crops from 'ushri land.
السوائم (al-sawa'im): Grazing livestock that are subject to zakat (sa'imah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Trade goods.
C) Cash (dirhams/dinars).
D) There is no order; the debtor chooses.

Key Takeaway
A demandable debt of kharaj or 'ushr prevents zakat, and the debt must be subtracted from the easiest asset to pay first.

Debt During the Zakat Year: Muhammad vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on debt during hawl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding debt incurred during the zakat year and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) when the debt is later absolved.

Core Idea
Debt during the zakat year: Muhammad prevents zakat; Abu Yusuf only reduces nisab.

This passage from al-Muhit presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between two great imams: Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf. The question is whether a debt that arises during the zakat year affects the obligation of zakat at the end. The text gives preference to Muhammad's view, and later commentators note that this is also the view of Zufar.

Guided Path
1
State the common scenario
A zakatable asset exists at the beginning of the hawl, but during the year, the owner incurs a debt that he must pay. The debt was not present at the start.
This is the 'dayn mu'tarid' – a debt that arises within the year.
2
Identify the two positions
Muhammad: the debt prevents zakat completely, like the wealth was destroyed. Abu Yusuf: the debt does not prevent zakat, but reduces the effective nisab proportionally.
The analogy: destruction vs. decrease.
3
Understand the analogy
If wealth is destroyed during the year, the hawl is broken and no zakat is due. Muhammad analogizes debt to destruction. Abu Yusuf analogizes debt to a decrease in wealth (like spending), where zakat is still due on the remaining amount if it reaches nisab.
This is the core qiyas.
4
Recognize the tarjih
The authors of al-Muhit present Muhammad's statement first, which indicates preference. Later authors confirm the tarjih to Muhammad's view.
Text says: 'وتقديمهم قول محمد يشعر بترجيحه'.
5
Apply the thamarah
If the creditor later absolves the debt: according to Muhammad, a new hawl begins; according to Abu Yusuf, the original year continues and zakat is due on the original nisab (subject to reduction).
This shows the practical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط : وأما الدين المعترض في خلال الحول فإنه يمنع وجوب الزكاة بمنزلة هلاكه عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف لا يمنع بمنزلة نقصانه ا هـ . وتقديمهم قول محمد يشعر بترجيحه وهو كذلك كما لا يخفى. وفائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا أبرأه فعند محمد يستأنف حولاً جديداً لا عند أبي يوسف كما في . : قوله : (وتقديمهم قول محمد يشعر بترجيحه) سيذكر المؤلف آخر باب زكاة المال ما يدل على أن هذا قول زفر حيث قال: وذكر في المجتبى الدين في خلال الحول لا يقطع حكم الحول وإن كان
And in al-Muhit: As for the debt that occurs during the year, it prevents the obligation of zakat like its destruction according to Muhammad, and according to Abu Yusuf it does not prevent, like its decrease. End quote. And their presentation of Muhammad's statement indicates its preference, and it is so, as is not hidden. The benefit of the difference appears if he [the creditor] absolves him: according to Muhammad, he begins a new year; according to Abu Yusuf, not. As in . : His statement: (And their presentation of Muhammad's statement indicates its preference) the author will mention at the end of the chapter on zakat of wealth what indicates that this is the statement of Zufar, where he said: and it is mentioned in al-Mujtaba that debt during the year does not cut off the ruling of the year, even if
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a debt that is incurred during the zakat year (hawl). The position of Muhammad (al-Shaybani) is that such a debt prevents zakat entirely, analogous to the destruction of the wealth partway through the year. Abu Yusuf holds that it does not prevent, but rather only reduces the nisab proportionally (i.e., the debt reduces the effective nisab amount). The text notes that the authors (probably of al-Muhit) give precedence to Muhammad's view. The practical difference: if the creditor later absolves the debt, then according to Muhammad, the debtor must start a new year for the remaining wealth; according to Abu Yusuf, the original year continues and zakat is due at the end. The last part references that the author of this sharh (the commentator) will later mention that this is actually the view of Zufar, as recorded in al-Mujtaba, where it says that debt during the year does not cut off the year's ruling.

Key Terms
الدين المعترض (al-dayn al-mu'tarid): A debt that arises during the zakat year, not pre-existing.
الهلاك (al-halak): Destruction of wealth; breaks the hawl.
النقصان (al-nuqsan): Decrease in wealth; reduces nisab without breaking hawl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Abu Yusuf
B) Muhammad al-Shaybani
C) Zufar
D) Both Muhammad and Abu Yusuf

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the preferred view is that a debt incurred during the year prevents zakat entirely, requiring a new hawl if later absolved.

Zakah Debt and Consumption of Nisab
Rulings on zakah when nisab is consumed after the year and when it is sold before the year

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a nisab is consumed after the year and then replaced, and explain why the new nisab is not immediately subject to zakah.

Core Idea
Consuming your nisab after the zakah year creates a debt that blocks zakah on new wealth until paid.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq examines a common scenario: what happens if you have a nisab, the year passes (so zakah is due), but you consume it before paying? The same applies if you sell it just before the year. The Hanafi school treats the unpaid zakah as a debt that occupies the new wealth, preventing repeated zakah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenarios and rulings
Read the passage and identify the three main scenarios: (1) two years unpaid with same nisab, (2) consumption after the year then new nisab, (3) selling grazing livestock before the year. Note the different rulings.
The two-year rule may need clarification; focus on consumption and sale branches.
2
Analyze the reasoning for consumption
Why does consumption after the year create a debt? Because zakah was wajib at the end of the year, but the wealth is gone; the obligation shifts to the dhimah as a debt. This debt then prevents zakah on new wealth.
3
Compare with perishing (not consumption)
If the nisab perishes naturally (not consumed by owner), the obligation is waived, and new wealth is zakatable. Understand the distinction.
4
Apply the sale branch
Selling a grazing animal before the year is considered consumption (istihlak). The substitute (e.g., another animal or cash) starts a new year. This is built on the principle that the original nisab is consumed by the sale.
This ruling applies to sa'imah only; non-grazing animals are different.
5
Note the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position is that debt from unpaid zakah prevents zakah on new property. This is the view adopted by later Hanafi authorities like those in Fath al-Qadir and al-Bada'i'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلو كان له نصاب حال عليه حولان ولم يزكه فيهما لا زكاة عليه في الحول الثاني، ولو كان له خمس وعشرون من الإبل لم يزكه حولين كان عليه في الحول الأول بنت مخاض وللحول الثاني أربع شياه، ولو كان له نصاب حال عليه الحول فلم يز كه ثم استهلكه ثم استفاد غيره وحال على النصاب المستفاد الحول لا زكاة فيه لاشتغال خمسة منه بدين المستهلك بخلاف ما لو كان الأول لم يستهلك بل هلك فإنه يجب في المستفاد لسقوط زكاة الأول بالهلاك، وبخلاف ما لو استهلك قبل الحول حيث لا يجب شيء. ومن فروعه ما إذا باع نصاب السائمة قبل الحول بيوم بسائمة مثلها أو من جنس آخر أو بدراهم يريد به الفرار من الصدقة أو لا يريد لا يجب عليه الزكاة في البدل إلا بحول جديد أو يكون له ما يضمه إليه في صورة الدراهم وهذا بناء على أن استبداله السائمة بغيرها مطلقاً استهلاك بخلاف غير السائمة. كذا في فتح القدير وفي البدائع
If he had a nisab on which two years passed and he did not pay zakah in them, no zakah is due on him for the second year. And if he had twenty-five camels and did not pay zakah for two years, he owes for the first year a bint makhad and for the second year four sheep. And if he had a nisab on which the year passed and he did not pay zakah, then he consumed it, then acquired other wealth, and the year passed on the acquired nisab, no zakah is due in it because five dirhams of it are occupied by the debt of the consumed (zakah). Contrary to if the first (nisab) was not consumed but perished, then zakah is due on the acquired because the zakah of the first is waived by destruction. And contrary to if he consumed before the year, where nothing is due. Among its branches: if he sells a nisab of grazing livestock one day before the year for a similar grazing animal or from another type or for dirhams, intending to evade charity or not, zakah is not due on the substitute except with a new year, or he has something to add to it in the case of dirhams. This is built on the fact that replacing a grazing animal with something else is absolutely consumption, contrary to non-grazing animals. Thus in Fath al-Qadir and al-Bada'i'.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents several rulings on zakah timing and loss. First, if a nisab remains for two years without payment, the zakah for the second year is not due? But the example with 25 camels contradicts, so the first statement likely means the second year's zakah is not due if the nisab is the same type? Actually the standard Hanafi view is that each year's zakah is independent. The text then addresses consumption: if someone has a nisab at the end of the year (so zakah is due) but he consumes it before paying, then later acquires a new nisab, no zakah is due on the new one because his dhimah is occupied by the unpaid zakah of the first (as a debt). Exception: if the first was lost (not consumed by him), then the debt is waived and he must pay on the new nisab. Also, if consumption occurred before the year, no zakah was ever due. Branches: selling a grazing animal just before the year to another grazing animal or cash: the new animal has a fresh year, because the sale is considered consumption. This is the mufta bihi position.

Key Terms
مستهلك (mustahlik): The original nisab that was consumed (used up) by the owner before paying zakah.
سائمة (sa'imah): Grazing livestock (camels, cattle, sheep/goats) that are kept for milk, breeding, or meat and allowed to graze freely.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He must pay zakah on the new nisab immediately.
B) He must pay zakah on the original nisab but not the new.
C) No zakah on either because the original was consumed.
D) He does not owe zakah on the new nisab even after a year passes until the previous zakah is paid.

Key Takeaway
Unpaid zakah creates a debt that blocks zakah on new wealth until the debt is discharged.
