8 min
Effect of Demandable Debt on Zakah Obligation
Ruling on kharaj and 'ushr debts affecting zakah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which debts prevent zakah and apply the Hanafi order of discharging debt from different types of nisab.

Core Idea
A demandable debt occupies the nisab and prevents zakah; debt is discharged from the least valuable property first.

In this lesson, we examine how various debts—such as kharaj (land tax) and 'ushr (tithe)—affect the obligation of zakah. The Hanafi school distinguishes between debts that are immediately demandable and those that are merely attached to property. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly calculating zakah when the owner has multiple types of wealth. The passage also provides a priority order for discharging debt to minimize the impact on zakah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on kharaj and 'ushr
The passage states that kharaj debt prevents zakah because it is demandable. Similarly, if 'ushr becomes a personal debt (e.g., through destruction of produce), it also prevents zakah. Note the key condition is that the debt is demandable (yutalabu bih).
2
Distinguish between original 'ushr obligation and personal 'ushr debt
The original obligation of 'ushr does not prevent zakah because it is attached to the produce, not a personal liability. But if the produce is destroyed, the 'ushr becomes a debt in the dhimah and then prevents zakah.
This distinction is unique to Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the priority order for discharging debt from multiple nisab types
The passage gives an order: first from cash, then trade goods, then grazing livestock. Within livestock, the least valuable type is used. If equal, the debtor chooses. This preserves more valuable or zakah-eligible property.
The order is based on ease of payment and preserving property subject to future zakah.
4
Understand the qualification from al-Mabsut
If the tax collector is present, he decides the order; if absent, the owner has the choice. This shows the interplay between public authority and private right in Hanafi law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقالوا دين الخراج يمنع وجوب الزكاة لأنه يطالب به، وكذا إذا صار العشر ديناً في الذمة بأن أتلف الطعام العشري صاحبه، فأما وجوب العشر فلا يمنع لأنه متعلق بالطعام وهو ليس من مال التجارة، وذكر الشارح وغيره إن كان للمديون نصب يصرف الدين إلى الأيسر قضاء فيصرف إلى الدراهم والدنانير ثم إلى عروض التجارة ثم إلى السوائم، فإن كانت أجناساً صرف إلى أقلها حتى لو كان له أربعون من الغنم وثلاثون من البقر وخمس من الإبل صرف إلى الغنم أو إلى الإبل دون البقر لأن التبيع فوق الشاة فإن استويا خير كأربعين من الغنم وخمس من الإبل. وقيل: يصرف إلى الغنم لتجب الزكاة في الإبل في العام القابل هكذا أطلقوا. وقيده في المبسوط بأن يحضر المصدق أي الساعي فإن لم يحضره فالخيار إلى صاحب المال إن شاء صرف الدين إلى السائمة وأدى الزكاة من الدراهم وإن شاء صرف الدين إلى الدراهم وأدى الزكاة من السائمة لأن في حق صاحب المال هما سواء ا هـ .
And they said: The debt of kharaj (land tax) prevents the obligation of zakah because it is demanded. Similarly, if 'ushr (tithe) becomes a debt in the dhimah, such as when the owner destroys the 'ushri food. As for the obligation of 'ushr, it does not prevent (zakah) because it is attached to the food, and it is not trade goods. And the commentator and others mentioned: if the debtor has different types of nisab, the debt is discharged from the easiest (to pay), so it is discharged from dirhams and dinars, then from trade goods, then from grazing livestock. If there are multiple types, it is discharged from the least (valuable) so that if he has forty sheep, thirty cows, and five camels, it is discharged from sheep or from camels, not from cows, because a tabi' (young cow) is worth more than a sheep. If they are equal, he chooses, like forty sheep and five camels. And it is said: it is discharged from sheep so that zakah becomes due on the camels in the coming year. This is how they expressed it in absolute terms. And al-Mabsut qualified it: that the collector (musaddiq) must be present; if he is not present, the owner has the choice: if he wishes, he discharges the debt from the grazing livestock and pays zakah from dirhams, or he discharges from dirhams and pays zakah from the grazing livestock, because from the owner's perspective both are equal.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage discusses which debts prevent zakah. Kharaj (land tax) is a demandable debt, so if someone owes kharaj, his nisab is considered 'occupied' and no zakah is due. Similarly, if 'ushr (tithe on land produce) becomes a personal debt (e.g., if the owner destroys the produce), it also prevents zakah. However, the original obligation of 'ushr itself does not prevent zakah on trade goods, because 'ushr is tied to the produce itself. The text then clarifies how to allocate the debt when the debtor has multiple types of nisab: the debt is paid from the easiest type to spare: cash first, then trade goods, then livestock. Within livestock, the least valuable type is used to settle the debt, so that the more valuable types remain subject to zakah. If the tax collector is absent, the owner has the option to decide which type to use for debt payment.

Key Terms
خراج (kharaj): A land tax levied on agricultural land; a public debt.
عشر ('ushr): Tithe (10% or 5%) on agricultural produce from non-kharaj land; when due, it's attached to the produce.
دين الخراج (dayn al-kharaj): The debt of kharaj, a demandable liability on land.
مصدق (musaddiq): The tax collector (sai) who collects zakah and other dues.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of property is the last to be used to settle a debt affecting zakah, according to the Hanafi order?
A) Dirhams and dinars
B) Trade goods
C) Grazing livestock
D) Land

Key Takeaway
Only demandable debts prevent zakah; the debt is discharged from the easiest property first to preserve the nisab for zakah.

Debt During the Zakah Year: Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on debt during the zakah year

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the positions of Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf on the effect of debt incurred during the zakah year and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
A debt during the year cuts off the zakah year like destruction according to Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf.

Zakah is a pillar of Islam with detailed rules regarding the annual cycle. A key nuance is how debts that arise during the year affect the obligation. This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute between the two great imams, Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf, on whether such a debt resets the year or merely reduces the nisab. Understanding this ikhtilaf is crucial for accurate fatwa on zakah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage from al-Muhit and note the views: Muhammad says the debt prevents zakah (like complete destruction), Abu Yusuf says it does not prevent (like a decrease).
Both are within the Hanafi school; the dispute is over the nature of the debt's effect.
2
Understand the analogies
Muhammad analogizes debt to 'halak' (destruction) – the year is nullified. Abu Yusuf analogizes to 'nuqsan' (decrease) – the nisab is reduced but the year continues.
The choice of analogy determines the ruling on year continuity.
3
Note the preferred view (tarjih)
The text indicates that mentioning Muhammad's view first suggests its preference, and the author confirms it is the relied-upon view.
Thus the mufta bihi is Muhammad's position.
4
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
If the debt is later forgiven, Muhammad requires a new year to begin, while Abu Yusuf continues the original year. This affects when zakah becomes due.
The difference only appears after forgiveness, not at the moment of debt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المحيط: وأما الدين المعترض في خلال الحول فإنه يمنع وجوب الزكاة بمنزلة هلاكه عند محمد، وعند أبي يوسف لا يمنع بمنزلة نقصانه ا هـ . وتقديمهم قول محمد يشعر بترجيحه وهو كذلك كما لا يخفى. وفائدة الخلاف تظهر فيما إذا أبرأه فعند محمد يستأنف حولاً جديداً لا عند أبي يوسف كما في المحيط أيضاً. وأما الحادث بعد الحول فلا يسقط الزكاة اتفاقاً كذا في الخانية وغيرها. وعلى هذا من ضمن دركاً في بيع فاستحق المبيع بعد الحول لم تسقط الزكاة لأن الدين إنما وجب عليه عند الاستحقاق كذا في غاية البيان.
And in al-Muhit: As for a debt that occurs during the year, it prevents the obligation of zakah like destruction (halak) according to Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, it does not prevent but is like a decrease (nuqsan). End quote. And their precedence (mentioning Muhammad's view first) suggests its preference, and it is so, as is not hidden. The practical difference appears when he releases (the debt): according to Muhammad, a new year begins; according to Abu Yusuf, not, as in al-Muhit. And a debt occurring after the year does not cancel zakah by agreement (ittifaqan), as in al-Khaniyah and others. And based on this, if someone guarantees a defect (dark') in a sale and the sold item is reclaimed after the year, zakah is not dropped because the debt only became due at the time of reclamation, as in Ghayat al-Bayan.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding debts that arise during the zakah year. Imam Muhammad holds that such a debt prevents zakah completely, just as if the nisab was destroyed (halak), meaning the year is interrupted and a new year begins after the debt is paid. Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the debt merely reduces the nisab, like a decrease (nuqsan), so it does not cancel the year; the owner must still consider the remaining wealth. The text indicates that the preferred view (mufta bihi) is Imam Muhammad's, as his opinion is often given precedence. The practical result: if the creditor later forgives the debt, according to Muhammad a new year starts from that point; according to Abu Yusuf the original year continues. Some other rules: a debt incurred after the year does not affect the obligation at all by consensus, and this also applies to a guarantee for a defect, where the debt is only realized after the year.

Key Terms
دين معترض (dayn mu'tarid): A debt that arises during the zakah year, after the nisab has been held.
هلاك (halak): Destruction of the nisab; cancels the year completely.
نقصان (nuqsan): Decrease; reduces the nisab but does not cancel the year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person incurs a debt in the middle of the zakah year and the creditor forgives it after the year, what is the result according to Imam Muhammad?
A) Zakah is due for the entire year from the beginning.
B) A new zakah year begins from the date of forgiveness.
C) No zakah is due at all.
D) Zakah is due from the start of the original year minus the debt amount.

Key Takeaway
The preferred Hanafi view is that a debt arising during the zakah year interrupts the year, requiring a new year after the debt is released.

Zakah on Usurped and Mixed Property (Ghasb)
Ruling on zakah for mixed usurped property and debt blockage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether zakah is due on usurped property mixed with the usurper's own, and explain the effect of a lingering debt obligation.

Core Idea
Mixing usurped property creates ownership for Abu Hanifa, but debt still blocks zakah — a tension within the school.

The previous discussion linked zakah to debt through kafalah. Now the text examines a more complex scenario: what if property is not only owed (as debt) but also usurped and mixed? This brings in a core intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and a further nuance about whether a debt that attaches to the property prevents the obligation of zakah. The passage is a critical refinement of the conditions for zakah.

Guided Path
1
Understand the kafalah example
The text first uses a case: ten guarantors each have 1000, but each is exempt from zakah because the creditor can claim from any. This illustrates that a guarantee debt (dayn kafalah) blocks zakah even though each physically has the nisab.
This sets up the principle that debt prevents zakah regardless of possession.
2
Analyze the two-usurper case
If A usurps 1000 from B, then C usurps the same 1000 from A, and B acquits both: A pays zakah (can recourse to C), C does not (no recourse). The debt of C is final, so it blocks zakah.
Contrast: here the debt arises from guarantee of the original owner's claim.
3
Examine the sultan-mixing case
A sultan usurps property and mixes it with his own. Abu Hanifa: mixing = consumption, so he owns it and must pay zakah. Sahibayn: no ownership without liability (daman), so no zakah.
The key difference is whether mixing transfers ownership.
4
Identify the problem with Abu Hanifa's view
Even if the sultan owns the mixed property, he still owes its value to the victim. That debt should block zakah. The text notes this as a problem (ishkal) and mentions Zufar holds the debt 'cuts' the year, while the three imams say it does not.
The author hints at a tension and suggests consulting al-Jawhara for reconciliation.
5
Determine the practical outcome
According to Abu Hanifa, the usurper pays zakah and the mixed property is inherited as his estate. According to the Sahibayn, there is no inheritance of the whole — only the original owner's share.
This thamarah al-khilaf shows real consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل كلامه الدين بطريق الأصالة وبطريق الكفالة، ولذا قال في المحيط: لو استقرض ألفاً فكفل عنه عشرة ولكل ألف في بيته وحال الحول فلا زكاة على واحد منهم لشغله بدين الكفالة لأن له أن يأخذ من أيهم شاء بخلاف ما إذا كان له ألف وغصب ألفاً وغصبها منه آخر له ألف وحال الحول على مال الغاصبين ثم أبرأهما فإنه يزكي الغاصب الأول ألفه والغاصب الثاني لا، لأن الغاصب الأول لو ضمن يرجع على الثاني والثاني لو ضمن لا يرجع على الأول فكان قرار الضمان عليه فصار الدين عليه مانعاً اهـ. وظاهره أنه لو لم يبرئهما لا يكون الحكم كذلك. وفي فتح القدير وغيره: لا يخرج عن ملك النصاب المذكور ما ملك بسبب خبيث ولذا قالوا: لو أن سلطاناً غصب مالاً وخلطه صار ملكاً له وجبت عليه الزكاة وورث عنه على قول أبي حنيفة لأن خلط دراهمه بدراهم غيره عنده استهلاك، أما على قولهما فلا يضمن فلا يثبت الملك لأنه فرع الضمان فلا يورث عنه لأنه مال مشترك فإنما يورث حصة الميت منه . وفي الولوالجية: وقوله أرفق بالناس إذ قل ما يخلو عن غصب .اهـ. هكذا ذكروا وهو مشكل لأنه وإن كان ملكه عند أبي حنيفة بالخلط فهو مشغول بالدين والشرط الفراغ عنه فينبغي أن لا تجب الزكاة فيه على قوله أيضاً، ولذا شرط مال حتى مستغرقاً. وقال زفر: يقطع اهـ. وظاهره أن عدم القطع أي عدم منعه وجوب الزكاة قول علمائنا الثلاثة خلاف ما هنا فتأمل وانظر ما في الجوهرة فلعله يفيد التوفيق .
And his statement includes debt by way of original (debtor) and by way of guarantee (kafalah). Hence he said in al-Muhit: If someone borrows a thousand and ten persons guarantee him, and each has a thousand in his home, and the year passes, no zakah is due on any of them because of occupation with the guarantee debt, since the creditor may take from any of them. Contrary to if he had a thousand and usurped a thousand and another person usurped it from him, and each has a thousand, and the year passes on the wealth of the two usurpers, then he acquits both: the first usurper pays zakah on his thousand, the second does not, because the first, if he pays, can recourse against the second, while the second cannot recourse against the first, so the burden of guarantee falls on the second, thus the debt prevents (zakah) for him. End quote. Its apparent meaning is that if he did not acquit them, the ruling would not be so. And in Fath al-Qadir and others: What is owned by a bad cause (sabab khabith) does not leave the ownership of the mentioned nisab. Hence they said: If a sultan usurps property and mixes it (with his own), it becomes his property and zakah is due on him, and it is inherited from him according to Abu Hanifa, because mixing his dirhams with others' is consumption according to him. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he does not become liable, so ownership is not established because it depends on liability (daman); thus it is not inherited because it is shared property; only the deceased's share is inherited. And in al-Walwalijiyya: 'His statement is kinder to people, for few are free from usurpation.' End quote. Thus they mentioned it, but it is problematic because even if it is his property according to Abu Hanifa by mixing, it is occupied by a debt, and the condition (for zakah) is freedom from debt, so zakah should not be due on it according to his view either. Hence he conditioned 'even if it is fully consumed (mustaghriqan).' And Zufar said: It cuts (the year). End quote. Its apparent meaning is that non-cutting (i.e., not preventing zakah) is the view of our three imams, contrary to what is here. So ponder and look at what is in al-Jawhara; perhaps it provides reconciliation.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage deals with zakah on usurped (ghasb) property and the effect of mixing. First, it connects to the previous discussion on debt by kafalah: if someone has money but also is a guarantor, that debt prevents zakah. Then it gives a case: two usurpers, one after the other. If the original owner acquits both, the first usurper pays zakah because he can claim from the second, but the second does not because he has no recourse. Then it discusses the sultan who usurps property and mixes it with his own. According to Abu Hanifa, mixing leads to ownership (by consumption), so the sultan must pay zakah and it is inherited. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, no ownership without liability, so no zakah and inheritance is only of the original owner's share. The text then notes a problem: even if ownership is established by mixing, there is still a debt owed to the original owner, which should prevent zakah. The author notes that Zufar holds that the debt 'cuts' the year (prevents zakah), while the three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold it does not. But the earlier text seemed to indicate a different position, so the author advises caution and refers to al-Jawhara for reconciliation.

Key Terms
غصب (ghasb): Usurpation; taking property illegally with the intention of ownership.
خلط (khalat): Mixing of properties, e.g., dirhams of the usurper with dirhams of the victim.
استهلاك (istihlak): Consumption; treating the property as one's own such that it is no longer distinguishable.
ضمان (daman): Liability; responsibility for loss or return of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because mixing does not cause consumption according to Zufar.

Key Takeaway
Ownership through mixing usurped property does not automatically free the property from the debt claim, creating a tension within the Hanafi school regarding the obligation of zakah.

Debt from Guarantee Prevents Zakat
States the ruling on zakat when wealth is occupied by debt from guarantee

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that zakat is not due on wealth encumbered by a debt from guarantee, and apply this ruling to distinguish cases where the debt is finally settled from those where it is not.

Core Idea
Debt from guarantee occupies wealth and prevents zakat, even if multiple guarantors exist.

The obligation of zakat requires that wealth be fully owned and free from encumbrances. One common encumbrance is debt arising from a guarantee (kafalah). In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling that such debt prevents zakat, even if the guarantor has not yet paid. The text from al-Muhit, cited in Bahr al-Ra'iq, clarifies this through two contrasting examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its general ruling
Zakat is normally wajib on wealth that reaches nisab and a lunar year passes. This is the baseline.
2
Identify the preventing condition
Debt that is claimable from the owner – including debt from guarantee – prevents the obligation because the wealth is not fully at the owner's disposal.
The key term is shughl al-mal bi al-dayn (occupation of wealth by debt).
3
Analyze the multiple guarantor example
If several people guarantee a single debt, each has a thousand dirhams and a year passes, none pays zakat because the creditor can claim from any of them. Each guarantor's wealth is fully occupied.
Al-Muhit uses this to show that the potential liability, not actual payment, matters.
4
Analyze the usurper example
Two usurpers: the first usurps from the owner, the second usurps from the first. After the owner releases them (ibra'), only the first pays zakat. Why? The first can recover from the second, so his wealth is not ultimately burdened. The second cannot recover, so his wealth remains occupied.
This illustrates that the burden of final liability is the decisive factor.
5
Articulate the underlying principle
A debt prevents zakat when it is actually claimable and not finally settled. Once the debt is waived or settled in a way that removes the owner's ultimate liability, the wealth becomes free and zakat becomes due (subject to other conditions).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شمل كلامه الدين بطريق الأصالة وبطريق الكفالة، ولذا قال في المحيط : لو استقرض ألفاً فكفل عنه عشرة ولكل ألف في بيته وحال الحول فلا زكاة على واحد منهم لشغله بدين الكفالة لأن له أن يأخذ من أيهم شاء بخلاف ما إذا كان له ألف وغصب ألفاً وغصبها منه آخر له ألف وحال الحول على مال الغاصبين ثم أبرأهما فإنه يزكي الغاصب الأول ألفه والغاصب الثاني لا، لأن الغاصب الأول لو ضمن يرجع على الثاني والثاني لو ضمن لا يرجع على الأول فكان قرار الضمان عليه فصار الدين عليه مانعاً اهـ. وظاهره أنه لو لم يبرئهما لا يكون الحكم كذلك.
His statement includes debt by way of original obligation and by way of guarantee. Thus, al-Muhit states: If a person borrows a thousand dirhams and ten persons stand as guarantors for him, each having a thousand dirhams at home, and a year passes, no zakat is due from any of them because their wealth is occupied by the debt of guarantee, since [the creditor] may take from whomever he wishes. Unlike the case where a person has a thousand dirhams and usurps a thousand, and another usurps that thousand from him, each [usurper] has a thousand, and a year passes on the wealth of the two usurpers, then [the original owner] releases them: the first usurper gives zakat on his thousand and the second does not, because if the first is held liable he can recover from the second, whereas if the second is held liable he cannot recover from the first. Thus the burden of liability is on him, and the debt becomes a preventer. End quote. And the apparent meaning is that if he does not release them, the ruling is not like that.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This is an expansion from al-Muhit explaining the ruling that debt prevents zakat. It covers two cases: first, if multiple people guarantee a loan, each has the same potential liability, so no zakat is due from any. Second, the example of two usurpers shows that if debt is waived (ibrā'), the one who bears ultimate liability still does not owe zakat, while the one who can recover from him does owe zakat. The key principle is that debt which is actually claimable or likely to be claimed prevents the obligation.

Key Terms
دين الكفالة (dayn al-kafalah): Debt arising from a guarantee, where the guarantor becomes liable if the principal fails to pay.
شغل المال بالدين (shughl al-mal bi al-dayn): The wealth is encumbered by a debt such that the owner is not considered fully independent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the two usurpers, why does the second usurper not have to pay zakat even after the creditor releases them?
A) Because he is not the original debtor.
B) Because the burden of liability is ultimately on him, so his wealth is considered occupied by debt.
C) Because his wealth is less than the nisab.
D) Because the creditor cannot claim from him.

Key Takeaway
Debt from guarantee prevents zakat because the wealth is occupied by a claimable liability.

Zakat on Mixed Usurped Property: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat for mixed usurped property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi positions on zakat for a sultan who mixes usurped property, and identify the condition of freedom from debt.

Core Idea
Mixing creates ownership for Abu Hanifa, but zakat is not due until debt is cleared.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a ruler who usurps property and mixes it with his own. The disagreement touches on fundamental questions: Does mixing (khalit) transfer ownership? And if so, does zakat become immediately obligatory? The key is the condition that the wealth must be free from debt (shughl al-dayn). We will trace the positions of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar, and how the later tradition resolves the tension.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core legal question
The central question is: Is zakat obligatory on a sultan who has mixed usurped property with his own? And what conditions affect the obligation?
The passage is a commentary expansion on earlier texts.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
According to Abu Hanifa, mixing similar dirhams constitutes consumption (istihlak), so ownership transfers to the sultan. Consequently, zakat is wajib and the property is inheritable.
Abu Hanifa's view is based on customary practice of mixing.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) argue that no liability (damān) attaches to mixing alone; therefore ownership does not transfer, and no zakat is due.
They treat the property as joint, not owned by the sultan.
4
Introduce Zufar's view on debt
Zufar holds that debt absolutely cuts off the obligation of zakat, regardless of ownership. Even if ownership is established, the presence of debt prevents zakat.
This is a stricter view within the Hanafi school.
5
Analyse the qualification: debt and ibrā'
Even under Abu Hanifa's view, the sultan's wealth is occupied by debt to the original owners. The text emphasises that zakat only becomes due after the creditors release him (ibrā'). Without ibrā', the condition of freedom from debt is not met.
This qualification is presented as a 'good condition that must be preserved'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتح القدير وغيره : لا يخرج عن ملك النصاب المذكور ما ملك بسبب خبيث ولذا قالوا: لو أن سلطاناً غصب مالاً وخلطه صار ملكاً له وجبت عليه الزكاة وورث عنه على قول أبي حنيفة لأن خلط دراهمه بدراهم غيره عنده استهلاك، أما على قولهما فلا يضمن فلا يثبت الملك لأنه فرع الضمان فلا يورث عنه لأنه مال مشترك فإنما يورث حصة الميت منه . وفي الولوالجية : وقوله أرفق بالناس إذ قل ما يخلو عن غصب .اهـ. هكذا ذكروا وهو مشكل لأنه وإن كان ملكه عند أبي حنيفة بالخلط فهو مشغول بالدين والشرط الفراغ عنه فينبغي أن لا تجب الزكاة فيه على قوله أيضاً، ولذا شرط مال حتى مستغرقاً. وقال زفر : يقطع اهـ. وظاهره أن عدم القطع أي عدم منعه وجوب الزكاة قول علمائنا الثلاثة خلاف ما هنا فتأمل وانظر ما في الجوهرة فلعله يفيد التوفيق . ممن شاء قوله : (الشغله بدين الكفالة أقول : إنما يتحقق الشغل في مال من يأخذ منه صاحب الدين فينبغي أن يكون المراد أنه لا تتعين الزكاة في مال واحد منهم لأن صاحب الدين له الخيار في الأخذ منهم، فكل منهم يحتمل أن يكون ماله مشغولاً لكن بعد تعيين صاحب الدين واحداً منهم للأخذ ظهر شغل مال ذلك الواحد وظهر عدم وجوب الزكاة في ماله بخلاف غيره منهم فإنه قد ظهر عدم ذلك فينبغي لزوم الزكاة في مالهم حينئذ لتحقق عدم الشغل تأمل . لكن قد يقال : إنه قبل الأخذ من أحدهم كان مال كل واحد بانفراده مستحقاً لقضاء الدين، فإذا مضى الحول كذلك لم يتحقق سبب وجوب الزكاة على واحد منهم قوله : (والغاصب الثاني (لا أي لا يزكي ألفه لما يذكره من أن إقرار الضمان عليه لكن يتعين تقييد ذلك بما إذا استهلك الغاضب الثاني الألف إذ لو بقيت معه يزكي ألفه لأنها سالمة من الضمان لأنه يلزمه رد ما غصبه قوله : (ولذا قوله لو أن سلطاناً غصب مالا وخلطه الخ) أي خلطه بماله، إما إذا لم يكن له مال وغصب أموال الناس وخلطها ببعضها فلا زكاة عليه لما في القنية : لو كان الخبيث نصاباً لا يلزمه الزكاة لأن الكل واجب التصدق عليه فلا يفيد إيجاب التصدق ببعضه ومثله في البزازية . قال في الشرنبلالية : في المبتغى - بالمعجمة - أن يبرئه أصحاب الأموال لأنه قبل الإبراء مشغول بالدين وهو قيد حسن يجب حفظه.
In Fath al-Qadeer and others: That which is acquired through an illicit means does not leave the ownership of the mentioned nisab. Hence they said: If a sultan usurps wealth and mixes it, it becomes his ownership, and zakat is obligatory upon him, and it is inherited from him according to Abu Hanifa because mixing his dirhams with others' dirhams is considered consumption (istihlak) in his view. As for the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he does not become liable for it (damān), so ownership is not established because it is subsidiary to liability (damān). Thus it is not inherited from him because it is joint property; only the deceased's share is inherited. And in al-Walwalijiyya: His statement 'more convenient for the people, for rarely is anyone free from usurpation.' End quote. They mention this, but it is problematic because even if the property becomes his ownership according to Abu Hanifa by mixing, it is occupied by debt, and the condition is that it be free; thus zakat should not be obligatory upon it according to his view as well, hence he stipulated that the wealth be [?] 'even if it is completely covered [by debt].' Zufar said: 'It cuts off [the obligation].' End quote. And its apparent meaning is that not cutting off—i.e., not preventing the obligation of zakat—is the view of our three imams, contrary to what is here. So reflect and look at what is in al-Jawhara, perhaps it provides reconciliation. 'From whomever he wishes.' His statement: (the occupation due to debt of guarantee) I say: Occupation is only realized in the wealth of the person from whom the creditor takes. So it should mean that zakat is not determined for any one of them, because the creditor has the option to take from any. So each of them is likely that his wealth is occupied, but after the creditor identifies one of them for taking, the occupation of that one's wealth becomes apparent, and the absence of zakat in his wealth becomes apparent, unlike the others: the absence [of occupation] becomes apparent, so zakat should be obligatory in their wealth then, due to the realization of non-occupation. Reflect. But it may be said: Before taking from any of them, the wealth of each individually was subject to repayment of the debt; if the year passed thus, the cause for zakat obligation was not realized for any one of them. His statement: (and the second usurper does not) i.e., he does not give zakat on his thousand due to what he mentions that the affirmation of liability is upon him. But this should be restricted to the case where the second usurper consumed the thousand; if it remains with him, he gives zakat on his thousand because it is safe from liability, since he is obliged to return what he usurped. His statement: (hence they said: if a sultan usurps wealth and mixes it, etc.) i.e., mixes it with his own wealth; as for if he has no wealth and usurps people's wealth and mixes them together, no zakat is due on him, because in al-Qunyah: If the illicit property reaches nisab, zakat is not obligatory because all of it must be given in charity, so obligating charity on part of it is not beneficial. Similarly in al-Bazzaziyya. It is stated in al-Shurnublaliyya: In al-Mubtagha (with shadda on the gheen): that the owners of the wealth release him, because before the release he is occupied by debt, and this is a good condition that must be preserved.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rulings for a sultan who usurps and mixes property. It begins by discussing a quotation from Fath al-Qadeer about property acquired by illicit means (khabeeth). The main dispute is between Abu Hanifa and his two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding ownership by mixing (khalit). According to Abu Hanifa, when a sultan mixes usurped property with his own (e.g., similar dirhams), he becomes the owner because the mixing amounts to consumption (istihlak). Consequently, zakat is obligatory and the property is inheritable. According to the two companions, no liability (damān) attaches to mixing itself, so ownership does not pass; the property remains joint. The author then notes a problem: even under Abu Hanifa's view, the sultan's wealth is occupied by debt (i.e., he owes the value to the original owners), so zakat should still not be obligatory. The text mentions Zufar's view that debt 'cuts off' the obligation absolutely. The commentary then explains the matn quotes regarding occupation (shughl) in cases of guarantee and the case of the second usurper. It clarifies that before the creditor chooses a payer, no one's obligation is fixed; after choice, only the chosen one's wealth is occupied. For the second usurper, the rule is conditional: if he consumed the usurped property, his wealth is occupied; if it remains, he must pay zakat because he must return the very property. Finally, the passage addresses a sultan who has no other wealth and mixes usurped goods; since all his property is illicit and must be given away, no zakat is due. The condition that the creditors must release him (ibrā') is emphasized as a 'good condition' that must be preserved for zakat to become obligatory.

Key Terms
خلط (khalit): Mixing of usurped property with one's own, which according to Abu Hanifa results in ownership if the property is fungible (mithli).
خبيث (khabeeth): Property acquired through impermissible means, such as usurpation or theft.
إبراء (ibrā'): Release from debt or liability by the creditors, a condition for zakat obligation on mixed property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what condition must be met for zakat to be obligatory on a sultan who has mixed usurped property with his own?
A) He must be able to distinguish the usurped property.
B) The owners must release him from liability (ibrā').
C) The property must be used in trade.
D) A year must pass after the mixing.

Key Takeaway
Zakat on mixed usurped property is not automatic; it requires that the wealth be free from debt (ibrā'), even according to Abu Hanifa's view that mixing transfers ownership.

Debt and Financial Obligations: A Hanafi Distinction
Clarifies which obligations are prevented by debt and which are not

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which obligations (zakat, 'ushr, kharaj, sadaqat al-fitr, kaffarah) are prevented by debt and which are not, citing the Hanafi position.

Core Idea
Debt blocks zakat and sadaqat al-fitr, but not 'ushr, kharaj, or kaffarah.

This lesson clarifies a crucial rule in Hanafi fiqh: debt does not universally prevent all financial obligations. While zakat and sadaqat al-fitr are waived for a debtor, the obligations of 'ushr, kharaj, and kaffarah remain binding. Understanding this distinction requires grasping the underlying principle—whether the obligation is tied to the person's wealth or to a specific asset or cause.

Guided Path
1
List the obligations mentioned
Identify the five obligations: zakat, 'ushr (agricultural tithe), kharaj (land tax), sadaqat al-fitr, and kaffarah (expiation).
2
Recall the rule for each
For each, determine whether debt prevents the obligation: zakat and sadaqat al-fitr are prevented; 'ushr, kharaj, and kaffarah are not.
The text uses 'al-ashah' for kaffarah, indicating the soundest view.
3
Understand the rationale
Reflect on why: 'ushr and kharaj are rights of the land, not dependent on the owner's solvency; kaffarah is based on 'al-qudra al-muyassara' (available ability) even if in debt.
Debt reduces one's capacity to pay but does not negate the cause-based obligations.
4
Note the sources
The ruling is cited from al-Khaniyya and al-Kashf al-Kabir, establishing its authority in the Hanafi school.
These are works of zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيد المصنف بالزكاة لأن الدين لا يمنع وجوب العشر والخراج ويمنع في صدقة الفطر كذا في الخانية. وأما التكفير بالمال فلا يمنع الدين وجوبه على الأصح . كذا الكشف الكبير من بحث القدرة الميسرة.
The author restricted [the ruling about debt] to zakat because debt does not prevent the obligation of 'ushr (tithe) and kharaj (land tax), but it does prevent in the case of sadaqat al-fitr (charity of fast-breaking). Thus in al-Khaniyya. As for expiation (kaffarah) with wealth, debt does not prevent its obligation according to the soundest view. Likewise in al-Kashf al-Kabir from the discussion of 'ability that is available' (al-qudra al-muyassara).
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is a concise ruling that distinguishes between different financial obligations. The author of the matn (Kanz al-Daqaiq) specifically mentions 'zakat' because debt only prevents zakat (and sadaqat al-fitr), but does not prevent 'ushr (tithe on agricultural produce) and kharaj (land tax). Furthermore, kaffarah (expiation for sins like breaking a oath) is not prevented by debt; it remains obligatory according to the correct view. The source references al-Khaniyya and al-Kashf al-Kabir. This shows that the Hanafi school differentiates based on the nature of the obligation: some are tied to the land or to a specific cause, independent of the payer's debt status.

Key Terms
العشر (al-'ushr): Tithe on agricultural produce from lands subject to 'ushr; obligation is connected to the land, not the owner's personal wealth.
الخراج (al-kharaj): Land tax on kharaj lands; also connected to the land.
القدرة الميسرة (al-qudra al-muyassara): The ability to perform an act if one has access to the means, even if in debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT prevented by debt?
A) Zakat on wealth.
B) Sadaqat al-fitr.
C) 'Ushr on crops.
D) Kaffarah (expiation) with wealth.

Key Takeaway
Debt prevents the obligation of zakat and sadaqat al-fitr, but not 'ushr, kharaj, or kaffarah, due to the different nature of these obligations.

Zakat on Found Property (Luqatah)
Ruling on zakat for found property after one year of announcement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat for found property (luqatah) and identify the legal reason (ownership not transferred) for the absence of zakat.

Core Idea
Found property does not become the finder's property; after a year, it must be given to the poor, so no zakat is due.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario: a person finds a sum of money and announces it for a year without locating the owner. The common misconception is that the finder becomes the owner after a year and must pay zakat. The Hanafi ruling clarifies the opposite: the finder must give the property in charity, and thus no zakat is owed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is finding property (luqatah). The ruling: the finder must announce it for a year.
Announcement (al-ta'reef) is a required process.
2
Determine the disposition after a year
If the owner is not found, the property must be given to the poor.
The finder does not become the owner.
3
Apply the zakat rule
Zakat is only due on wealth that one owns completely. Since the finder does not own it, no zakat is owed.
Ownership is a key condition for zakat.
4
Refute the common error
The common misconception is that the finder becomes owner after a year. The Hanafi school rejects this.
The finder is merely a trustee.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الولوالجية : رجل التقط ألف درهم وعرفها سنة المنة اهـ . و به صرح في شرح المنظومة ويجب عليه تفريغ ذمته برده إلى أربابه إن علموا وإلا إلى الفقراء
In al-Walwalijiyya: A person picked up a thousand dirhams and made the announcement for a year [without finding the owner]. End quote. And it is stated clearly in Sharh al-Manzooma that he must clear his liability by returning it to its owners if known, otherwise to the poor.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This brief passage addresses the situation of luqatah (found property). If a person finds a sum of money (here one thousand dirhams) and announces it for a year without the owner being identified, the property does not become his own; instead, he must give it in charity to the poor. Consequently, no zakat is due on it because he is not the true owner. The ruling from Sharh al-Manzooma emphasizes that he must discharge his responsibility by returning it to the original owners if known, or else to the poor. This underscores the Hanafi principle that ownership of luqatah does not transfer to the finder; hence no zakat obligation arises.

Key Terms
لقطة (luqatah): Property found by someone who does not know its owner; the finder must attempt to locate the owner for a year.
التعريف (al-ta'reef): The process of announcing found property in public places for a year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What must a person do with found property (luqatah) after announcing it for a year without finding the owner?
A) Keep it and pay zakat on it.
B) Return it to the state.
C) Give it in charity to the poor.
D) Use it for personal expenses.

Key Takeaway
Found property (luqatah) does not become the finder's property; after a year of unsuccessful announcement, it must be given in charity, and no zakat is due.

Condition for Zakat on Mixed Property: Additional Assets and Unknown Owners
Refines the condition of freedom from debt with scenarios of additional assets and unknown owners

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition for zakat on mixed property, specifically determining when debt does not prevent zakat due to other assets or unknown creditors.

Core Idea
Zakat on mixed property is due only if surplus nisab remains after debt or if the debt has no worldly claimant.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines the well-known condition that debt prevents zakat. It clarifies that if the debtor has other assets (outside the mixed property) to cover the debt, he still must pay zakat on the surplus reaching nisab. Additionally, if the original owners of usurped property are unknown, the debt is considered inactive and does not block zakat. These distinctions are crucial for ruling on zakat in cases of hidden ownership or unjust rulers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the base condition
The act is paying zakat on property that includes usurped or mixed assets. The base condition from earlier Hanafi texts is that the property must be free from debt (qayd al-ibrā').
The passage begins by quoting al-Nahr qualifying this condition.
2
Understand the refinement: existence of other assets
If the person has other wealth (outside the mixed property) that can pay the debt, the mixed property is no longer fully occupied. He pays zakat on the surplus after deducting the debt, provided the surplus reaches nisab.
Al-Sa'diyya states this clearly: ‘he only gives zakat on what exceeds it if it reaches nisab.’
3
Distinguish known vs unknown owners
If the usurped property's original owners are known, the debt is real and prevents zakat. If they are unknown (no heirs), the debt has no worldly claimant, so zakat is due because the property has become his ownership by mixing.
The teacher (shaykh) provided this answer to resolve the original difficulty.
4
Note the exception for basic needs
Basic needs (al-hawa'ij al-asliyya) like housing and clothing cannot be used to pay the debt. If someone's only assets are such basic needs equal to the debt, he is consider.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهو قيد حسن الخ) قال في النهر : وينبغي أن يقيد بما إذا لم يكن له مال غيره يوفي منه الكل أو البعض، فإن كان زكى ما قدر على وفائه. ثم رأيته في الحواشي السعدية قال : محمل ما ذكروه ما إذا كان له مال غير ما استهلكه بالخلط يفضل عنه فلا يحيط الدين بماله وهذا طبق ما فهمته والله تعالى قلت : وقد رأيت ما يفيده في الفصل العاشر من التتارخانية حيث قال عن فتاوى الحجة : ومن ملك أموالاً غير طيبة أو غصب أموالاً وخلطها ملكها بالخلط ويصير ضامناً، وإن لم يكن له سواها نصاب فلا زكاة عليه في تلك الأموال وإن بلغت نصاباً لأنه مديون ومال المديون لا ينعقد سبباً لوجوب الزكاة عندنا ا.هـ. وذكر في الشرنبلالية مثل ما في السعدية. وبالجملة فوجوب الزكاة عليه مقيد بما إذا أبرأه الغرماء أو بما إذا كان له مال يوفي دينه وإلا فلا وبه يندفع الإشكال لكن لا بد أن يكون معه نصاب زائد على ما يوفي دينه لأن ما كان مشغولاً بالدين لا زكاة فيه وإنما يزكي ما زاد عليه إذا بلغ نصاباً كما تفيده عبارة السعدية خلافاً لما يوهمه كلام النهر. وعلى هذا فلم تجب عليه زكاة ما غصبه بل زكاة ماله الزائد عليه، ففي هذا الجواب نظر فتدبر لا يقال قد يحمل على ما إذا كان له مال آخر من غير جنس مال الزكاة كدور السكنى وثياب البدن ونحوها فإذا كان له ما يساوي ما عليه تلزمه الزكاة لأن ما عليه مما غصبه وخلطه صار ملكه وله جهة وفاء مما ذكر لأنا نقول : ما كان من الحوائج الأصلية لا يصير به غنياً، فلو كان مديوناً بمايساوي حوائجه الأصلية وقلنا بوجوب الزكاة في ذلك الدين لزم إيجاب الزكاة على الفقير الذي يحل له أخذ الزكاة، ولأن المصرح به أن الدين يصرف إلى مال الزكاة حتى لو كان عليه دين وله مال الزكاة وغيره يصرف الدين إلى مال الزكاة ولو من غير جنسه خلافاً لزفر حتى لو تزوج امرأة على خادم بغير عينه وله مائتا درهم وخادم يصرف الدين إلى المائتين دون الخادم خلافاً لزفر . صرح بذلك في البدائع. فلا يمكن الحمل المذكور تأمل. وقد يجاب عن أصل الإشكال كما أفاده شيخنا حفظه الله تعالى بأن ما غصبه وخلطه بماله إن كان أصحابه معلومين فلا كلام في وجوب ضمانه لهم وعدم وجوب الزكاة عليه بقدره قبل أداء ضمانه، وإن كانوا غير معلومين أي لا هم ولا ورثتهم فعليه زكاته لأنه صار ملکه ،بالخلط وهو وإن كانت ذمته مشغولة بقدره لكن هذا دين ليس له مطالب من جهة العباد في الدنيا فلا يمنع وجوب الزكاة. قلت لكن سيذكر المؤلف في أواخر فصل زكاة الغنم عن المبسوط أن الظلمة بمنزلة الغارمين والفقراء حتى قال محمد بن سلمة: يجوز دفع الصدقة لوالي خراسان. وذكر قاضيخان في الجامع الصغير لو أوصى بثلث ماله للفقراء فدفع إلى السلطان الجائر سقط ا هـ . فكونه فقيراً يجوز دفع الصدقة إليه ينافي وجوب الزكاة عليه. نعم سيأتي في باب المصرف السلطان
His statement: (and this is a good condition, etc.) Al-Nahr said: It should be qualified by the case where he has no other wealth to pay the debt in full or in part; if he does have, he gives zakat on what he can pay. Then I saw in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyya that he said: The interpretation of what they mentioned is when he has wealth other than what he consumed by mixing, which exceeds the debt, so the debt does not encompass all his wealth. This is in accordance with what I understood, and Allah knows best. I say: And I have seen what benefits in the tenth chapter of al-Tatarkhaniyya where it says from Fatawa al-Hujja: Whoever owns properties that are not good (ill-gotten) or usurps properties and mixes them, he owns them by mixing and becomes liable (damin). If he has no other wealth besides them that reaches nisab, no zakat is due on those properties, even if they reach nisab, because he is in debt, and the wealth of a debtor does not become a cause for zakat obligation according to us. End quote. And al-Shurnublaliyya mentioned similar to al-Sa'diyya. In summary, the obligation of zakat on him is conditioned upon the creditors releasing him or upon his having wealth to pay his debt; otherwise not. This removes the difficulty. But he must have a nisab surplus over what he pays his debt with, because wealth occupied by debt has no zakat; he only gives zakat on what exceeds it if it reaches nisab, as indicated by the expression of al-Sa'diyya, contrary to what al-Nahr's statement might suggest. According to this, he does not owe zakat on what he usurped; rather, he owes zakat on his excess wealth. In this answer there is consideration, so reflect. It cannot be said that it may be interpreted as when he has other wealth not of the same type as zakat wealth, like residential houses, body garments, etc., and if he has what equals his debt, zakat becomes obligatory because what he owes [from usurpation] after mixing becomes his ownership and he has a way to pay from what was mentioned. For we say: That which is from basic needs does not make him wealthy (ghani). If he were in debt to the extent of his basic needs and we said zakat is obligatory on that debt, it would necessitate zakat on a poor person who is allowed to take zakat. Also because it is explicitly stated that debt is assigned to zakat wealth—even if he has both zakat wealth and other wealth, the debt is assigned to the zakat wealth, even if of a different type, contrary to Zufar. For example, if he marries a woman for a unspecified servant, and he has two hundred dirhams and a servant, the debt is assigned to the two hundred, not to the servant, contrary to Zufar. This is stated in al-Bada'i. So the aforementioned interpretation is not possible; reflect. And the original difficulty may be answered as our teacher (may Allah preserve him) clarified: If the owners of what he usurped and mixed are known, there is no question about the obligation of compensation to them and the non-obligation of zakat on that amount before paying the compensation. If they are unknown (i.e., neither they nor their heirs are known), then he must pay zakat on it because it has become his ownership by mixing, and even though his liability is occupied by its value, this debt has no claimant from the people in the world, so it does not prevent zakat. I say: But the author will mention later, in the end of the chapter on zakat on sheep from al-Mabsut, that oppressors (zalamah) are in the position of debtors (gharimeen) and poor, so much so that Muhammad ibn Salama said: It is permissible to give sadaqa to the governor of Khurasan. Qadikhan in al-Jami' al-Saghir said: If one bequeaths a third of his wealth for the poor and gives it to an oppressive sultan, it is counted. Thus, the fact that he is a poor person to whom sadaqa may be given contradicts the obligation of zakat upon him. Indeed, it will come later in the chapter on the disbursement (masrif) of the sultan.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This lengthy passage is a critical refinement (hashiya-style) discussing the conditions under which a sultan (or any person) who has mixed usurped property must pay zakat. It begins with the condition 'ibrā' (release by creditors) being a 'good condition' but refines it: if the debtor has other assets to pay the debt, he should pay zakat on what he can pay. Al-Nahr and al-Sa'diyya opine that the debt must be assessed against the total wealth; if there is other wealth sufficient to cover the debt, then the mixed property is not fully occupied. The key distinction: zakat is not due on the usurped portion itself but on any surplus wealth that exceeds the debt. However, basic needs like houses and clothes cannot be used to pay the debt, so if the debt equals such basics, the person is effectively poor and no zakat is due. Finally, an important distinction: if the original owners of the usurped property are known, the debt exists in reality and prevents zakat; if the owners are unknown, the debt has no worldly claimant, so it does not prevent zakat. This is supported by the later statement that oppressors are treated like debtors or poor, which would prevent zakat. The passage concludes by noting that the author will address this later in the chapter on disbursement.

Key Terms
قيد الإبراء (qayd al-ibrā'): The condition that the creditors release the debtor from liability, so that his wealth becomes free for zakat.
الحوائج الأصلية (al-hawa'ij al-asliyya): Basic needs such as housing and clothing, which are not considered available for debt payment.
ظلمة (zalamah): Oppressive rulers, who may be treated as debtors (gharimeen) or poor for zakat purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under which condition is zakat due on usurped property that has been mixed with one's own wealth?
A) Always, if the total property reaches nisab.
B) Only if the original owners are known and have released the debtor.
C) Only if the debtor has other assets to cover the debt and retains a nisab surplus, OR if the original owners are unknown.
D) Only if the debtor has no other assets and the owners are known.

Key Takeaway
Zakat on mixed usurped property is not automatically prevented by debt; it depends on whether the debtor has other assets to pay the debt and whether the original owners are known.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Effect of Debt on Zakat Obligation
Ruling on debt preventing zakat, including usurped property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on debt and zakat, distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and Zufar, and apply the rule to cases of usurped property with known and unknown owners.

Core Idea
Debt occupies zakat wealth; zakat is only due on surplus after debt, and only if that surplus reaches nisab.

This lesson examines how debt affects the obligation of zakat. While debt does not prevent other obligations like 'ushr or kaffarah, it does prevent zakat on the amount owed. We also explore an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Zufar regarding the assignment of debt to different types of assets, and how this applies to a person who usurps property and mixes it with his own.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Debt prevents zakat on the amount of debt. The debtor is considered 'occupied' by debt, so that portion of wealth is not subject to zakat.
This is the majority Hanafi view.
2
Note the exceptions
Debt does not prevent 'ushr, kharaj, sadaqat al-fitr, or kaffarah. The restriction is specific to zakat.
These are separate obligations with different conditions.
3
Apply the condition of surplus
If the debtor has other assets beyond what is owed, zakat is due on the surplus, provided it reaches nisab.
The ruling is conditional on having no other means to pay the debt.
4
Analyze the dispute with Zufar
Zufar holds that debt should be assigned proportionally to all assets, including non-zakat types. The majority (Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn) assign debt to zakat-type wealth first.
The practical consequence is demonstrated in the example of marrying a woman for a servant while having 200 dirhams and a servant.
5
Apply to usurped property
If a person usurps property and mixes it with his own, he becomes liable. If the owners are known, no zakat is due until restitution. If owners are unknown, zakat becomes due because the debt has no worldly claimant.
This is based on texts from al-Mabsut and Qadi Khan.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يوفي دينه لأن ما كان مشغولاً بالدين لا زكاة فيه وإنما يزكي ما زاد عليه إذا بلغ نصاباً كما تفيده عبارة السعدية خلافاً لما يوهمه كلام النهر. وعلى هذا فلم تجب عليه زكاة ما غصبه بل زكاة ماله الزائد عليه، يصرف الدين إلى المائتين دون الخادم خلافاً لزفر . صرح بذلك في البدائع. فلا يمكن الحمل المذكور تأمل. وقد يجاب عن أصل الإشكال كما أفاده شيخنا حفظه الله تعالى بأن ما غصبه وخلطه بماله إن كان أصحابه معلومين In al-Mubtagha: that the owners of the wealth discharge him, because before discharge he is occupied with debt, and this is a good restriction that must be preserved. The author restricted to zakat because debt does not prevent the obligation of 'ushr and kharaj, but prevents sadaqat al-fitr, as in al-Khaniyya. As for expiation with wealth, debt does not prevent its obligation on the correct view, as in al-Kashf al-Kabir from the discussion of capacity. In al-Walwalijiyya: a man picked up a thousand dirhams and announced them for a year, then it is obligatory for him to free his liability by returning them to their owners if known, else to the poor. His statement: 'it is a good restriction' etc. In al-Nahr: it should be restricted to when he has no other wealth to pay all or part of it; if he had, he would pay zakat on what he could pay. Then I saw in al-Hawashi al-Sa'diyya that the meaning of what they mentioned is when he has wealth other than what he consumed by mixing, so that debt does not encompass all his wealth, consistent with what I understood. I said: I saw what benefits in the tenth chapter of al-Tatarikhaniyya from Fatawa al-Hujja: whoever acquires impure wealth or usurps wealth and mixes it, becomes owner by mixing and becomes liable; if he has only that as nisab, no zakat is due on that wealth even if it reaches nisab because he is a debtor, and the wealth of a debtor does not constitute a cause for zakat according to us. In al-Shirnblaliyya it is like al-Sa'diyya. In summary, the obligation of zakat on him is conditional upon the creditors discharging him or he having wealth to pay his debt; otherwise not, and with this the difficulty is resolved. Thus, no zakat is due on what he usurped, but only on his surplus wealth; so in this answer there is consideration. This is explicitly stated in al-Bada'i. So the mentioned interpretation cannot be made. The original difficulty can be answered as our teacher (may Allah preserve him) stated: what he usurped and mixed with his wealth: if its owners are known, there is no question about liability to them and no zakat on that amount before payment of his liability; if they are unknown, i.e., neither they nor their heirs, then he pays zakat on it because it becomes his property by mixing, and although his liability is occupied by that amount, this debt has no claimant from people in this world, so it does not prevent zakat obligation. I say: But the author will mention later in the chapter of zakat on grazing livestock from al-Mabsut that oppressors are like debtors and poor, so that Muhammad ibn Salama said: it is permissible to give charity to the ruler of Khurasan. Qadi Khan mentioned in al-Jami' al-Saghir: if one bequeaths a third of his wealth to the poor and pays it to the unjust ruler, it is valid. So his being poor (eligible for charity) contradicts zakat being obligatory on him. Yes, it will come in the chapter of disbursement: the ruler. Then he gave it in charity and he has a thousand dirhams, then the year passed on his thousand; he pays zakat on it by istihsan because the thousand given in charity is a debt upon him at present, because the owner may permit the charity.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage addresses the effect of debt on zakat obligation. It begins by stating that a debtor's wealth is not subject to zakat until creditors discharge him. The author notes exceptions: 'ushr, kharaj, sadaqat al-fitr, and kaffarah. It then discusses a person who picks up a lost item and must return it to its owners or give to the poor. The commentary by al-Nahr restricts the rule to when the debtor has no other assets. The discussion extends to a usurper who mixes stolen property with his own; if he has no other wealth, no zakat is due because he is a debtor. The relied-upon position is that zakat obligation is conditional upon being cleared of debt or having surplus assets. A distinction is made between known and unknown owners: if unknown, zakat becomes due because the debt has no worldly claimant. A final example shows that if a person borrows money and gives it in charity, his own wealth still attracts zakat after a year by istihsan because the debt is not yet due. Throughout, the Hanafi view is contrasted with Zufar's opinion that debt is assigned to zakat wealth regardless of type.

Key Terms
الدين (al-dayn): A financial obligation that makes a person a debtor; it prevents zakat on the amount of debt in Hanafi law.
الخلط (al-khalt): Mixing one's own lawful property with usurped or impure property, causing ownership transfer by mixing (damān).
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Discharge from debt by the creditor; a condition for zakat when no other assets exist.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a person has 500 dirhams and owes 300 dirhams, and his other assets are insufficient to pay the debt, how is zakat calculated?
A) Zakat is due on the full 500 dirhams because debt does not affect zakat on cash.
B) Zakat is due only on the surplus of 200 dirhams if that surplus reaches nisab; the 300 owed are exempt because they are occupied by debt.
C) No zakat is due at all because debt nullifies the nisab entirely.
D) Zakat is due on 500 dirhams minus the value of basic necessities.

Key Takeaway
Debt prevents zakat on the equivalent amount, but surplus wealth above debt is subject to zakat if it reaches nisab.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Freedom from Basic Needs in Zakat
Condition that zakatable wealth must be free from basic needs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of freedom from basic needs for zakat, apply it to cash, tools, and books, and distinguish between zakatable and non-zakatable items within the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
Wealth earmarked for basic needs is legally non-existent—no zakat on it.

The Hanafi school teaches that zakat is only due on surplus wealth. This lesson unpacks the condition that the nisab must be free from basic needs (al-hajat al-asliyya). We'll explore what qualifies as a basic need, the treatment of cash and trade tools, and a notable intra-madhhab difference regarding cash.

Guided Path
1
Define basic needs
Identify the definition of al-hajat al-asliyya as necessities that avert destruction, both actual (food, housing) and potential (debt).
The source gives a list: maintenance, houses, clothes, tools, books for scholars, etc.
2
Apply to cash
Cash held with the intention of spending on basic needs is treated as non-existent; zakat is not due even after a year.
This is the majority Hanafi view; note the contrary opinion in Mi'raj al-Diraya.
3
Distinguish trade tools
Trade tools consumed in the work (soap for washerman) are basic needs and exempt; those sold by weight/measure (dye for dyer) are trade goods subject to zakat.
The key is whether the item is consumed or exchanged as commodity.
4
Analyze books
Books for scholars are a basic need and exempt; for non-scholars, they are not basic needs but still require trade intention for zakat.
If not for trade, no zakat due to lack of growth intention (nama').
5
Recognize growth condition
The nisab must be growing (nama')—either actually through trade or presumed as with cash and livestock.
Growth is a separate condition; without it, even surplus wealth may not be zakatable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشرط فراغه عن الحاجة الأصلية لأن المال المشغول بها كالمعدوم، وفسرها في شرح المجمع لابن الملك بما يدفع الهلاك عن الإنسان تحقيقاً أو تقديراً، فالثاني كالدين والأول كالنفقة ودور السكني وآلات الحرب والثياب المحتاج إليها لدفع الحر أو البرد وكآلات الحرفة وأثاث المنزل ودواب الركوب وكتب العلم لأهلها، فإذا كان له دراهم مستحقة ليصرفها إلى تلك الحوائج صارت كالمعدومة كما أن الماء المستحق لصرفه إلى العطش كان كالمعدوم وجاز عنده التيمم اهـ. فقد بأن من معه دراهم وأمسكها بنية صرفها إلى حاجته الأصلية لا تجب الزكاة إذا حال الحول وهي عنده ويخالفه ما في معراج الدراية في فصل زكاة العروض أن الزكاة تجب في النقد كيفما أمسكه للنماء أو للنفقة .اهـ. وكذا في البدائع في بحث النماء التقديري : ومن آلات الحرفة الصابون والحرض للغسال لا للبقال بخلاف العصفر والزعفران للصباغ والدهن والعفص للدباغ فإنها واجبة فيه لأن المأخوذ فيه بمقابلة العين وقوارير العطارين ولجم الخيل والحمير المشتراة للتجارة ومقاودها وجلالها إن كان من غرض المشتري بيعها بها ففيها الزكاة وإلا فلا كذا في فتح القدير. وما في النهاية من أن التقييد بالأهل في الكتب ليس بمفيد لما أنه إن لم يكن من أهلها وليست هي للتجارة لا تجب فيها الزكاة وإن كثرت لعدم النماء، وإنما يفيد ذكر الأهل في حق مصرف الزكاة فإذا كانت له كتب تساوي مائتي درهم وهو محتاج إليها للتدريس وغيره يجوز صرف الزكاة إليه، وأما إذا كان لا يحتاج إليها وهي تساوي مائتي درهم لا يجوز صرف الزكاة إليه .اهـ. فغير مفيد لأن كلامهم في بيان ما هو من الحوائج الأصلية ولا شك أن الكتب لغير الأهل ليست منها وهو تقييد مفيد كما لا يخفى. وشرط أن يكون النصاب نامياً والنماء في اللغة بالمد الزيادة والقصر بالهمز خطأ.
And the condition is that he be free from basic needs, because wealth occupied by needs is like non-existent. In Sharh al-Majma' by Ibn al-Malik, it is explained as what averts destruction from a human being actually or potentially: the second like debt, the first like maintenance, houses of residence, instruments of war, clothes needed for protection from heat or cold, tools of the trade, household furniture, riding animals, and books of knowledge for their owners. So if he has dirhams designated to be spent on those needs, they become like non-existent, just as water designated for thirst is like non-existent and tayammum becomes permissible. It is evident that whoever has dirhams and holds them with the intention of spending on his basic needs, zakat is not obligatory when the year passes while they are with him. But this is contradicted by what is in Mi'raj al-Diraya in the chapter of zakat on goods: that zakat is obligatory on cash no matter how he holds it, for growth or for nafaqah. Similarly in al-Bada'i' in the discussion of presumed growth: from tools of the trade, soap and lye for the washerman, not for the grocer, unlike safflower and saffron for the dyer, and oil and gallnut for the tanner, for they are subject to zakat because the price taken is in exchange for the substance; and the bottles of perfumers, halters of horses and donkeys bought for trade, and their leads and saddle-cloths: if the purpose of the buyer is to sell them with them, then zakat is due on them, otherwise not, as in Fath al-Qadir. What is in al-Nihaya that restricting to 'owners' in the case of books is not useful because if he is not their owner and they are not for trade, no zakat is due on them even if numerous due to lack of growth; mentioning 'owners' is only beneficial regarding the disbursement of zakat: if he has books worth two hundred dirhams and needs them for teaching etc., it is permissible to give zakat to him; but if he does not need them and they are worth two hundred dirhams, it is not permissible to give zakat to him. So it is not useful, because their words are in explaining what is from basic needs, and there is no doubt that books for non-owners are not from them, and it is a useful restriction, as is clear. And the condition that the nisab be growing, and growth in language with a long vowel is increase, and with a short vowel (hamz) is an error.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi condition that zakatable wealth must be free from basic needs (al-hajat al-asliyya). It defines basic needs as necessities that avert destruction, such as food, housing, clothing, tools of trade, household furniture, means of transport, and books for scholars. Wealth earmarked for these needs is considered non-existent, so no zakat on it. A dispute is noted: some sources (Mi'raj al-Diraya) say cash is always subject to zakat regardless of intention, but the author clarifies that the dominant Hanafi view exempts cash held for basic needs. The text then details trade tools: consumables like dye for dyers are subject to zakat because they are sold by weight or measure, whereas equipment like bottles or halters are only zakatable if intended for sale. For books, a distinction is made: books for scholars are a basic need and thus exempt; books for non-scholars are not basic needs but still require the intention of trade for zakat. Finally, growth (nama') is defined linguistically as increase, not defect.

Key Terms
الحاجة الأصلية (al-hajat al-asliyya): Basic needs that avert destruction, including food, housing, clothing, tools of trade, books for scholars, etc.
النماء (al-nama'): Growth potential of wealth; a condition for zakat obligation. Can be actual (trade) or presumed (cash, livestock).
آلات الحرفة (alat al-hirfa): Tools of trade; if consumed in the work (like soap for washerman), they are basic needs and exempt; if sold by weight/measure (like dye), they are trade goods subject to zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under Hanafi law, are books of knowledge subject to zakat?
A) Yes, always, because they have value.
B) No, never, because they are not trade goods.
C) It depends: if owned by a scholar and needed for his work, they are exempt; if owned by a non-scholar and not for trade, they are also exempt but for lack of growth intention.
D) Yes, if they are worth more than the nisab regardless of the owner.

Key Takeaway
Zakat is only due on wealth surplus to basic needs; the definition of basic needs includes cash, tools, and books when tied to essential needs.

Zakat on Grazing Livestock Below Market Value and Ibn al-Sabil
Verification: a person with nisab of livestock worth less than 200 dirhams can take zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a grazing livestock owner receiving zakat when his livestock's value is below the cash nisab, and explain the rationale.

Core Idea
Zakat on grazing livestock is by headcount, not value; poverty for zakat eligibility is assessed by overall wealth, not zakatability of assets.

This lesson examines a subtle but important verification: a person who owns a nisab of grazing livestock that is worth less than 200 dirhams is still obliged to pay zakat on it (by number), but he is also eligible to receive zakat because his total wealth is insufficient for his needs. This ruling demonstrates the Hanafi principle that zakat obligations and eligibility are assessed independently. We will also touch on the related case of the wayfarer (ibn al-sabil) and the discussion about books as a basic need.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario
Read the passage carefully. The key scenario is a person who owns a nisab of grazing livestock (e.g., 40 sheep) but that livestock's market value is less than 200 dirhams (the nisab for cash). The same person also has an obligation to pay zakat on that livestock.
Note that the nisab for grazing livestock is based on headcount, not value.
2
Understand the dual ruling
The passage states that this person is both liable to pay zakat (because the headcount nisab is met) and eligible to receive zakat (because his overall wealth is below the poverty threshold). Grasp that these two obligations/eligibilities coexist.
This coexistence is specific to the Hanafi school.
3
Extend to ibn al-sabil
Similarly, a traveler (ibn al-sabil) may have property back home that is above nisab, but while traveling he may run out of money. He can receive zakat even though zakat is due on his property back home.
The key is his current need, not his total wealth.
4
Return to the books discussion
The latter part of the passage revisits an earlier discussion about whether books are considered basic needs. The author argues that the restriction to owners is useful because for them books are a basic need, while for non-owners the exemption comes from the lack of trade intention.
This illustrates how conditions interact.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تحقيق مسألة . من له نصاب سائمة لا تساوي مائتي درهم أنه يحل له أخذ الزكاة مع وجوب الزكاة عليه، وكذلك ابن السبيل له أخذ الزكاة مع وجوبها عليه ، ماله الذي ببلده قوله : (وهو تقييد مفيد كما لا يخفى) قال في النهر : هذا غير سديد إذ الكلام في شرائط وجوب الزكاة التي منها الفراغ عن الحوائج الأصلية، ومقتضى القيد وجوبها على غير الأهل لما أنها ليست من الحوائج الأصلية في حقهم وليس بالواقع لفقد شرط آخر وهو نية التجارة. فالأهل وغير الأهل في نفي الوجوب سواء ا هـ . قلت : لا يخفى عليك أن قول المؤلف أنه تقييد مفيد بناء على أنها لغير الأهل ليست من الحوائج الأصلية لا أنه تجب الزكاة فيها عليه ، فقوله وحوائجه الأصلية) لا يشمل الكتب إلا لمن هو أهلها فيفيد أنه لا زكاة فيها، وأما لمن هو غير أهلها فمسكوت عنه هنا، ثم يستفاد حكمه من قوله نام ولو تقديراً فيعلم أنه إذا لم يقصد بها التجارة لا تجب فيها الزكاة عليه أيضاً. ثم إن عبارة الهداية هكذا: وعلى هذا كتب العلم
Verification of an issue: whoever has a nisab of grazing livestock that is not worth two hundred dirhams, it is permissible for him to take zakat while zakat is obligatory upon him; likewise, the wayfarer (ibn al-sabil) may take zakat while zakat is obligatory upon him, for his wealth that is in his home country. His statement: (it is a useful restriction, as is clear). Al-Nahr said: This is not sound, because the discussion is about the conditions of zakat obligation, which include freedom from basic needs. The implication of the restriction is that zakat is obligatory on non-owners because books are not basic needs for them, but this is not the reality due to the lack of another condition, namely intention of trade. So owners and non-owners are the same in negating obligation. I say: It is not hidden from you that the author's statement that it is a useful restriction is based on the fact that for non-owners, books are not basic needs, not that zakat is obligatory on them. His statement 'and his basic needs' does not include books except for their owner, so it indicates no zakat on them; as for non-owners, it is silent here, then its ruling is derived from his statement 'growing even presumptively', so it is known that if he does not intend trade, no zakat is due on them either. Then the expression of al-Hidayah is thus: 'And on this, books of knowledge'.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage verifies a specific issue: a person who owns a nisab of grazing livestock (e.g., 40 sheep) that is worth less than 200 dirhams is still obliged to pay zakat on it (by number, not value), but he is also eligible to receive zakat because his wealth is not sufficient for his needs. Similarly, a traveler (ibn al-sabil) who owns property in his homeland may be obliged to pay zakat on it but can still receive zakat while traveling if his money runs out. The passage then returns to the earlier discussion about books. Al-Nahr criticized the author's statement that restricting books to 'owners' is a useful qualification, arguing that both owners and non-owners are exempt from zakat if not for trade. The author responds that the restriction is useful because for owners, books are a basic need, so exemption is direct; for non-owners, the exemption comes from the lack of growth intention. Thus the condition is clarifying rather than limiting.

Key Terms
السائمة (al-sa'ima): Grazing livestock that are allowed to pasture freely for most of the year; subject to zakat by number.
ابن السبيل (ibn al-sabil): A traveler who is stranded and needs financial help, even if he has wealth back home.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can a person who owns 40 sheep worth 100 dirhams (below nisab of cash) both pay zakat and receive zakat?
A) No, because paying zakat indicates wealth, so he cannot receive.
B) Yes, he pays zakat on the sheep (one sheep) and may receive zakat because his wealth is insufficient for his needs.
C) He only pays zakat if the sheep reach nisab by value, which they don't, so no zakat and he can receive.
D) He does not pay zakat because the value is below nisab, and he can receive as a poor person.

Key Takeaway
A person can be both a payer and a recipient of zakat in the Hanafi school if his livestock's market value is below the poverty line even if the headcount reaches nisab.

Basic Needs and Growth: Conditions for Zakat in Hanafi Fiqh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions of freedom from basic needs and growth for zakat, and apply them to distinguish assets subject to zakat from those exempt.

Core Idea
Wealth tied to basic needs is like non-existent; only growing assets (actually or potentially) incur zakat.

Zakat is not a flat tax on all wealth. The Hanafi school requires that the nisab (minimum amount) be free from the owner's basic needs and capable of growth. This lesson unpacks what counts as a 'basic need' and what 'growth' means, with practical examples that often surprise students. We will see why cash saved for a house purchase may not be subject to zakat, and why a scholar's books are treated differently from a trader's inventory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions
The passage states two conditions for zakat obligation: freedom from basic need (al-hājah al-aṣliyyah) and the nisab being growing (nāmī). Note how these are derived from the broader principle that zakat is on surplus wealth that can increase.
Growth can be actual (trade, breeding) or estimable (potential to increase).
2
Define 'basic need' according to Ibn al-Malik
Basic need is anything that prevents destruction or harm, either actually (food, clothing, shelter) or potentially (debt that must be repaid). Wealth reserved for such needs is exempt from zakat.
The water-for-thirst analogy clarifies: water reserved to save life is like non-existent for tayammum; similarly, dirhams set aside for basic needs are like non-existent for zakat.
3
Analyze the dispute on cash holdings
Compare the view in Ma'arij al-Diraya (zakat due on cash regardless of intention) with the majority Hanafi view that intention matters. Understand how the commentator reconciles this by distinguishing wealth reserved for immediate needs vs. savings.
The commentator sides with the majority view that intention to spend on basic needs exempts cash from zakat.
4
Evaluate the 'books for scholars' debate
The passage discusses whether books for scholars are a basic need. The majority says yes for scholars (exempt from zakat), no for non-scholars (not exempt unless for trade). Trace the commentator's reasoning using the growth condition.
Al-Nahr's objection is addressed: books for non-scholars are not basic needs, but still no zakat because they lack growth (not intended for trade).
5
Apply the conditions to tools of trade
The passage gives examples: soap for a washerman is exempt (tool of trade), but safflower for a dyer attracts zakat because it is consumed and replaced by a paid product. Understand the distinction based on whether the owner's labour or the substance is being compensated.
This illustrates the 'growth' condition: items that are stock-in-trade are growing (via trade), while tools are not.

فيعلم أنه إذا لم يقصد بها التجارة لا تجب فيها الزكاة عليه أيضاً. ثم إن عبارة الهداية هكذا: وعلى هذا كتب العلم يقال نما المال نماء ينمي وينمو نمواً وأنماه الله كذا في المغرب. وفي الشرع هو نوعان: حقيقي تبين أنها أمة ودية اللحية التي تنبت بعد حقها، والمال المتصادق على عدم وجوبه والهبة التي رجع فيها بعد الحول من جملة مال الضمار، فغير مطلقاً لأن الذي كان في يده المال في الحول كان متمكناً من الانتفاع به فلم يكن ضماراً في حقه، وكذا من لم يكن في يده إذ لا ملك له ظاهراً في الحول وإنما الحق في التعليل ما قدمناه عن الولوالجي من أنه بمنزلة الهالك بعد الوجوب، ومال الضمار هو الدين المجحود والمغصوب إذا لم يكن عليهما بينة فإن كان عليهما بينة وجبت الزكاة إلا في غصب السائمة فإنه ليس على صاحبها الزكاة، وإن كان الغاصب مقراً. كذا في الخانية. وفيها أيضاً من باب المصرف الدين المجحود إنما لا يكون نصاباً إذا حلفه
