المانع فيكون في حكم الهالك . وصححه في التحفة كذا في غاية البيان، وصححه في الخانية أيضاً وعزاه إلى السرخسي. ومنه المفقود والآبق والمأخوذ مصادرة والمال الساقط في البحر والمدفون في الصحراء المنسي مكانه، فلو صار في يده بعد ذلك فلا بد له من حول لأهلها وآلات المحترفين لما قلنا . قلنا قال في العناية : يعني أنها ليست بنامية وأورد عليه الاعتراض المار، وأنت خبير بأنه على تفسير قوله قلنا بما ذكره الاعتراض وارد لكن رده في الحواشي السعدية بأن الظاهر أنه إشارة إلى قوله لأنها مشغولة الخ ) فلا يرد قوله أن قوله لأهلها غير مقيد ههنا» لأن الكلام إذا كان في الحوائج الأصلية لا بد من التقييد فلا وجه لقصر الإشارة إلى التعليل الثاني مع كونه خلاف الظاهر ثم الاعتراض عليه فتأمل ا.هـ. وهذا ما أجاب به المؤلف ومشعر بما قلنا . قوله : (فغير صحيح مطلقاً) قال في النهر : فيه بحث فإن تعليل الفتح بقوله «لأنه كان غائباً غير مرجو القدرة على الانتفاع به ظاهر في أن كونه ضماراً يعني بالنسبة إلى المالك الأصلي، نعم هو بالنسبة إلى من كان في يده كالهالك بعد الوجوب فتدبره ا هـ . وأنت خبير بأن ما ذكره المؤلف مبني على أنه لا ملك فيه للمالك الأصلي، والمأخود في مفهوم الضمار غيبته مع قيام الملك لا مطلق الغيبة فأنى يكون ضماراً بدون الملك إلا أن يدعي ذلك؟ ثم رأيت الشيخ إسماعيل اعترض على النهر فقال فيه : إن تعليل الفتح ظاهر في كونه ضماراً لو كان ملكاً لمن غاب عنه إذ ذاك، والظاهر خلافه إذ لا
Then he gave it in charity and he had one thousand dirhams. Then the year completed on his thousand, he pays zakat on it by istihsan because the thousand given in charity is a debt upon him at present due to the possibility that the owner may permit the charity. End. And a condition is his being free from basic need because wealth occupied with it is like non-existent. It was explained in Sharh al-Majma' by Ibn al-Malik as that which repels destruction from a person either actually or potentially. The second is like debt and the first like maintenance, residential houses, weapons of war, clothes needed to repel heat or cold, tools of trade, household furniture, riding animals, and books of knowledge for their owners. So if he has dirhams reserved to spend on those needs, they become like non-existent, just as water reserved for quenching thirst becomes non-existent and tayammum is permissible for him. End. So it has become clear that if someone has dirhams and holds them with the intention of spending them on his basic need, zakat is not obligatory when the year passes while they are with him. And the opposite is what is in Ma'arij al-Diraya in the chapter of zakat on goods, that zakat is obligatory on cash however he holds it, for growth or for expenditure. End. Likewise in al-Bada'i' in the discussion of estimated growth: from the tools of trade are soap and cleaning agent for a washerman, not for a grocer, unlike safflower and saffron for a dyer and oil and gallnut for a tanner, for zakat is obligatory on them because what is taken is in exchange for the substance; and the glass bottles of perfumers, and the bridles of horses and donkeys bought for trade, and their leads and coverings, if the buyer's purpose is to sell them with them, then zakat is on them, otherwise not. So in Fath al-Qadir. And what is in al-Nihaya that the restriction to 'owners' regarding books is not useful, because if he is not an owner and they are not for trade, no zakat is due even if many, due to lack of growth. Rather the mention of 'owners' benefits regarding the disbursement of zakat: if he has books worth two hundred dirhams and he needs them for teaching etc., it is permissible to give him zakat; but if he does not need them and they are worth two hundred dirhams, zakat may not be given to him. End. So it is not useful because their speech is about explaining what is part of basic needs, and without doubt books for non-owners are not from them, and it is a useful restriction, as is not hidden. And a condition is that the nisab be growing, and growth in language (with elongation) is increase, and with shortening (with hamza) is an error. Investigation of a question: Whoever has a nisab of grazing livestock not worth two hundred dirhams, it is permissible for him to take zakat despite zakat being obligatory on him, likewise the wayfarer may take zakat despite zakat being obligatory on him, his wealth in his town. So his statement 'and his basic needs' does not include books except for their owner, so it indicates that there is no zakat on them. As for non-owners, it is silent here. We said: In al-'Inaya it is said: Meaning they are not growing. Al-Nahr said: There is investigation in it, for the explanation of al-Fath with his statement 'because it was absent, not hoped for ability to benefit from it' is apparent that it is dhimar with respect to the original owner. Yes, with respect to the one who had it in his hand, it is like destroyed after obligation. So ponder it. End. Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the conditions for zakat obligation. It begins by discussing the condition of being free from basic needs (hawa'ij asliyyah). The commentator clarifies that wealth tied up in essential needs is treated as non-existent, so no zakat is due. He defines basic needs as anything that prevents destruction or harm, including maintenance, housing, clothing, tools of trade, and books for scholars. He then addresses a conflict with Ma'arij al-Diraya which holds that zakat is due on cash regardless of intention. The commentator reconciles by distinguishing wealth reserved for immediate needs vs. savings. The discussion continues with the definition of growth (nama'), which includes both actual increase (e.g., breeding, trade) and estimable growth (ability to increase). This leads to the concept of mal al-Ḍimār (lost/unreachable property) which is excluded from zakat because the owner cannot benefit from it. Various examples are given: denied debt without proof, lost property, runaway slaves, etc. There is a detailed dispute about whether books are considered basic needs for non-scholars. The commentator upholds that they are not, so they are not exempt from zakat unless intended for trade. The passage also includes a special ruling: a person with grazing livestock worth less than 200 dirhams may accept zakat despite zakat being due on him, because the nisab is incomplete in value. Finally, the commentator discusses the definition of Ḍimār in detail, citing Fath al-Qadir and resolving a dispute about its application.

Key Terms
الحاجة الأصلية (al-hājah al-aṣliyyah): Basic need: that which prevents destruction or harm, actually or potentially.
النماء (al-namā'): Growth: actual increase (trade, breeding) or estimable (potential to increase).
التقديري (al-taqdīrī): Estimable: potential for increase even if not realized.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following items is NOT considered a basic need for the purpose of zakat exemption?
A) A scholar's books
B) A washerman's soap
C) A dyer's safflower
D) A tailor's sewing machine

Key Takeaway
Zakat is only due on wealth that is both surplus to basic needs and capable of growth; cash saved for necessities is exempt.

Zakat — Almsgiving
beginner
6 min
Mal al-Ḍimār: When Lost Property Exempts from Zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define mal al-Ḍimār and identify which types of lost or unreachable wealth are exempt from zakat, including the internal Hanafi dispute on denied debts with proof.

Core Idea
Wealth that the owner cannot benefit from (mal al-Ḍimār) is not subject to zakat, even if ownership is intact.

Imagine you still own a camel, but it has been stolen and you have no witness to prove it. Are you required to pay zakat on it each year? The Hanafi school says no, because the wealth is 'lost' (ḍimār) — you cannot use it. But what if you have a witness? Here, a famous ikhtilaf among the three imams arises. This lesson unpacks the concept of mal al-Ḍimār, its definition, examples, and the critical dispute between Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad.

Guided Path
1
Define mal al-Ḍimār
The passage defines it as 'every property whose benefit is not accessible while ownership remains' (e.g., denied debt, stolen goods, lost property). Note the root meaning of 'ḍimār': hidden or lost.
The key is inability to benefit, not loss of ownership.
2
Identify why it is exempt from zakat
Zakat requires the owner to be able to benefit from the wealth; ḍimār lacks this. The passage compares it to 'destroyed after obligation' — it is as if the wealth perished after the obligation started, but since it was never usable, no obligation arose.
The analogy clarifies why no zakat is due even if the wealth later returns.
3
Analyze the internal Hanafi dispute on denied debt with proof
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say: if the creditor has a reliable witness, the debt is not ḍimār because the witness can enforce payment. Imam Muhammad says: even with a witness, the debt is ḍimār because the judge may not accept the witness or may be unjust. The passage notes that Imam Muhammad's view is the mufta bihi (relied-upon).
This is a subtle point about the probability of recovering the debt.
4
List examples of mal al-Ḍimār from the passage
Examples include: denied debt without proof, stolen goods without witnesses, lost property, money sunk in the sea, hidden treasure whose location is forgotten, runaway slaves, goods seized by oppressors. Also, a mare whose ownership is disputed after the husband claims it was an enslaved woman (mahr case).
Each example shares the feature: owner cannot practically access the wealth.
5
Apply the rule to the special case of grazing livestock
A person with a nisab of grazing livestock worth less than 200 dirhams may accept zakat even though zakat is obligatory on him. This is because his livestock is not 'growing' in value, and he is considered needy. The passage uses this to show that ḍimār and poverty are distinct.
This demonstrates the interaction of conditions: if the nisab lacks value, it may be treated as ḍimār or non-growing.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq on the conditions for zakat obligation. It begins by discussing the condition of being free from basic needs (hawa'ij asliyyah). The commentator clarifies that wealth tied up in essential needs is treated as non-existent, so no zakat is due. He defines basic needs as anything that prevents destruction or harm, including maintenance, housing, clothing, tools of trade, and books for scholars. He then addresses a conflict with Ma'arij al-Diraya which holds that zakat is due on cash regardless of intention. The commentator reconciles by distinguishing wealth reserved for immediate needs vs. savings. The discussion continues with the definition of growth (nama'), which includes both actual increase (e.g., breeding, trade) and estimable growth (ability to increase). This leads to the concept of mal al-Ḍimār (lost/unreachable property) which is excluded from zakat because the owner cannot benefit from it. Various examples are given: denied debt without proof, lost property, runaway slaves, etc. There is a detailed dispute about whether books are considered basic needs for non-scholars. The commentator upholds that they are not, so they are not exempt from zakat unless intended for trade. The passage also includes a special ruling: a person with grazing livestock worth less than 200 dirhams may accept zakat despite zakat being due on him, because the nisab is incomplete in value. Finally, the commentator discusses the definition of Ḍimār in detail, citing Fath al-Qadir and resolving a dispute about its application.

Key Terms
مال الضمار (māl al-ḍimār): Lost/unreachable property: wealth whose benefit is not accessible despite ownership.
الدين المجحود (al-dayn al-majḥūd): Denied debt: a debt the debtor refuses to acknowledge.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Clear evidence, usually two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered mal al-Ḍimār according to Imam Muhammad?
A) A debt admitted by the debtor who is wealthy
B) A denied debt supported by a reliable witness
C) Usurped grazing livestock where the usurper denies
D) Money that a friend borrowed and regularly pays back

Key Takeaway
Mal al-Ḍimār is exempt from zakat: wealth the owner cannot practically use, such as denied debt without proof or stolen goods without witnesses, and the relied-upon view extends this even to debts with witnesses.

Zakat — Almsgiving

Mal al-Dimar: Inaccessible Wealth and Zakat
Definition of mal al-dimar and its effect on zakat with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of mal al-dimar and determine its effect on zakat obligation, including the intra-Hanafi dispute over denied debts with evidence.

Core Idea
Mal al-dimar is inaccessible wealth; zakat is not due on it because the condition of growth is absent.

This lesson examines the concept of mal al-dimar (inaccessible wealth) from the Hanafi legal tradition. Understanding this concept is crucial for correctly applying the zakat obligation, as it distinguishes between wealth subject to zakat and wealth exempt due to inability to benefit. The source passage from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Bahr al-Raiq provides a detailed definition and discusses various examples and intra-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Define the two types of growth (nama')
Identify the distinction between real growth (by offspring/trade) and estimative growth (potential to increase through control). This frames the condition for zakat.
Growth is a key condition for zakat in the Hanafi school.
2
State the definition of mal al-dimar
Extract the definition: wealth from which benefit is not possible despite ownership. Recognize that lack of benefit (ability) is the core element.
The owner cannot benefit due to physical inaccessibility or legal barriers.
3
Identify the examples and borderline cases
Go through the examples given: denied debt without evidence, usurped property without evidence, missing person's property, etc. Note the exceptions and refinements from the text.
Some cases (e.g., debt with evidence) are disputed among the Hanafi imams.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on denied debts with evidence
Compare Abu Hanifa's position (zakat due if there is evidence) with Muhammad's (no zakat even with evidence) and Abu Yusuf's partial agreement. Understand the reasoning: Muhammad argues evidence may fail in court.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.
5
Apply the rule: when is zakat due after recovery?
If mal al-dimar is later recovered, a new hawl (year) must begin from the date of recovery, because the condition of growth was absent during the period of inaccessibility.
This follows from the loss of estimative growth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة. وفي الشرع هو نوعان: حقيقي وتقديري فالحقيقي الزيادة بالتوالد والتناسل والتجارات والتقديري تمكنه من الزيادة بكون المال في يده أو يد نائبه فلا زكاة على من لم يتمكن منها في ماله كمال الضمار، وهو كل مال غير مقدور الانتفاع به مع قيام أصل الملك. كذا في البدائع. فما في فتح القدير من أن مهر المرأة التي تبين أنها أمة ودية اللحية التي تنبت بعد حقها، والمال المتصادق على عدم وجوبه والهبة التي رجع فيها بعد الحول من جملة مال الضمار، فغير صحيح مطلقاً لأن الذي كان في يده المال في الحول كان متمكناً من الانتفاع به فلم يكن ضماراً في حقه، وكذا من لم يكن في يده إذ لا ملك له ظاهراً في الحول وإنما الحق في التعليل ما قدمناه عن الولوالجي من أنه بمنزلة الهالك بعد الوجوب، ومال الضمار هو الدين المجحود والمغصوب إذا لم يكن عليهما بينة فإن كان عليهما بينة وجبت الزكاة إلا في غصب السائمة فإنه ليس على صاحبها الزكاة، وإن كان الغاصب مقراً. كذا في الخانية. وفيها أيضاً من باب المصرف الدين المجحود إنما لا يكون نصاباً إذا حلفه القاضي وحلف، أما قبل ذلك يكون نصاباً حتى لو قبض منه أربعين درهماً يلزمه أداء الزكاة .اهـ. وعن محمد: لا تجب الزكاة وإن كان له بينة لأن البينة قد لا تقبل والقاضي قد لا يعدل وقد لا يظهر بالخصومة بين يديه المانع فيكون في حكم الهالك . وصححه في التحفة كذا في غاية البيان، وصححه في الخانية أيضاً وعزاه إلى السرخسي. ومنه المفقود والآبق والمأخوذ مصادرة والمال الساقط في البحر والمدفون في الصحراء المنسي مكانه، فلو صار في يده بعد ذلك فلا بد له من حول جديد لعدم الشرط وهو النمو، وأما المدفون في حرز ولو دار غيره إذا نسيه فليس منه فيكون نصاباً. واختلف المشايخ في المدفون في أرض مملوكة أو كرم؛ فقيل بالوجوب لإمكان الوصول، وقيل لا لأنها غير حرز وأما إذا أودعه ونسي المودع قالوا: إن كان المودع من الأجانب فهو ضمار، وإن كان من معارفة وجبت الزكاة لتفريطه بالنسيان في غير محله . وقيدنا الدين بالمجحود لأنه لو كان على مقر ملي أو معسر تجب الزكاة لإمكان الوصول إليه ابتداء أو بواسطة التحصيل، ولو كان على مقر مفلس فهو نصاب عند أبي حنيفة لأن تفليس القاضي لا يصح عنده، وعند محمد لا يجب لتحقق الإفلاس عنده بالتفليس، وأبو يوسف مع محمد في تحقق الإفلاس ومع أبي حنيفة في حكم الزكاة رعاية لجانب الفقراء. كذا في الهداية. فأفاد أنه إذا قبض الدين زكاه لما مضى.
The Book of Zakat. In Islamic law, it (growth) is of two types: real and estimative. Real is increase by offspring, reproduction, and trade. Estimative is the potential for increase by the wealth being in his hand or the hand of his agent. So no zakat is due on one who does not have ability (to benefit) from his wealth, such as mal al-dimar (inaccessible wealth). It is every wealth from which benefit is not possible despite the existence of original ownership. Thus, what is in Fath al-Qadir that the dower of a woman who turns out to be a slave, the blood-money of a beard that grows after it (the right), wealth mutually agreed not to be obligatory, and a gift that was taken back after the year are from mal al-dimar – that is not absolutely correct, because the one who had the wealth in his hand during the year had the ability to benefit from it, so it was not dimar for him. Similarly, the one who did not have it in his hand, because for him there is no apparent ownership during the year. The correct reasoning is what we have mentioned from al-Walwaji that it is like something that perished after obligation. Mal al-dimar is a debt that is denied and (property) that is usurped when there is no evidence for either. If there is evidence, zakat is obligatory, except in the case of usurped grazing livestock (sa'imah), for which its owner is not liable for zakat even if the usurper admits (the usurpation). Thus in al-Khaniyyah. In it also from the section on disbursement: a debt that is denied is not considered a nisab only if the judge administers an oath to him and he swore. Before that, it is considered a nisab, such that if he takes forty dirhams from it, he is obligated to pay zakat. End. From Muhammad: Zakat is not obligatory even if he has evidence, because the evidence may not be accepted, the judge may not be just, and a bar may not appear through litigation before him, so it is considered as perished. This was deemed correct in al-Tuhfah, similarly in Ghayat al-Bayan, and also in al-Khaniyyah, attributed to al-Sarakhsi. Included (in dimar) are: the missing person, the runaway slave, what is taken as a fine, wealth fallen into the sea, and wealth buried in a desert whose location is forgotten. If it comes into his hand thereafter, he must start a new year because the condition (of growth) is absent. As for wealth buried in a secure place, even if in another's house, if he forgets it, it is not dimar, so it is a nisab. The scholars differed regarding wealth buried in owned land or a vineyard: some said it is obligatory (zakat) due to possibility of access, some said no because it is not a secure place. If he deposited it and the depositee forgot, they said: if the depositee is a stranger, it is dimar; if he is an acquaintance, zakat is obligatory due to negligence in forgetting in an improper place. We restricted (the ruling) to a debt that is denied, because if it is upon an acknowledging solvent or insolvent person, zakat is obligatory due to the possibility of access initially or through collection. If it is upon an acknowledging bankrupt person, it is a nisab according to Abu Hanifa because bankruptcy declared by a judge is not valid in his view. According to Muhammad, it is not obligatory because bankruptcy is established by the declaration. Abu Yusuf agrees with Muhammad on the establishment of bankruptcy and with Abu Hanifa on the ruling of zakat, out of consideration for the poor. Thus in al-Hidayah. So it is inferred that if he collects the debt, he gives zakat for the past (years).
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute on the concept of Mal al-Dimar (inaccessible wealth) and its impact on zakat obligation. The author, Ibn Nujaym (d. 970 AH), explains that growth (nama') is of two types: real (increase by offspring or trade) and estimative (potential for increase through control). Zakat is only obligatory when the owner has actual or estimative ability to benefit. Mal al-Dimar is defined as wealth from which benefit is not possible despite ownership. Examples include a dower of a woman who turns out to be a slave, blood-money for a beard that grows later—but these are critiqued because the owner had ability during the year. The correct definition per al-Walwaji is that it is like wealth that perished after obligation became due. The core examples are: a denied debt or usurped property without evidence. If there is evidence, zakat is due, except for usurped grazing livestock (sa'imah) which has no zakat even if the usurper admits. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Muhammad holds that even with evidence, zakat is not due because the evidence may not be accepted by the judge. This view is deemed correct in several works. Other types include: missing property, runaway slave, confiscated goods, goods lost at sea, buried wealth whose location is forgotten—all are dimar. If such wealth is later recovered, a new year must begin because the condition of growth was interrupted. Wealth buried in a secure place (even in another's home) that is forgotten is not dimar, so it remains nisab. There is a difference among scholars on land vs vineyard. For forgotten deposits: if depositee is a stranger, it's dimar; if acquaintance, zakat is due because he was negligent. Debt is qualified as 'denied' because if the debtor acknowledges and is able or insolvent, zakat is due due to possibility of access. If debtor acknowledges but is declared bankrupt by judge, Abu Hanifa says zakat is due because judicial bankruptcy is invalid; Muhammad says no because bankruptcy is established; Abu Yusuf takes middle ground. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is not explicitly given here, but the text from al-Hidayah indicates that upon collection, one gives zakat for past years.

Key Terms
مال الضمار (mal al-dimar): Wealth from which benefit is not possible even though ownership remains, such as a denied debt without evidence.
النماء (al-nama'): Growth, which is the condition for zakat. Real growth is increase by offspring or trade; estimative growth is potential increase through control.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is considered Mal al-Dimar?
A) A loan given to a person who acknowledges the debt and is wealthy.
B) A stolen camel without any evidence against the thief.
C) A gift that was taken back after the year ends.
D) Wealth buried in one's own house but forgotten.

Key Takeaway
Mal al-dimar is inaccessible wealth exempt from zakat; but the ruling differs when evidence exists, with Imam Muhammad's view (no zakat) being the relied-upon position.

Abu Hanifa's Three-Fold Classification of Debts for Zakat
Presenting Abu Hanifa's three-fold classification of debts and zakat rulings per type

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify debts into strong, medium, and weak according to Abu Hanifa and determine the zakat ruling for each type.

Core Idea
Zakat on debt depends on what the debt replaced: trade goods, personal property, or non-property.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym lays out Abu Hanifa's seminal classification of debts for zakat. Understanding this tripartite division is essential for correctly applying zakat to receivables, a common issue in personal finance and business.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three types of debts: strong (qawi), medium (mutawassit), and weak (da'if), and note what each is a substitute for (e.g., loan/trade goods, non-trade items, non-property).
Strong: loan or trade capital. Medium: price of personal items. Weak: mahr, diyah, etc.
2
Determine the trigger for zakat obligation
For each type, state when zakat becomes obligatory: strong – after one year (but deferrable until collection); medium – upon collecting a nisab; weak – upon collecting a nisab and completing a new year.
3
Distinguish the treatment of past years
Identify which categories count past years (medium) and which require a new year (weak). For strong, zakat is due for the year that passed even if not yet collected.
Strong: past year counted. Medium: past years counted (correct narration). Weak: new year after collection.
4
Classify given examples
Apply the classification to examples such as price of a house, loan given, mahr, price of a car, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال في فتح القدير : وهو غير جار على إطلاقه بل ذلك في بعض أنواع الدين ولنوضح ذلك فنقول : قسم أبو حنيفة الدين على ثلاثة أقسام : قوي وهو بدل القرض ومال التجارة، و متوسط وهو بدل ما ليس للتجارة كثمن ثياب البذلة وعبد الخدمة ودار السكني، وضعيف وهو بدل ما ليس بمال كالمهر والوصية وبدل الخلع والصلح عن دم الا العمد والدية وبدل الكتابة والسعاية. ففي القوي تجب الزكاة إذا حال الحول ويتراخى القضاء إلى أن يقبض أربعين درهماً ففيها درهم، وكذا فيما زاد بحسابه . وفي المتوسط لا تجب ما لم يقبض : نصاباً ويعتبر لما مضى من الحول في صحيح الرواية. وفي الضعيف لا تجب ما لم يقبض نصاباً ويحول الحول بعد القبض عليه وثمن السائمة كثمن عبد الخدمة. ولو ورث ديناً على رجل فهو كالدين الوسط،
He said in Fath al-Qadir: It (the ruling) is not absolute; rather that applies only to some types of debt. Let us clarify: Abu Hanifa divided debt into three types: Strong debt, which is the substitute for a loan and trade capital; Medium debt, which is the substitute for what is not for trade, such as the price of clothing for personal use, a slave for service, and a house for dwelling; Weak debt, which is the substitute for what is not property, such as dower, bequest, consideration for khul' and settlement for blood money other than intentional killing, diyah, and the substitute for kitabah and sa'ayah. For strong debt, zakat becomes obligatory when the year elapses, but its payment is deferred until he collects forty dirhams, then one dirham is due, and similarly for more proportionately. For medium debt, it (zakat) is not obligatory until he collects a nisab, and consideration is given to the past year in the correct narration. For weak debt, it is not obligatory until he collects a nisab, and a year begins after collection. The price of grazing livestock is like the price of a slave for service. If one inherits a debt owed by a man, it is like medium debt.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from Ibn Nujaym explaining Abu Hanifa's classification of debts for zakat purposes, quoted from Fath al-Qadir. The author clarifies that the previous general rule about collecting debt and giving zakat for past years is not absolute but depends on the type of debt. Abu Hanifa divides debt into three categories: (1) Strong debt (qawi): this is the price/compensation for a loan or trade goods. Zakat is obligatory when the year has passed, but payment is deferred until one collects 40 dirhams (a nisab), and then one gives 1 dirham (2.5%) for that amount, and for more proportionately. (2) Medium debt (mutawassit): this is the price for items not meant for trade, like used clothes, a service slave, or a residential house. Here, zakat is not obligatory until one collects a full nisab, and then one gives zakat for all past years (i.e., the year starts from the time the debt was incurred). The text says 'in the correct narration' (fi sahh al-riwayah), but there is also another narration that may differ. (3) Weak debt (da'if): this is compensation for something that is not property per se, such as mahr, bequest, consideration in khul', blood money for unintentional killing, kitabah, etc. For weak debt, zakat is not obligatory until one collects a nisab, and then a new year begins from the time of collection. The author also notes that the price (thaman) of grazing livestock (sa'imah) is treated like the price of a service slave, i.e., medium debt. If one inherits a debt, it is also considered medium debt. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the ruling is clear. Note: The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position for medium debt is that zakat is not due until nisab is collected, and then zakat for past years is given, but there is a weaker narration that requires a new year. The text mentions 'al-sahh' is as stated.

Key Terms
الدين القوي (al-dayn al-qawi): Debt that is a substitute for a loan or trade capital; zakat is obligatory after one year but payment deferred until collection.
الدين المتوسط (al-dayn al-mutawassit): Debt that is a substitute for non-trade items (e.g., used clothes, service slave, house); zakat due only after collecting nisab, then past years are counted.
الدين الضعيف (al-dayn al-da'if): Debt that is a substitute for something not considered property (e.g., mahr, diyah); zakat due only after collecting nisab and completing a new year.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa's classification, which type of debt requires a new year (hawl) after collection before zakat becomes due?
A) Strong debt (qawi) such as a loan.
B) Medium debt (mutawassit) such as the price of a service slave.
C) Weak debt (da'if) such as mahr (dower).
D) All debts require a new year after collection.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's three-fold classification of debts—strong, medium, and weak—determines whether zakat is due immediately after the year, after collection with past years counted, or after collection plus a new year.

Abu Hanifa's Three Debt Categories for Zakat
Classifying debts into strong, medium, weak with zakat rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's three debt categories for zakat, identify examples, and explain the payment conditions for each.

Core Idea
Zakat on debts depends on the source: strong, medium, or weak, each with different timing for payment.

Abu Hanifa uniquely classified debts into three categories based on the nature of the underlying property. This lesson unpacks those categories and their respective zakat rulings, forming the foundation for understanding Hanafi zakat on receivables.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rationale
Debts are substitutes for property. The zakat treatment varies because the original property may or may not be zakatable if kept.
Strong debt replaces zakatable property; medium replaces non-zakatable property; weak replaces non-property.
2
Define each category
Strong: loan repayment and price of trade goods. Medium: price of personal items like clothes, service slave, dwelling. Weak: dowry, bequest, blood-money, kitabah, s'aayah.
Examples are given in the text.
3
State the obligation and payment condition
Strong: zakat obligates yearly, payment deferred until receipt of 40 dirhams, then 1 dirham per 40. Medium: not obligatory until receipt of nisab, then past years considered (sound narration). Weak: not obligatory until receipt of nisab and a full year after receipt.
For medium, there is a second narration where zakat starts only from receipt.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi difference
Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held all debts equal: zakat obligatory before receipt, payable on any receipt except kitabah and s'aayah.
This will be detailed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الزكاة. وأما المدفون في حرز ولو دار غيره إذا نسيه فليس منه فيكون نصاباً. واختلف المشايخ في المدفون في أرض مملوكة أو كرم؛ فقيل بالوجوب لإمكان الوصول، وقيل لا لأنها غير حرز. وأما إذا أودعه ونسي المودع قالوا: إن كان المودع من الأجانب فهو ضمار، وإن كان من معارفة وجبت الزكاة لتفريطه بالنسيان في غير محله. وقيدنا الدين بالمجحود لأنه لو كان على مقر ملي أو معسر تجب الزكاة لإمكان الوصول إليه ابتداء وفي الضعيف لا تجب ما لم يقبض نصاباً ويحول الحول بعد القبض عليه. وثمن السائمة كثمن عبد الخدمة. ولو ورث ديناً على رجل فهو كالدين الوسط. وروي أنه كالضعيف. وعندهما الديون كلها سواء تجب الزكاة قبل القبض، العاقلة الدية لأن وجوبها بطريق الصلة إلا أن يقول الأصل إن المسببات تختلف بحسب اختلاف الأسباب. ولو آجر عبده أو داره بنصاب إن لم يكونا للتجارة لا تجب ما لم يحل الحول بعد القبض في قوله وإن كان للتجارة وجهين: إما أن يكون بدلاً عن مال وبقي ذلك المال في يده لا تجب فيه الزكاة كبدل عبيد الخدمة وثياب البدن ففي أصح الروايتين عن أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لا تجب فيه الزكاة لما مضى، وفي الرواية الأخرى تجب الزكاة إذا قبض المائتين. وإما أن يكون بدلاً عن مال لو بقي ذلك المال في يده تجب الزكاة فيه كبدل عروض التجارة فلا خلاف بين أصحابنا في وجوب الزكاة فيه، واختلافهم في نصاب الأداء فقال أبو حنيفة رحمه الله: يقدر ذلك بأربعين. وعندهما تجب في قليل المقبوض وكثيره إلا الدية على العاقلة وبدل الكتابة فإنهما اشترطا فيهما حولان الحول بعد قبض المالين لأن كل الديون صحيحة سوى هذين، ثم الديون الصحيحة التي تجب فيها الزكاة اختلفوا فيها فقال أصحابنا: لا يجب إخراج الزكاة عنها قبل القبض وقال الشافعي في الجديد: إذا كان الدين حالاً على مليء معترف به في الظاهر والباطن وجب إخراج زكاته وإن لم يقبضه. لنا: أنه لو وجب التعجيل للزم إخراج الكامل عن الناقص وذلك لا يجوز كإخراج البيض عن السود، وهذا لأن الدين أنقص من العين بدليل أن أداء الدين عن العين لا يجوز. اهـ. قوله: (وكلما قبض شيئاً زكاه أي وكلما قبض شيئاً يلزمه أداء زكاة ذلك القدر قل المقبوض أو كثر ا هـ ما رأيته قوله: (وإن كان للتجارة كان حكمه كالقوي الخ أقول: هذا مخالف لما في المحيط حيث قال: وفي أجرة مال التجارة أو عبد التجارة روايتان في رواية لا زكاة فيها حتى يقبض ويحول عليها الحول لأن المنفعة ليست بمال حقيقة فصار كالمهر وفي ظاهر الرواية تجب الزكاة فيها ويجب الأداء إذا قبض منها مائتي درهم لأنها بدل عن مال ليس بمحل لوجوب الزكاة فيه لأن المنافع مال حقيقة لكنها ليست بمحل لوجوب الزكاة لأنها لا تصلح لأنها لا تبقى سنة ا.هـ. قلت: وهذا صريح في أنه على الرواية الأولى من الدين الضعيف، وعلى ظاهر الرواية من المتوسط لا من القوي لأن المنافع ليست
Book of Zakat. As for the buried (wealth) in a safe place, even if it is someone else's house, if he forgets it, then it is not from it (the category of forgotten wealth), so it is a nisab. And the scholars differed regarding wealth buried in owned land or a vineyard: some said it is obligatory (zakat) because it is possible to access it, and some said no because it is not a safe place. As for if he deposited it and the depositor forgot it, they said: if the depositor is a stranger, it is a lost property (dhimar), and if he is an acquaintance, zakat is obligatory due to his negligence in forgetting in an inappropriate place. And we restricted debt to the disputed one because if it is upon an acknowledging solvent person or an insolvent one, zakat is obligatory due to possibility of accessing it initially or through collection. And if it is upon an acknowledging bankrupt person, then it is a nisab according to Abu Hanifa because the judge's declaration of bankruptcy is not valid according to him. According to Muhammad, it is not obligatory due to the realization of bankruptcy in his view by the declaration. Abu Yusuf agrees with Muhammad on the realization of bankruptcy and with Abu Hanifa on the ruling of zakat, out of consideration for the poor. This is in al-Hidayah. So it indicates that if he receives the debt, he pays zakat for the past. He said in Fath al-Qadir: This is not absolute, but that applies to some types of debt. Let us clarify: Abu Hanifa divided debt into three categories: Strong: the substitute for a loan and trade goods; Medium: the substitute for something not for trade, like the price of ordinary clothes, a service slave, and a dwelling house; Weak: the substitute for something that is not property, like the dowry, bequest, substitute for khul', compromise for deliberate bloodshed, blood-money, substitute for kitabah, and earnings. For the strong, zakat is obligatory when a year passes, and payment is deferred until he receives forty dirhams, then one dirham is due, and similarly for more proportionally. For the medium, it is not obligatory until he receives a nisab, and the past year is considered in the sound narration. For the weak, it is not obligatory until he receives a nisab and a year passes after receipt. And the price of grazing livestock is like the price of a service slave. And if he inherits a debt from a man, it is like the medium debt. And it is narrated that it is like the weak. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), all debts are equal: zakat is obligatory before receipt, and whenever he receives anything, he pays zakat for it, whether little or much, except the debt of kitabah and earnings. And in one narration, they also exclude blood-money before judgment and compensation for injury because they are not truly debts, hence guarantee for kitabah is invalid, and the blood-money is not taken from the estate of one of the 'aqila who died because its obligation is as a gift, unless the principle says that causes differ according to different reasons. And if he rents his slave or his house for a nisab, if they are not for trade, it is not obligatory until a year passes after receipt in his view, and if they are for trade, its ruling is like the strong because the rent of trade property is like the price of trade property in the sound narration. End. In al-Walwaljiyya: And if one of two partners frees a jointly owned slave and they choose the master to guarantee the manumitter, if the slave was for trade, its ruling is the ruling of the medium debt—this is correct. And if the slave was for service, likewise. And if they choose the slave's earnings, its ruling is the ruling of the weak debt. End. And the implication of the first is that if the slave was for trade, the ruling of this debt is the ruling of the strong debt, and the definition of the mentioned debts is what agrees with what is in al-Bada'i. Consider. The poor Mahmud Ahmad ibn Abd al-Ghani, writer of these margins, says: I saw here on the margin of al-Bahr in the handwriting of some virtuous person the following: In Ghayat al-Bayan: Then a debt, if it is a substitute for property, is of two types: either it is a substitute for property that, if that property remained in his hand, zakat would not be obligatory on it, like the substitute for service slaves and body clothes; then in the most sound of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, zakat is not obligatory for the past, and in the other narration, zakat is obligatory when he receives two hundred. Or it is a substitute for property that, if that property remained in his hand, zakat would be obligatory on it, like the substitute for trade goods; then there is no difference among our scholars regarding the obligation of zakat on it, and their difference is regarding the nisab for payment: Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said it is estimated by forty. According to them, it is obligatory on the little and much of what is received, except the blood-money on the 'aqila and the substitute for kitabah, for which they stipulated the passing of a year after receiving the money because all debts are valid except these two. Then the valid debts on which zakat is obligatory, they differed about them: our scholars said it is not obligatory to pay zakat on them before receipt, and al-Shafi'i said in his new view: if the debt is due on a solvent person who acknowledges it outwardly and inwardly, it is obligatory to pay its zakat even if he has not received it. Our evidence: if immediate payment were obligatory, then paying the complete from the incomplete would be necessary, and that is not permissible, like exchanging white for black. And this is because a debt is less than cash, as evidenced by the fact that paying a debt with cash is not permissible. End. His statement: 'And whenever he receives anything, he pays zakat for it' means that whenever he receives anything, he is obligated to pay the zakat of that amount, whether the received amount is little or much. End. I saw it. His statement: 'And if it is for trade, its ruling is like the strong' etc. I say: This contradicts what is in al-Muhit where he said: Concerning the rent of trade property or a trade slave, there are two narrations. In one narration, there is no zakat on it until he receives it and a year passes over it because benefit is not truly property, so it becomes like a dowry. In the apparent narration, zakat is obligatory on it and payment is obligatory when he receives from it two hundred dirhams because it is a substitute for property that is not a subject for zakat obligation because benefits are truly property but are not a subject for zakat obligation because they are not suitable for lasting a year. End. I said: This is explicit that in the first narration it is from the weak debt, and in the apparent narration it is from the medium, not from the strong, because benefits are not
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the classification of debts for zakat purposes. The author first discusses forgotten buried wealth, then moves to the main topic: zakat on debts. The core is Abu Hanifa's threefold classification of debts: strong (qawi), medium (mutawassit), and weak (da'if). Strong debts are substitutes for property that would have been zakatable if kept (e.g., loan repayment, trade goods price). For these, zakat is obligatory annually but payment is deferred until the owner receives at least forty dirhams, then one dirham per forty is due. Medium debts are substitutes for non-trade property (e.g., price of personal clothing, service slave, dwelling). According to the sound narration, zakat is not obligatory until a nisab is received, but the past years are considered (i.e., zakat for previous years becomes due upon receipt). Weak debts are substitutes for non-property (e.g., dowry, bequest, blood-money). Zakat is not obligatory until a nisab is received and a full year passes after receipt. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held that all debts are equal: zakat is obligatory before receipt, and whenever any amount is received, zakat for that amount is due, except for kitabah and earnings. A narration also excludes blood-money and compensation before judgment. Abu Hanifa's view distinguishes three categories; the Sahibayn's view simplifies. The passage also includes a Hanafi response to al-Shafi'i, who held that zakat on a debt owed by a solvent acknowledging person is immediately obligatory. The Hanafi argument is that paying zakat on a debt before receipt would be paying complete zakat on an incomplete asset (since debts are less than cash), which is impermissible by analogy with exchanging black for white. The head of the 'illa is that the debt is deficient; one cannot give full zakat on it before actual possession. The passage also discusses related issues like rent for trade property, which follows the strong or medium category depending on the narration. The commentary from Ghayat al-Bayan simplifies to two types: debts on property that would not be zakatable if kept (like service slaves) and those that would be (like trade goods). The author also notes a marginal comment from al-Muhit showing two narrations for rent of trade property, indicating a different classification. Overall, this passage is a detailed exposition of Hanafi intra-school differences and a defense against the Shafi'i position.

Key Terms
دين قوي (dayn qawiyy): Debt that is a substitute for property that would be subject to zakat if kept, such as repayment of a loan or the price of trade goods.
دين متوسط (dayn mutawassit): Debt that is a substitute for property that is not subject to zakat if kept, such as the price of personal effects like clothing or a service slave.
دين ضعيف (dayn da'if): Debt that is a substitute for something that is not considered property (mal) in sharia, such as dowry, bequest, or blood-money.
ضمار (dhimar): A lost or forgotten property that is not considered in one's possession; no zakat due.
عروض التجارة (urud al-tijara): Goods acquired for trade; their price is a strong debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, when is zakat payable on a strong debt (dayn qawiyy)?
A) Immediately when the year passes, even without receipt.
B) Only after receiving the entire debt amount.
C) Payment is deferred until receiving at least 40 dirhams, then one dirham per 40.
D) Only after receiving a nisab and one full year after receipt.

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa divides debts for zakat into strong (defer payment until 40 dirhams received), medium (cash nisab needed, past years count), and weak (cash nisab plus a new year).

Zakat — Almsgiving
intermediate
6 min
Hanafi vs. Shafi'i: Is Zakat Due on Debt Before Receipt?
Hanafi response to Shafi'i on zakat debt timing using qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qiyas argument (completeness vs deficiency) to explain why zakat on debt is not due before receipt, and contrast it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Immediate zakat on debt before receipt would be paying complete zakat on an incomplete asset, which is impermissible.

The school of al-Shafi'i holds that if a debt is owed by a solvent and acknowledging debtor, zakat is due immediately at the end of the lunar year, even if the money has not been received. The Hanafis respond with a powerful qiyas (analogical reasoning) that highlights the deficiency of debt compared to cash. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
The dispute is whether zakat on a debt becomes obligatory before the creditor physically receives the money from a solvent debtor.
Shafi'i jadeed view says yes; Hanafi says no.
2
State the Hanafi position
Zakat is not obligatory on a debt before receipt; payment is deferred until actual receipt, with conditions depending on the debt type (strong/medium/weak).
This applies even if the debtor is reliable.
3
Examine the Hanafi qiyas argument
The Hanafis argue: if immediate payment were obligatory, it would mean paying complete zakat on an incomplete asset (since debt is less than cash). This is impermissible, analogous to exchanging black for white. The 'illa (effective cause) is the deficiency of debt compared to cash, proven by the rule that paying a debt with cash is invalid.
The analogical reasoning is: paying complete zakat (kamil) on a deficient asset (naqis) is like exchanging a healthy for an unhealthy egg.
4
Evaluate the practical difference
Hanafi: the creditor waits until receipt of nisab (or 40 dirhams for strong debt) before paying; no penalty for delay. Shafi'i: the creditor must pay zakat immediately at year-end, even if the debtor hasn't paid, and must then recover the zakat from the debtor? Actually, Shafi'i rule is that the creditor pays from his own wealth, then upon receipt, the debt is reduced by the zakat paid? There are details but the main difference is timing.
This lesson focuses on the jurisprudential reasoning rather than all practical details.

أو بواسطة التحصيل، ولو كان على مقر مفلس فهو نصاب عند أبي حنيفة لأن تفليس القاضي لا يصح عنده، وعند محمد لا يجب لتحقق الإفلاس عنده بالتفليس، وأبو يوسف مع محمد في تحقق الإفلاس ومع أبي حنيفة في حكم الزكاة قوي وهو بدل القرض ومال التجارة، ومتوسط وهو بدل ما ليس للتجارة كثمن ثياب البذلة وعبد الخدمة ودار السكني، وضعيف وهو بدل ما ليس بمال كالمهر والوصية وبدل الخلع والصلح عن دم العمد والدية وبدل الكتابة وفي الضعيف لا تجب ما لم يقبض نصاباً ويحول الحول بعد القبض عليه. وثمن السائمة كثمن عبد الخدمة. ولو ورث ديناً على رجل فهو كالدين الوسط. وروي أنه كالضعيف. وعندهما الديون كلها سواء تجب الزكاة قبل القبض، كان حكمه كالقوي لأن أجرة مال التجارة كثمن مال التجارة في صحيح الرواية. ا هـ. وفي الولوالجية: وأما إذا أعتق أحد الشريكين عبداً مشتركاً واختاروا المولى تضمين المعتق إن كان العبد للتجارة فحكمه حكم دين الوسط هو الصحيح، وإن كان العبد للخدمة فكذلك أيضاً، وإن اختار استسعاء العبد فحكمه حكم الدين الضعيف اهـ. ومقتضى الأول أن العبد إذا كان للتجارة فحكم هذا الدين حكم الدين القوي، وقد صرح تعريف الديون In al-Walwaljiyya: And if one of two partners frees a jointly owned slave and they choose the master to guarantee the manumitter, if the slave was for trade, its ruling is the ruling of the medium debt—this is correct. And if the slave was for service, likewise. And if they choose the slave's earnings, its ruling is the ruling of the weak debt. End. And the implication of the first is that if the slave was for trade, the ruling of this debt is the ruling of the strong debt, and the definition of the mentioned debts is what agrees with what is in al-Bada'i. Consider. The poor Mahmud Ahmad ibn Abd al-Ghani, writer of these margins, says: I saw here on the margin of al-Bahr in the handwriting of some virtuous person the following: In Ghayat al-Bayan: Then a debt, if it is a substitute for property, is of two types: either it is a substitute for property that, if that property remained in his hand, zakat would not be obligatory on it, like the substitute for service slaves and body clothes; then in the most sound of the two narrations from Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, zakat is not obligatory for the past, and in the other narration, zakat is obligatory when he receives two hundred. Or it is a substitute for property that, if that property remained in his hand, zakat would be obligatory on it, like the substitute for trade goods; then there is no difference among our scholars regarding the obligation of zakat on it, and their difference is regarding the nisab for payment: Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, said it is estimated by forty. According to them, it is obligatory on the little and much of what is received, except the blood-money on the 'aqila and the substitute for kitabah, for which they stipulated the passing of a year after receiving the money because all debts are valid except these two. Then the valid debts on which zakat is obligatory, they differed about them: our scholars said it is not obligatory to pay zakat on them before receipt, and al-Shafi'i said in his new view: if the debt is due on a solvent person who acknowledges it outwardly and inwardly, it is obligatory to pay its zakat even if he has not received it. Our evidence: if immediate payment were obligatory, then paying the complete from the incomplete would be necessary, and that is not permissible, like exchanging white for black. And this is because a debt is less than cash, as evidenced by the fact that paying a debt with cash is not permissible. End. His statement: 'And whenever he receives anything, he pays zakat for it' means that whenever he receives anything, he is obligated to pay the zakat of that amount, whether the received amount is little or much. End. I saw it. His statement: 'And if it is for trade, its ruling is like the strong' etc. I say: This contradicts what is in al-Muhit where he said: Concerning the rent of trade property or a trade slave, there are two narrations. In one narration, there is no zakat on it until he receives it and a year passes over it because benefit is not truly property, so it becomes like a dowry. In the apparent narration, zakat is obligatory on it and payment is obligatory when he receives from it two hundred dirhams because it is a substitute for property that is not a subject for zakat obligation because benefits are truly property but are not a subject for zakat obligation because they are not suitable for lasting a year. End. I said: This is explicit that in the first narration it is from the weak debt, and in the apparent narration it is from the medium, not from the strong, because benefits are not
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the classification of debts for zakat purposes. The author first discusses forgotten buried wealth, then moves to the main topic: zakat on debts. The core is Abu Hanifa's threefold classification of debts: strong (qawi), medium (mutawassit), and weak (da'if). Strong debts are substitutes for property that would have been zakatable if kept (e.g., loan repayment, trade goods price). For these, zakat is obligatory annually but payment is deferred until the owner receives at least forty dirhams, then one dirham per forty is due. Medium debts are substitutes for non-trade property (e.g., price of personal clothing, service slave, dwelling). According to the sound narration, zakat is not obligatory until a nisab is received, but the past years are considered (i.e., zakat for previous years becomes due upon receipt). Weak debts are substitutes for non-property (e.g., dowry, bequest, blood-money). Zakat is not obligatory until a nisab is received and a full year passes after receipt. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) held that all debts are equal: zakat is obligatory before receipt, and whenever any amount is received, zakat for that amount is due, except for kitabah and earnings. A narration also excludes blood-money and compensation before judgment. Abu Hanifa's view distinguishes three categories; the Sahibayn's view simplifies. The passage also includes a Hanafi response to al-Shafi'i, who held that zakat on a debt owed by a solvent acknowledging person is immediately obligatory. The Hanafi argument is that paying zakat on a debt before receipt would be paying complete zakat on an incomplete asset (since debts are less than cash), which is impermissible by analogy with exchanging black for white. The head of the 'illa is that the debt is deficient; one cannot give full zakat on it before actual possession. The passage also discusses related issues like rent for trade property, which follows the strong or medium category depending on the narration. The commentary from Ghayat al-Bayan simplifies to two types: debts on property that would not be zakatable if kept (like service slaves) and those that would be (like trade goods). The author also notes a marginal comment from al-Muhit showing two narrations for rent of trade property, indicating a different classification. Overall, this passage is a detailed exposition of Hanafi intra-school differences and a defense against the Shafi'i position.

Key Terms
دين قوي (dayn qawiyy): Debt that is a substitute for property that would be subject to zakat if kept, such as repayment of a loan or the price of trade goods.
دين متوسط (dayn mutawassit): Debt that is a substitute for property that is not subject to zakat if kept, such as the price of personal effects like clothing or a service slave.
دين ضعيف (dayn da'if): Debt that is a substitute for something that is not considered property (mal) in sharia, such as dowry, bequest, or blood-money.
ضمار (dhimar): A lost or forgotten property that is not considered in one's possession; no zakat due.
عروض التجارة (urud al-tijara): Goods acquired for trade; their price is a strong debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the core Hanafi proof against immediate zakat on debt before receipt?
A) Quranic verse that zakat is only on property in hand.
B) Hadith that no zakat on a debt until received.
C) Qiyas: paying complete zakat on an incomplete asset is like exchanging white for black eggs.
D) Ijma of the Companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school defers zakat on debts until actual receipt because debt is considered deficient, making immediate payment impermissible by qiyas.

The Hanafi Refutation of Shafi'i on Zakat on Uncollected Debts
Refutes Shafi'i on zakat before receipt and presents Hanafi debt classification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on zakat on debts before receipt and refute the Shafi'i argument using the principle of paying complete zakat on incomplete assets.

Core Idea
You cannot pay complete zakat on a debt before receipt, because a debt is incomplete property.

This passage presents the Hanafi classification of debts for zakat and then directly confronts the Shafi'i view that zakat must be paid on uncollected debts if the debtor is wealthy. The Hanafi school argues that a debt is inherently deficient compared to cash, so requiring zakat before receipt would be paying in full for something partial. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of the conditions of ownership (milk) in zakat.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that Hanafis generally do not require zakat on a debt until it is actually received (qabd). However, there is internal classification: strong debts (from trade goods) may require zakat upon receipt of any amount, while weak debts (from non-zakatable items) may not require zakat until a year after receipt.
Intra-madhhab differences exist between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on nisab of payment.
2
State the Shafi'i opposing position
Imam al-Shafi'i in his new opinion (al-qawl al-jadid) holds that if a debt is due, the debtor is wealthy and acknowledges the debt publicly and privately, then the creditor must pay zakat at the end of the year even if the debt has not been collected.
Shafi'i considers such a debt as effectively cash in hand because it can be claimed immediately.
3
Analyze the Hanafi rebuttal (qiyas)
The Hanafis argue that requiring zakat before receipt would mean paying the complete zakat (2.5% of the full value) on an incomplete asset (the debt is not yet possessed). This is invalid by qiyas, like exchanging a white sheep for a black one (an unequal exchange). A debt is deficient compared to cash, as shown by the fact that one cannot pay a debt with the same debt.
The key principle: al-kamil 'an al-naqis (full for deficient) is not allowed.
4
Examine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under the Hanafi ruling, if a person has a debt on a wealthy person that remains unpaid for years, no zakat is due until the debt is collected. Under the Shafi'i view, zakat would be due each year even if the debt is not received, potentially forcing the creditor to pay zakat from other funds.
This difference has significant implications for financial planning and charity.

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed Hanafi classification of debts for zakat purposes and includes a major intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) as well as an inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The author begins by stating a narration that a certain debt is 'like weak,' then presents the view of the Sahibayn that all debts are equal: zakat is due before receipt, and whenever any amount is received, zakat must be paid, with exceptions only for the debts of kitabah (contract of manumission) and sa'ayah (earning of a slave). In another narration, they also exclude diyah (blood money) before judgment and arsh (compensation for injury). The reason for these exceptions is that such obligations are not truly debts in the full sense. Then the text addresses renting out a slave or house: if they are not for trade, no zakat until a year passes after receipt (according to Abu Hanifa); if they are for trade, the rent is treated like a strong debt because rent from trade property is akin to the price of trade property. Next, the case of a jointly owned slave freed by one partner: if the other partner chooses to hold the freed one liable (tadmeen), the debt is intermediate; if the slave was for service, still intermediate; but if they choose istis'a' (letting the slave earn), the debt is weak. The author notes a contradiction with al-Muhit regarding whether such a debt is strong or intermediate. The marginal gloss from Ghayat al-Bayan clarifies by stating that debts as substitutes for property are of two types: (1) substitute for non-zakatable property (like service slaves or clothes) – in the soundest narration from Abu Hanifa, no zakat, in another narration, zakat when 200 dirhams are received; (2) substitute for zakatable property (like trade goods) – no dispute among Hanafis that zakat is obligatory, but they differ on the nisab of payment: Abu Hanifa requires 40 dirhams, while the Sahibayn require any amount. Then the text presents the Shafi'i position: if a debt is due on a wealthy, acknowledging debtor, zakat must be paid even before receipt. The Hanafi response is that accelerating zakat would mean paying the complete (zakat) on an incomplete (debt), which is invalid, analogous to exchanging black for white. A debt is inherently deficient compared to cash, as paying a debt from cash is not permissible. This demonstrates the Hanafi commitment to actual possession (qabd) for zakat on debts.

Key Terms
الدين القوي (al-dayn al-qawiy): A strong debt, e.g., from sale of trade goods; zakat due upon receipt of any amount (Sahibayn) or minimum 40 dirhams (Abu Hanifa).
الدين الضعيف (al-dayn al-da'if): A weak debt, e.g., from non-zakatable assets (household slaves, clothing) or diyah/kitabah; zakat not due until nisab after receipt and year passes (one narration).
دين الوسط (dayn al-wasat): Intermediate debt, e.g., from a freed slave where partner chooses tadmeen; ruling between strong and weak; correct view is intermediate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires actual receipt for zakat on debts, refuting the Shafi'i view that acknowledgment by a wealthy debtor makes the debt equivalent to cash.

Zakat on Acquired Cash with a Weak Debt: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on zakat for mustafād with weak debt

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on zakat for cash acquired during the year when one has a weak debt, and apply the practical consequence if the debtor dies insolvent.

Core Idea
Acquired cash merges with a weak debt's year, but Abu Hanifa defers zakat until receipt of 40 dirhams; Sahibayn obligate immediately.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), on a nuanced zakat scenario. When a person has only a weak debt (dīn ḍa‘īf) and then acquires cash during the year, does zakat become due on that cash at the end of the year? The answer depends on whether the acquired cash is treated independently or as part of the debt. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens your ability to apply the Hanafi rules for delaying zakat on debts until receipt.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the two positions
Read the passage: a person has a debt of 200 dirhams and acquires 100 dirhams during the year. The acquired cash is joined to the debt's year by consensus. At year-end, Abu Hanifa says no zakat on the acquired until at least 40 dirhams are received from the debt; Sahibayn say zakat is due even without any receipt.
The key term 'mustafād' refers to the acquired cash.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta‘līl) of each imam
Abu Hanifa views the acquired as attached to the weak debt, which itself is not subject to zakat until receipt. Thus the acquired follows the debt. Sahibayn view the joining (ḍamm) as making the acquired like cash in hand at the beginning of the year, so zakat is due on the acquired independently.
The reasoning is based on the principle of dependency vs. independence.
3
Examine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
If the debtor dies insolvent, Abu Hanifa drops the zakat on the acquired because it depended on the debt, which is now void. Sahibayn hold the zakat remains due because the acquired became like cash in hand from the start.
This shows the real-world impact of the ikhtilāf.
4
Apply the principle to other cases mentioned
The passage also discusses sold goods before delivery (strong debt ruling), gifting a debt, and discharging the debtor. These illustrate how the strong/weak distinction works in related scenarios.
Focus on the core dispute; the other cases reinforce the concept.

حال الحول إلا شهراً ثم استفاد ألفاً فتم الحول على المائتين لا تجب عليه زكاة الألف ما لم يأخذ من الدين أربعين درهماً فصاعداً في قول أبي حنيفة لأنه لا تجب عليه زكاة المائتين ما لم يقبض أربعين درهماً فإذا لم يجب عليه الأداء عن الأصل لا يجب عن الفائدة ا هـ . ورأيت أيضاً بخطه هنا عند قول صاحب البحر وعندهما تجب لأنه بالضم صار كالموجود الخ ظاهر تعليلهما بقوله «صار كالموجود في ابتداء الحول» يعطي أن النقد لو كان موجوداً من ابتداء الحول غير مستفاد فيجب فيه الزكاة بعد حولان الحول وإن كان أقل من النصاب بالاتفاق ويكون النقد نصاباً بضمه إلى [؟] في البزازية : له مائة نقد ومائة دين على الناس يجب الزكاة وكمل أحدهما بالآخر ا هـ. وقال قاضيخان رجل له مائة درهم في يده ومائة أخرى ديناً له على غيره فحال الحول، ذكر عصام رحمه الله تعالى أن عليه الزكاة وهو محمول على ما إذا كان الدين بدل مال التجارة ويكون المديون ملياً مقراً بالدين. ا هـ . ما رأيته.
زكاة عليه فيه سواء كان ثمن مبيع أو قرضاً أو غير ذلك. صرح به قاضيخان في فتاواه، لكن قيده في المحيط يكون المديون معسراً، أما لو كان موسراً فهو استهلاك وهو تقييد حسن يجب حفظه. وذكر في القنية أن فيه روايتين.
Then al-Walwalji said: All this applies if he has no other trade goods. But if he has other trade goods, then what is received from the weak debt becomes joined to what he has, so zakat becomes obligatory on it even if it does not reach the nisab. Similarly in al-Muhit: If someone has two hundred dirhams as a debt and during the year acquires one hundred dirhams, the acquired is joined to the debt in its year by consensus. When the year completes on the debt, he is not obligated to pay zakat on the acquired until he receives forty dirhams. According to them (the two companions), he is obligated even if he receives nothing. The benefit of the dispute appears if the debtor dies insolvent: according to him (Abu Hanifa), the zakat on the acquired falls because it was made dependent on the debt, so it falls with its falling; according to them, it is obligatory because by joining it became like existing at the beginning of the year, so he owes zakat on the cash, not the debt. And we previously mentioned that the sold item before delivery does not require zakat on the buyer. And in al-Muhit, in explaining the divisions of debt, that the sold item before delivery is said not to be a nisab because ownership is deficient due to lack of possession; but the correct view is that it is a nisab because it is a substitute for property over which his hand was established, and he could potentially have possession of the substitute, so his hand is considered remaining on the nisab by virtue of legal capability. According to this, their saying 'zakat is not obligatory' means before its receipt; after its receipt, zakat becomes due for the past period like a strong debt. And in al-Muhit: A man gifted a debt he had on someone and appointed an agent to collect it, and he did not collect it until zakat became due on it; then the zakat is on the donor because the donee's receipt is like the owner's receipt. Then know that all this applies if he did not discharge the debtor. If he discharged the debtor after the year, then there is no zakat on it even though it is property in reality. Reflect. Then I saw in al-Walwaljiyya explicit mention that there are three narrations. His saying: 'And when the year completes etc.' The bare marginal notes say: I saw in the handwriting of some scholars on the margin of al-Bahr here at his saying 'And when the year completes' the following text: And Qadikhan said: A man has two hundred dirhams due on another man, the year completes except one month, then he acquires a thousand, the year completes on the two hundred: zakat on the thousand is not obligatory on him unless he takes from the debt forty dirhams or more according to Abu Hanifa, because zakat on the two hundred is not obligatory on him until he receives forty dirhams, and if payment on the principal is not obligatory, it is not obligatory on the benefit. And I also saw in his handwriting here at the saying of the author of al-Bahr 'And according to them it is obligatory because by joining it became like existing etc.' the apparent meaning of their reasoning by saying 'it became like existing at the beginning of the year' gives that if cash were existing from the beginning of the year, not acquired, zakat would be obligatory after the year even if it is less than the nisab by agreement, and the cash would be nisab by joining it to ... in al-Bazzaziyya: He has one hundred cash and one hundred debt on people, zakat is obligatory, and one completes the other. And Qadikhan said: A man has one hundred dirhams in hand and another one hundred as a debt due on someone else, the year completes; Isam mentioned that zakat is obligatory on him, and it is carried on the case where the debt is in place of trade goods and the debtor is solvent and acknowledging the debt. I saw it.
Zakat is obligatory on it whether it is the price of a sale or a loan or other. Qadikhan stated this in his Fatawa, but he restricted it in al-Muhit to the debtor being insolvent. If he is solvent, it is consumption, and this is a good restriction that must be memorized. And it is mentioned in al-Quniyya that there are two narrations.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with the complex issue of zakat on weak debts (dīn ḍa‘īf). It begins by citing al-Walwaljī's statement: if a person has no other trade goods, the debt is treated as weak and zakat is not due until receipt; but if he has other trade goods, the received debt becomes part of the trade stock and zakat is due immediately. Then it presents a scenario from al-Muhīt: a person has a debt of 200 dirhams and during the year acquires 100 dirhams. By consensus, the acquired cash is added to the debt's year. However, when the year completes on the debt, Abu Hanifa holds that zakat on the acquired is not due until at least 40 dirhams are received from the debt, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that zakat is due immediately, even without receipt. The practical difference appears if the debtor dies insolvent: according to Abu Hanifa, the zakat on the acquired falls because it was dependent on the debt; according to the Sahibayn, it remains due because the addition made it like cash in hand at the start of the year. The passage then discusses the ruling on sold goods before delivery: the correct view is that such goods count as nisab because they are a substitute for property that was in one's possession, so zakat becomes due after receipt like a strong debt. Next, a case of gifting a debt: if the donor appoints an agent to collect, and the agent delays until zakat is due, the zakat is on the donor. Finally, it addresses discharging the debtor after the year: if the debtor is discharged, no zakat is due because the debt has been extinguished. The margin notes add Qadikhan's view that if the acquired cash exceeds 40 dirhams, zakat is not due until that amount is received, and discuss the restriction that the debtor must be insolvent for zakat to be due; if solvent, the discharge is considered consumption (istihlāk) and zakat may not be due. This passage is a rich intra-Hanafi dispute with practical implications.

Key Terms
دين ضعيف (dīn ḍa‘īf): A debt that is not backed by tangible trade assets or where the debtor is insolvent; zakat on it is not due until receipt.
مستفاد (mustafād): Cash or property acquired during the year, which may be added to the existing debt for zakat purposes.
ضم (ḍamm): The legal principle of adding acquired property to an existing debt for the purpose of calculating zakat.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf) of the dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding zakat on acquired cash?
A) The nisab threshold changes.
B) The ruling on gifting a debt becomes different.
C) If the debtor dies insolvent, Abu Hanifa drops the zakat on the acquired; Sahibayn keep it.
D) The acquired cash is never subject to zakat according to either.

Key Takeaway
The acquisition of cash during the year does not automatically trigger zakat if the only other asset is a weak debt; Abu Hanifa waits for receipt of 40 dirhams, while the Sahibayn obligate immediately.
