Categories of Wealth for Zakat: Natural vs. Acquired and Intention
Defining two categories of wealth for zakat and when intention is required for trade goods and livestock.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two categories of wealth for zakat, explain why gold and silver are khilqī, and apply the rules for valid intention on trade goods and livestock.

Core Idea
Gold and silver are zakatable by nature; trade goods require intention tied to an exchange act.

Zakat is not uniform across all types of wealth. Some assets, like gold and silver, are inherently zakatable, while others, like merchandise and livestock, only become so through human intention and action. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi classification of wealth into khilqī (natural) and fi‘lī (acquired), and clarifies when and how intention makes property subject to zakat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
The passage divides all wealth into khilqī (natural) and fi‘lī (acquired). Determine which assets fall into each category based on the text.
Khilqī is limited to gold and silver; fi‘lī includes everything else.
2
Understand why gold and silver are khilqī
Gold and silver are suited for direct use in meeting basic needs, so they are inherently ready for trade without requiring a specific intention. Zakat is due on them regardless of the owner's intention.
This is a unique Hanafi position.
3
Recognize the intention requirement for trade goods and livestock
For trade goods (ʿurūḍ), a valid intention for trade (niyyat al-tijāra) is necessary. For livestock, intention for pasturing (niyyat al-isāma) is required. The intention must be connected to an act of acquisition or pasturing.
Intention alone, without a connected contract or act, is insufficient.
4
Learn when intention is valid
Intention can be explicit (ṣarīḥ) at the time of a trade contract or implicit (dalāla) from the circumstances. It is not valid for property acquired without exchange, such as inheritance or gifts, unless later transacted.
Contracts like gift, bequest, and charity do not support trade intent.
5
Note exceptions and special cases
Inherited livestock is zakatable even without intention if it reaches nisab. Agricultural produce is subject to ʿushr or kharāj, not trade zakat, even if intended for sale.
These exceptions highlight the primacy of inherent factors over intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولم يبين المصنف رحمه الله ما يكون محلاً للنماء التقديري من الأموال وحاصله أنها قسمان خلقي وفعلي. فالخلقي الذهب والفضة لأنها تصلح للانتفاع بأعيانها في دفع الحوائج الأصلية فلا حاجة إلى الإعداد من العبد للتجارة بالنية إذ النية للتعيين وهي متعينة للتجارة بأصل الخلقة فتجب الزكاة فيها، نوى التجارة أو لم ينو أصلاً أو نوى النفقة. والفعلي ما سواهما فإنما يكون الإعداد فيها للتجارة بالنية إذا كانت عروضاً، وكذا في المواشي لا بد فيها من نية الإسامة لأنها كما تصلح للدر والنسل تصلح للحمل وللركوب. ثم نية التجارة والإسامة لا تعتبر ما لم تتصل بفعل التجارة والإسامة. ثم نية التجارة قد تكون صريحاً وقد تكون دلالة، فالصريح ينوي عند عقد التجارة أن يكون المملوك به للتجارة سواء كان ذلك العقد شراء أو إجارة، وسواء كان ذلك الثمن من النقود أو من العروض، فلو نوى أن يكون للبذلة لا يكون للتجارة وإن كان الثمن من النقود. فخرج ما ملكه بغير عقد كالميراث فلا تصح فيه نية التجارة إذا كان من غير النقود إلا إذا تصرف فيه فحينئذ تجب الزكاة. كذا في شرح المجمع للمصنف. وفي الخانية ولو ورث سائمة كان عليه الزكاة إذا حال الحول، نوى أو لم ينو. وخرج أيضاً ما إذا دخل من أرضه حنطة تبلغ قيمتها قيمة نصاب ونوى أن يمسكها ويبيعها فأمسكها حولاً لا تجب فيها الزكاة كما في الميراث، وكذا لو اشترى بذراً للتجارة وزرعها في أرض عشر استأجرها كان فيها العشر لا غير كما لو اشترى أرض خراج أو عشر للتجارة لم يكن عليه زكاة التجارة إنما عليه حق الأرض من العشر أو الخراج. وخرج ما ملكه بعقد ليس فيه مبادلة أصلاً كالهبة والوصية والصدقة أو ملكه بعقد هو مبادلة مال بغير مال كالمهر وبدل الخلع والصلح عن دم العمد وبدل العتق فإنه لا تصح فيه نية التجارة وهو الأصح لأن التجارة كسب المال ببدل هو مال والقبول هنا اكتساب المال بغير بدل أصلاً فلم يكن من باب التجارة فلم تكن النية مقارنة لعمل التجارة. كذا صححه في
And the author (may Allah have mercy on him) did not clarify what qualifies as a locus for potential growth (namā' taqdīrī) among properties. The summary is that they are two types: khilqī (natural) and fi‘lī (acquired). Khilqī is gold and silver, because they are suitable for direct use in fulfilling basic needs, so there is no need for the servant to prepare them for trade by intention; intention is for designation, and they are designated for trade by original nature, so zakat is obligatory on them whether he intends trade or does not intend at all, or intends spending. The fi‘lī is whatever is other than these two; preparation for trade is only by intention if they are trade goods. Similarly for livestock, intention for pasturing (isāmah) is necessary because, just as they are suitable for milk and offspring, they are also suitable for riding and carrying loads. Then the intention for trade and pasturing is not considered unless it is connected to an act of trade or pasturing. Then intention for trade may be explicit or implicit. The explicit is that he intends at the time of the trade contract that the property is for trade, whether that contract is purchase or lease, and whether the price is cash or goods. If he intends it for personal use (bādhlah), it is not for trade even if the price is cash. This excludes what is owned without a contract, like inheritance, so intention for trade is not valid for it if it is other than cash, unless he transacts with it; then zakat becomes due. This is in Sharh al-Majma‘ by the author. And in al-Khāniyya: If he inherits grazing livestock, zakat is due on him when the year completes, whether he intends or not. Also excluded is when he receives wheat from his land whose value reaches the nisab and he intends to store it and sell it, and stores it for a year: no zakat is due on it, as in inheritance. Similarly, if he buys seeds for trade and plants them in land he rented as ʿushr land, only the ʿushr is due, just as if he bought kharāj or ʿushr land for trade, he is not liable for trade zakat; he only owes the land tax of ʿushr or kharāj. Also excluded is what he owns through a contract that is not an exchange at all, like gift, bequest, charity, or through a contract that is an exchange of property for non-property like dowry, khulʿ compensation, settlement for deliberate killing, and emancipation compensation: intention for trade is not valid in them, and this is the correct view, because trade is earning property in exchange for property, and here the acceptance is acquiring property without any exchange, so it is not from the category of trade, so the intention is not connected to a trade act. This is how it is verified in...
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies the types of wealth subject to zakat based on potential for growth (namā'). The author says wealth is of two kinds: khilqī (natural) and fi‘lī (acquired). Khilqī includes gold and silver, which by their nature are suited for trade and satisfying needs; therefore, zakat is wajib on them regardless of intention, even if the owner intends to spend them. Fi‘lī includes everything else, such as trade goods (ʿurūḍ) and livestock. For trade goods, intention (niyya) for trade is required at the time of acquisition through an exchange contract, and the intention must be connected to an act of trade. If the intention is for personal use (bādhlah), it does not count. Livestock requires intention for pasturing (isāmah) to be considered for zakat, because they can also be used for riding. The passage also lists cases where intention is ineffective: property acquired without a contract (inheritance, gift, bequest, charity) cannot become trade goods by mere intention unless the owner later transacts with it. Similarly, agricultural produce from land is subject to ʿushr or kharāj, not trade zakat. The ruling is from Sharh al-Majma‘ and al-Khāniyya, and is the relied-upon position.

Key Terms
خلقي (khilqī): Natural type of wealth (gold and silver) that is subject to zakat by its inherent nature, without need for intention.
فعلي (fi‘lī): Acquired type of wealth (trade goods, livestock) that becomes subject to zakat only through human intention and action.
نية التجارة (niyyat al-tijāra): Intention to use property for trade; must be connected to an act of trade (e.g., purchase) to be valid.
نية الإسامة (niyyat al-isāma): Intention to pasture livestock for grazing; required for zakat on livestock.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Livestock
C) Gold and silver
D) Agricultural produce

Key Takeaway
Gold and silver are inherently zakatable, whereas trade goods and livestock require a valid intention connected to an act of trade or pasturing to become subject to zakat.

Key Refinements in Zakat on Debts and Borrowed Trade Goods
Critical refinement and intra-madhhab dispute on zakat rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why discharging a solvent debtor before the year is consumption not obligation, and distinguish the two Hanafi positions on whether borrowed trade goods become zakatable by intention.

Core Idea
Discharging a solvent debtor before the year ends is consumption, not obligation; borrowed goods may become trade goods by intention—a disputed Hanafi point.

This marginal note from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq refines a common ruling: discharging a debtor before the year passes. It corrects a misunderstanding about weak debts. Then it shifts to a deeper dispute: when someone borrows trade goods intending trade, do those goods become subject to zakat? The text records an internal Hanafi difference that has practical consequences for merchants.

Guided Path
1
Understand the original ruling
Recall that zakat on a debt is due only after receipt of payment, provided a year has passed since the debt was contracted. Deliberate discharge (ibrā') before receipt cancels the debt and thus the zakat obligation, but only if the debtor is insolvent.
2
Identify the refinement regarding weak debts
The commentator from al-Nahr corrects: the restriction 'debtor must be insolvent' is a limitation of a general statement, but it is incorrect for weak debts. Weak debts (such as those from insolvent debtors) do not require zakat before receipt even if a year passes. Hence discharging a solvent weak-debtor is consumption before the obligation arises.
Weak debt: debt from an insolvent person; it is not considered property for zakat until paid.
3
Recognize the dispute on borrowing for trade
The text moves to a different issue: if a person borrows trade goods (qarḍ of ʿurūḍ) with the intention to trade, do those goods become 'trade goods' for zakat purposes? Some later Hanafi scholars say yes; others say no.
4
Trace the dalil aqli for the affirmative position
Those who say yes argue that a loan ultimately becomes an exchange of property for property (the borrower must return an equivalent). Thus, the loan is like a barter from the start, and intention can make it a trade.
Dalil aqli: rational analogy based on the nature of exchange.
5
Determine the thamarah (practical consequence)
If borrowed goods become trade goods, they are zakatable if they reach nisab and a full year passes. If not, no zakat is due. This affects merchants who frequently borrow stock with trade intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (وهو تقييد حسن الخ) قال في النهر : هذا ظاهر في أنه تقييد للإطلاق وهو غير صحيح في الضعيف كما لا يخفى ا هـ . أي لأن الضعيف لا يجب فيه الزكاة قبل القبض ما لم يمض حول فيكون إبراء الموسر استهلاكاً قبل الوجوب قوله : (وإليه أشار في الجامع كما في البدائع) نص عبارة البدائع : ولو استقرض عروضاً ونوى أن تكون للتجارة اختلف المشايخ فيه؛ قال بعضهم: تصير للتجارة لأن القرض ينقلب معاوضة المال بالمال في العاقبة وإليه أشار في الجامع أن كان له مائتا درهم لا مال له غيرها فاستقرض من رجل قبل حولان الحول خمسة أقفزة لغير التجارة ولم
His saying: (And it is a good restriction etc.) He said in al-Nahr: This is apparently a restriction of the absolute, and it is not correct regarding the weak, as is not hidden. That is, because zakat is not obligatory on the weak debt before receipt until a year passes, so discharging a solvent debtor is consumption before obligation. His saying: (And he indicated it in al-Jami‘ as in al-Bada’i‘) The text of al-Bada’i‘: If he borrows trade goods and intends them for trade, the scholars differed regarding it. Some said they become trade goods because a loan turns into an exchange of property for property in the end. And he indicated it in al-Jami‘: If he has two hundred dirhams and has no other property, and he borrows from someone before the year passes five measures of grain for non-trade, and...
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This marginal note (hāshiya) from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq comments on the earlier restriction (taqyīd) that the debtor must be insolvent for zakat to be due. The author of al-Nahr points out that this restriction is a limitation of the general rule, but it is not correct regarding weak debts, because weak debts do not require zakat before receipt until a year passes. Therefore, discharging a solvent debtor before that would be consumption before the obligation is established. The note then moves to a different issue: borrowing trade goods with the intention for trade. It quotes from al-Badā’i‘ that some Mashāyikh hold that borrowed goods become trade goods because a loan eventually becomes an exchange of property. This is indicated in al-Jāmi‘ by the example of a person who has only 200 dirhams and borrows five measures of grain for non-trade before the year passes. The text is cut off, but the point is that the intention for trade in borrowed goods is a subject of difference among later Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
إبراء (ibrā'): Discharge of a debt by the creditor; if done after the year, it cancels zakat because the debt is extinguished.
استهلاك (istihlāk): Consumption of the debt by the creditor through discharge; in this context, it prevents zakat because it is done before the obligation is due.
قرض (qarḍ): A loan of fungible goods or money; the borrower must return an equivalent. Intention for trade may affect zakat.
عروض (ʿurūḍ): Trade goods (merchandise) other than cash or animals; subject to zakat if intended for trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because weak debts require zakat only after receipt
C) Because discharging a solvent debtor is consumption before obligation
D) Because weak debts are not property

Key Takeaway
The timing of discharge and the intention behind borrowing can affect zakat liability in Hanafi fiqh; the passage refines the first issue and leaves the second as an open dispute.

Zakat on Debts: The Condition of Insolvency and the Strong/Weak Debt Distinction
Ruling on zakat on debts with condition of insolvency and a gloss on the restriction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on zakat on debts, explain the condition of insolvency, and apply the distinction between strong and weak debts to determine when zakat is due.

Core Idea
Zakat on a debt is only due when the debtor is insolvent and the debt is strong.

This passage examines the obligation of zakat on debts. While some scholars initially state an absolute obligation, later Hanafi authorities refine it: zakat is only due when the debtor is insolvent and the debt is strong (i.e., received and a year passed). The discussion highlights the importance of distinguishing between strong and weak debts and the role of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Read the passage and note that Qadikhan states zakat is due on any debt, whether from a sale, loan, or other transaction. This is the starting point.
2
Note the restriction from al-Muhit
The author in al-Muhit restricts this ruling: zakat is only due if the debtor is insolvent. If the debtor is solvent and the creditor forgives the debt, it is considered wastage (istihlak), not a discharge of zakat.
The restriction is considered 'good and must be preserved' according to the original author.
3
Understand the refinement from al-Nahr
Al-Nahr points out that the restriction is not correct for weak debts (dayn da'if). Weak debts are those not yet received or for which a year has not passed. For weak debts, zakat is not due at all before receipt, so the absolute ruling cannot apply.
This refinement shows that the restriction is only relevant for strong debts.
4
Distinguish strong vs weak debts
Apply the Hanafi classification: a strong debt (dayn qawi) is one that is due and a year has passed; a weak debt (dayn da'if) is one that is not yet received or a year has not passed. Zakat is only due on strong debts.
This distinction is key to understanding the disagreement.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The mufta bihi (operative position) is that zakat on a debt is wajib only if the debtor is insolvent and the debt is strong. The condition of insolvency applies only to strong debts; weak debts do not incur zakat regardless.
This integrates both the restriction and the refinement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زكاة عليه فيه سواء كان ثمن مبيع أو قرضاً أو غير ذلك. صرح به قاضيخان في فتاواه، لكن قيده في المحيط يكون المديون معسراً، أما لو كان موسراً فهو استهلاك وهو تقييد حسن يجب حفظه. قوله: (وهو تقييد حسن الخ) قال في النهر : هذا ظاهر في أنه تقييد للإطلاق وهو غير صحيح في الضعيف كما لا يخفى ا هـ . أي لأن الضعيف لا يجب فيه الزكاة قبل القبض ما لم يمض حول فيكون إبراء الموسر استهلاكاً قبل الوجوب
Zakat is due on it whether it is the price of a sale, a loan, or otherwise. Qadikhan stated clearly in his Fatawa, but al-Muhit restricted it to when the debtor is insolvent. If he is solvent, then it is wastage, and this restriction is good and must be preserved. His statement: (It is a good restriction, etc.) Al-Nahr said: This appears to be a restriction of the absolute, but it is not correct regarding the weak debt, as is not hidden. That is because zakat is not obligatory on a weak debt before receipt until a year passes, so the solvent debtor's remission is wastage before obligation.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the obligation of zakat on debts, followed by a critical refinement of that ruling. The base ruling, from Qadikhan's Fatawa, states that zakat is due on any debt whether it originates from a sale, a loan, or other transaction. However, al-Muhit restricts this ruling to cases where the debtor is insolvent; if the debtor is solvent and the creditor forgives the debt, that act is considered wastage (istihlak) rather than a valid discharge of zakat obligation. The commentator in al-Nahr then refines this restriction: the absolute ruling that zakat is due on debts is not correct for a 'weak' debt (dayn da'if), such as a debt that has not been received and for which a year has not passed. In that case, zakat is not yet due, so the creditor's act of forgiving a solvent debtor is indeed wastage before the obligation arises. The key legal point is that zakat on a debt is only obligatory if the debt is 'strong' (qawi) and a year has passed; forgiving a solvent debtor before that time is a voluntary act that does not discharge the zakat duty.

Key Terms
ضعيف (da'if): A debt that is not yet due for zakat because it has not been received or a year has not passed.
استهلاك (istihlak): Wastage or consumption of property without legal benefit, here referring to forgiving a debt when zakat is due.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does al-Nahr criticize the restriction of the absolute ruling to insolvency?
A) Because the restriction is not mentioned in the Quran
B) Because the restriction does not apply to weak debts
C) Because the restriction contradicts Qadikhan
D) Because the restriction was forgotten

Key Takeaway
Zakat on a debt is wajib only when the debtor is insolvent and the debt is strong; weak debts incur no zakat until received and a year passes.

Intention for Trade on Borrowed Goods and Changing Intention
Dispute on intention for trade and change of intention in Hanafi zakat law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether borrowed goods become trade goods by intention, and explain the asymmetry in changing intention for trade goods vs personal goods.

Core Idea
Trade requires action; abandoning trade requires only intention.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq deals with two subtle issues in Hanafi zakat law: first, an intra-Hanafi dispute about borrowed goods intended for trade, and second, the rule on changing the intention for goods from personal use to trade or vice versa. Understanding the asymmetry—that establishing trade needs an act, but removing trade status can be done by mere intention—is crucial for correctly applying zakat obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main issues
The passage addresses: (1) whether borrowed goods (qard) become trade goods by intention alone, and (2) the effect of changing intention on goods already owned.
Look for the phrase 'ولو استقرض' and 'ولو اشترى' as markers.
2
Analyze the dispute on borrowed goods
Note that some scholars reject intention for trade on borrowed goods because a loan is a gratuity; the preferred view (supported by al-Jami') accepts it.
The author says 'الظاهر أنها تكون للتجارة' – the apparent view.
3
Understand the asymmetry in changing intention
For goods purchased for personal use, later intention for trade is ineffective until an actual sale occurs. For goods already intended for trade, a change to personal use is effective by mere intention.
The reason: trade is an action (عمل) requiring a physical act; abandoning trade is a cessation (ترك العمل), attained by intention.
4
Examine the analogy to other Hanafi rules
Al-Zayla'i draws parallels to resident/traveler, fasting/breaking fast, etc., where status is established by intention but not removed by intention. However, he equates ulufa and sa'ima, which may be later corrected.
This analogy highlights the principle that some legal statuses are created by intention, others by action.
5
Apply the thamarah al-khilaf
If intention for trade on borrowed goods is effective, zakat is due; otherwise not. For change of intention, goods become trade goods only after sale, but leave trade status immediately upon new intention.
Practical consequence for zakat calculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استقرض عروضاً ونوى أن تكون للتجارة اختلف المشايخ، والظاهر أنها تكون للتجارة وإليه أشار في الجامع كما في البدائع. ولو اشترى عروضاً للبذلة والمهنة ثم نوى أن تكون للتجارة بعد ذلك لا تصير للتجارة مالم يبعها فيكون بدلها للتجارة لأن التجارة عمل فلا تتم بمجرد النية بخلاف ما إذا كان للتجارة فنوى أن تكون للبذلة خرج عن التجارة بالنية وإن لم يستعمله لأنها ترك العمل فتتم بها . قال الشارح الزيلعي ونظيره المقيم والصائم والكافر والعلوفة والسائمة حيث لا يكون مسافراً ولا مفطراً ولا مسلماً ولا سائمة ولا علوفة بمجرد النية ويكون مقيماً وصائماً وكافراً بالنية .اهـ. فقد سوى بين العلوفة والسائمة.
And if one borrows goods and intends them for trade, the scholars differed, and the apparent view is that they become for trade, and al-Jami' alluded to this as in al-Bada'i. And if one purchases goods for personal use and professional use, then later intends them for trade, they do not become for trade until he sells them, so their replacement becomes for trade, because trade is an action and is not completed by mere intention. Unlike if it was for trade and he intends it for personal use, it leaves trade by intention even if he does not use it, because that is abandoning an action, so it is completed by intention. The commentator al-Zayla'i said: Its analogy is the resident, the fasting person, the disbeliever, the fodder-fed animal, and the grazing animal: where one is not a traveler, not a break-faster, not a Muslim, not a grazing animal, and not a fodder-fed animal by mere intention, and one becomes a resident, a faster, and a disbeliever by intention. So he equated between the fodder-fed and the grazing animal.
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage handles two distinct but related issues in Hanafi zakat law. First, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute about borrowing goods (qard) with the intention for trade. Some scholars hold that the intention is ineffective because a loan is a gratuity, not a trade; others, including the author's preferred view, hold that borrowed goods become trade goods by intention, and this is supported by al-Jami'. The practical consequence is that if the borrower intends trade, zakat becomes due on the borrowed goods. Second, the passage discusses changing intention: if one buys goods for personal use and later intends them for trade, they do not become trade goods until the owner actually sells them; but if goods were intended for trade and the owner changes intention to personal use, the goods leave the trade category by mere intention. This asymmetry is because trade is an action (requiring a trade act), while abandoning trade is a cessation of action (completed by intention). Al-Zayla'i draws an analogy to other Hanafi legal principles: residency, fasting, and disbelief are established by intention alone, whereas their opposites require action. He equates fodder-fed (ulufa) and grazing (sa'ima) animals, but this is later corrected in other sources.

Key Terms
علوفة (ulufa): Fodder-fed livestock; not subject to zakat as sa'ima (grazing).
سائمة (sa'ima): Grazing livestock; subject to zakat if the owner intends them for grazing.
نية التجارة (niyyat al-tijara): Intent for trade; transforms certain assets into trade goods for zakat purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They immediately become trade goods
B) They become trade goods only after he sells them
C) They never become trade goods
D) They become trade goods if he uses them

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi zakat law, intention for trade can transform borrowed goods into trade assets, but changing intention is asymmetrical: leaving trade requires only intention, while entering trade requires an act of sale.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Does Intention Turn Borrowed Goods into Trade Goods for Zakat?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on borrowing and intention for zakat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the conflict between the apparent view and the soundest view in the Hanafi school regarding whether borrowed goods become trade goods by intention, and identify the practical consequence for zakat.

Core Idea
Borrowed goods do not become trade goods by mere intention because a loan is akin to ariyah.

This passage presents a dispute among Hanafi scholars on a subtle point: if someone borrows goods (like wheat or cloth) and intends them for trade, do the goods become subject to zakat as trade assets? Some say yes, based on the power of intention, but the most authoritative later opinion, upheld by Shaykh al-Islam, says no. Understanding this dispute sharpens your grasp of how intention interacts with contractual categories.

Guided Path
1
State the two positions
First, identify the apparent view (from al-Jami') that borrowed goods become trade goods with intention, and the opposing view (Shaykh al-Islam) that they do not.
Both views are within the Hanafi school.
2
Analyse the proof of the apparent view
The apparent view relies on the principle that intention can change the category of property, analogized to how intention alone can change a person's status (e.g., fasting by intention).
Al-Zayla'i gave analogies of resident/traveler, fasting/non-fasting, etc.
3
Analyse the proof of the opposing view
The opposing view argues that a loan (qard) is a gratuitous contract like ariyah (loan for use), and intention for trade in a gratuitous contract is ineffective because the borrower never 'trades' with the goods until an actual sale occurs.
Shaykh al-Islam emphasizes that a loan is not a trade act.
4
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If intention works, zakat is due on the borrowed goods after one year; if not, no zakat, unless the borrower sells them and then holds the proceeds for trade.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The author mentions that his adopted view is the apparent one, but then clarifies that the soundest view (al-asahh) is that intention does not work, as stated by Shaykh al-Islam.
Thus the reliable position for fatwa is that borrowed goods are not trade assets by mere intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he borrows goods and intends them for trade, the mashayikh differed, and the apparent view is that they become for trade, and to this he pointed in al-Jami' as in al-Bada'i'. And if he bought goods for personal use and service, then later intended them for trade, they do not become for trade unless he sells them, so their substitute becomes for trade because trade is an action and is not completed by mere intention, unlike if they were for trade and he intended them for personal use: they exit trade by intention even if he does not use them, because it is abandoning an action and is completed by intention. Al-Sharih al-Zayla'i said: and its analogy is the resident, the fasting person, the disbeliever, the fodder-fed animal, and the grazing animal, where one does not become a traveler, nor a non-fasting person, nor a Muslim, nor a grazing animal, nor a fodder-fed animal by mere intention, but one becomes a resident, fasting, and a disbeliever by intention. So he equated between fodder-fed and grazing. So his statement: 'He borrowed for non-trade' is evidence that if he borrows for trade, it becomes for trade. And some said: it does not become for trade even with intention because a loan is a favor and is a donation, not trade, so the intention of trade did not occur concurrent with trade, so it is not considered. So based on what was indicated in al-Jami', if he intends trade, zakat is obligatory on what he borrowed, and it is not said that it is occupied by debt because the debt is directed to the dirhams in his hand as previously transmitted from al-Sharih al-Zayla'i, such that if the value of the bushels he borrowed increases, the increase in their value is added to the two hundred dirhams in his hand, so zakat becomes obligatory on them as well. Similarly, if it did not increase, the loan is directed to them even if it results in a deficiency from the nisab, because they are added to the trade wealth, so he pays zakat on both when a year passes. Then, the author adopted one of the two opinions here contrary to the soundest view because in al-Dhakhira, after mentioning similar to the above passage from al-Bada'i', Shaykh al-Islam said in Sharh al-Jami': the soundest is that it – i.e., the intention for trade in a loan – does not work because a loan is like a loan for use (ariyah) and the intention for loans for use is not valid. And the meaning of Muhammad's statement 'He borrowed wheat for non-trade' is that he borrowed wheat that was with the lender for non-trade, and the benefit of that is that when it is returned to him, it returns for non-trade; if it was with the lender for trade, then when returned, it returns for trade.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether borrowed goods become subject to zakat as trade goods if the borrower intends them for trade. The presented view (apparent) is that they do, based on the principle that intention can transform goods from one category to another, as shown in the analogy with personal use goods and the example of a grazing animal. However, the author notes that the soundest view, according to Shaykh al-Islam, is that intention alone does not work because a loan is a gratuitous contract (like 'ariyah), and the intention for trade in such a contract is not legally effective. The text also clarifies that if intention does work, then zakat calculations must include the borrowed goods with other trade assets, even if that brings the total above nisab. The author cautions that his adopted opinion here may not be the strongest in the school.

Key Terms
قرض (qard): A loan of goods or money that must be repaid.
نية (niyyah): The intention in the heart that distinguishes an act of worship or changes the legal status of property.
تجارة (tijarah): The activity of buying and selling with the intention of profit.
عارية (ariyah): A gratuitous loan of an object for use, with the obligation to return the same object.

The soundest Hanafi view is that borrowed goods do not become trade goods by mere intention, because a loan is a gratuitous contract like 'ariyah.

Intention and Zakat Classification: A Hanafi Critical Refinement
Ruling on intention's effect on zakat status of grazing vs. fodder-fed animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the asymmetric effect of intention on zakat classification of livestock according to Hanafi sources, and identify the internal contradiction and the proposed reconciliation.

Core Idea
Intention alone can declassify a grazing animal but not reclassify a fodder-fed one—yet some Hanafi texts contradict this.

This lesson unravels a nuanced debate within the Hanafi school about whether a mere intention can change the zakat status of livestock. The transmitted reports in al-Nihaya and Fath al-Qadir hold that a grazing animal becomes fodder-fed by intention, but not the reverse. However, other authoritative sources like al-Mabsut and al-Khulasa suggest that intention can create grazing status by abandoning fodder-feeding. The author, Zayn al-Din Ibn Nujaym, attempts a reconciliation and leaves us with a puzzle that calls for deeper study.

Guided Path
1
State the transmitted ruling
Identify the two rulings from al-Nihaya and Fath al-Qadir: a grazing animal (sa'imah) becomes a fodder-fed animal ('ulufah) by mere intention; but a fodder-fed animal does not become a grazing animal by intention alone.
This asymmetry is the starting point of the discussion.
2
Understand the author's reconciliation
Ibn Nujaym reconciles the conflicting reports by distinguishing the context: the first statement applies when the animal is still in the pasture and the owner intends it to become fodder-fed but does not actually act to feed it; the second applies when the owner intends to fodder-feed after removing the animal from pasture.
The reconciliation turns on the timing and the presence or absence of actual action.
3
Identify the contradictory report
The author then cites a passage from al-Nahr quoting al-Diraya: if a person intended to sell, use, or fodder-feed a grazing animal but did not act, the animal remains sa'imah. Conversely, if a person intended for a fodder-fed animal to become grazing, it becomes sa'imah because the essence of grazing is established by abandoning fodder-feeding.
This directly contradicts the transmitted reports, as it allows intention to create sa'imah status.
4
Analyze the nature of the contradiction
Note that the contradictory report uses the principle that 'the meaning of sa'imah is established by abandoning action (tark al-'amal).' In the transmitted reports, intention alone is sufficient to change from sa'imah to 'ulufah, but not vice versa. Here, intention is sufficient both ways.
The author does not resolve the contradiction but instructs the reader to ponder it.
5
Evaluate the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: if an owner intends to change the feeding status of livestock during the zakat year, does the zakat obligation change? Under the transmitted reports, if he intends a grazing animal to become fodder-fed, it becomes exempt; but if he intends a fodder-fed animal to become grazing, it does not become liable. Under the contradictory report, intention can make a fodder-fed animal liable as sa'imah.
The mufta bihi is not clearly identified, but the author's reconciliation suggests a middle course.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمنقول في النهاية وفتح القدير أن العلوفة لا تصير سائمة بمجرد النية والسائمة تصير علوفة بمجردها، وقد ظهر لي التوفيق بينهما أن كلام الشارح محمول على ما إذا نوى أن تكون السائمة علوفة وهي في المرعى ولم يرد بالعمل أن يعلفها وكلام غيره محمول على ما إذا نوى أن تكون علوفة بعد إخراجها من المرعى، وهذا التوفيق يدل عليه ما في النهاية في ، يستهلك إلا قفزة حتى حال الحول، لا زكاة عليه ويصرف الدين إلى مال الزكاة دون الجنس الذي ليس بمال الزكاة. يصير قال في النهر أقول في الدراية : لو أراد أن يبيع السائمة أو يستعملها أو يعلفها فلم يفعل حتى حال الحول فعليه زكاة السائمة لأنه نوى العمل ولم يعمل فلم ينعدم به وصف الأسامة، ولو نوى في العلوفة صارت سائمة لأن معنى الأسامة يثبت بترك العمل وقد ترك العمل حقيقة. كذا في المبسوط والخلاصة، وهذا يخالف النقلين فتدبره .
And what is transmitted in al-Nihaya and Fath al-Qadir is that a fodder-fed animal does not become a grazing animal by mere intention, and a grazing animal becomes a fodder-fed animal by mere intention. And the reconciliation between them has been made clear to me: that the statement of the commentator is interpreted as the case when he intends the grazing animal to be fodder-fed while it is in pasture and does not intend to actually fodder-feed it, and the statement of others is interpreted as when he intends it to be fodder-fed after removing it from the pasture. And this reconciliation is indicated by what is in al-Nihaya... it consumes except a bushel until the year passes, there is no zakat on it, and the debt is directed to zakat wealth, not to the genre that is not zakat wealth. It becomes... He said in al-Nahr: I say in al-Diraya: If he wanted to sell the grazing animal or use it or feed it fodder, but did not do so until the year passed, then zakat as a grazing animal is due on him because he intended the action but did not perform it, so the description of graze is not negated. And if he intended for the fodder-fed animal, it becomes grazing because the meaning of graze is established by abandoning action, and he indeed abandoned action. Thus in al-Mabsut and al-Khulasa, and this contradicts the two transmitted reports, so ponder it.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage addresses a legal nuance within the Hanafi school regarding the effect of intention on the zakat classification of livestock. According to transmitted reports in al-Nihaya and Fath al-Qadir, a fodder-fed animal does not become a grazing animal by intention alone, but a grazing animal becomes fodder-fed by intention alone. The author provides a reconciliation: the commentator's statement (al-Zayla'i) applies when the animal is still in the pasture and the owner merely intends to fodder-feed but does not act; others' statements apply when the animal is removed from pasture. The author then cites a passage from al-Nahr's commentary on al-Diraya that presents a different ruling: if a person intends to sell, use, or fodder-feed a grazing animal but does not act, the animal remains sulimah for zakat. However, if he intends for a fodder-fed animal to become grazing, it does become grazing because the essence of sulimah is established by abandoning action (i.e., not feeding fodder). This contradicts the earlier transmitted reports, and the author leaves it for reflection.

Key Terms
سائمة (sa'imah): Livestock that grazes in pasture for most of the year and is subject to zakat at a different rate.
علوفة ('ulufah): Fodder-fed animals that are not grazing and thus not subject to the same zakat rules as sa'imah.
نية (niyyah): The intention that changes the legal status of property or acts.
ترك العمل (tark al-'amal): Abandoning action; in this context, abandoning fodder-feeding is the basis for establishing sa'imah status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the transmitted reports in al-Nihaya and Fath al-Qadir, what is the ruling on a fodder-fed animal if the owner intends it to become a grazing animal?
A) It becomes a grazing animal by intention alone.
B) It does not become a grazing animal by intention alone.
C) It becomes a grazing animal only if the owner actually takes it to pasture.
D) It becomes a grazing animal only after a year.

Key Takeaway
Intention alone asymmetrically changes zakat classification in Hanafi fiqh—from sa'imah to 'ulufah, but not vice versa—though some texts challenge this symmetry.
